Allah's Existence
beginner
5 min
Maturidi's Proof from Change
Maturidi argues from universal change to the world's creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses observable change and decay in all physical things to argue that the world cannot be eternal and must be created.

Core Idea
Change and decay in every body prove it cannot be eternal.

Maturidi begins with a simple observation: even a perfect human cannot prevent his own body from decay. This pattern applies to all living and non-living things. The presence of change, diversity, and repulsion in bodies shows that they are not self-sustaining and depend on a Creator. This lesson walks through that logical argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get the overall sense, then read the English translation. Note the progression from human to inanimate.
The argument is condensed; take your time.
2
Identify the key observations
Maturidi uses several examples: the inability to prevent bodily decay, the similarity of living and dead things, the gathering of opposing natures, and the variety in nature. List them.
3
Trace the conclusion
Notice how Maturidi moves from these observations to the conclusion that anything bearing accidents cannot be eternal. This is the logical core of his proof.
This conclusion is the foundation for the world's creation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ببدء حاله في وجوده وهو أيضًا عاجز عن إصلاح ما يفسد من جسمه وأجزاء بدنه في حالة كماله في القوة والعلم. وكذلك الحال في الأموات والجمادات، فإن ما يجرى في الأحياء جار فيها أيضا. ومن المعلوم أن كل محسوس لا يخلو جسمه عن اجتماع طبائع مختلفة ومتضادة فيه ؛ فهي الحالة التي تؤدي بالمحسوس وأجزائه إلى التنافر والتباعد لأنفسها لولا التدبير من الله تعالى. ومن بين الأحياء الموجودة في الطبيعة ما هو طيب وخبيث، وما هو صغير وكبير، وما هو حسن وقبيح، وما إلى ذلك؛ وهذه بلا شك آيات التغير والزوال. وما احتمل تلك العوارض في وجوده لم يجز كونه قديما . جـ) وأما عن الاستدلال الذي لجأ إليه الماتريدي حتى يثبت كون العالم مخلوقا، فهو يذكرنا فيه بالطريقة العقلية التي يشير بها إلى التغيرات الطارئة على الأجسام التي سبق ذكرها. ورغم أن الماتريدي لم يتقدم هنا بمقولة الجوهر والعرض المستخدمة فيما بعد لإثبات حدوث العالم، فهي مع ذلك تلعب دور الريادة في سلسلة أفكاره حول الدليل الذي يشير إلى ما نشاهد من تلك التغيرات في الأجسام. لقد ذهب الماتريدي إلى استعمال كلمة «الصفة) بدل (العرض واقتنع بأن هذا الاستعمال أصح وأقرب إلى المصطلحات الإسلامية، إذ وردت كلمة العرض في القرآن الكريم بمعنى متاع الدنيا والمقصود بها الأشياء المادية . وهي وقد ذكر الماتريدي أقاويل من يدعى قدم العالم على ما عليه في ست نقاط آتية: أ) كون شيء من شيء بلا منشئ كما نشاهد ذلك في العالم المحسوس، أي في الشاهد. بـ) وجود الأشياء بالضرورة من قبل منشئ حكيم في حدود مبدأ العلة والمعلول. جـ) القول بقدم الطينة الأصل، وحَدَث الصنعة، نحو ما يحدث في مثال الأحياء من النطفة والبيضة . د) وحَدَث الطينة بعوارض حلّت بها، فانقلبت إلى ما عليه الطبائع من الاعتدال والاختلاف. هـ) وجود العالم وحدَثُه بالبارى سبحانه . و) القول بوجود الأصل للعالم وسموه هيولى
In the beginning of his state in his existence, he is also unable to rectify what corrupts from his body and parts of his body in the state of his perfection in strength and knowledge. And likewise is the case with the dead and inanimate objects, for what occurs in the living also occurs in them. And it is known that every sensible thing, its body is not free from the gathering of different and opposing natures in it; so it is the state that leads the sensible thing and its parts to repulsion and separation from themselves were it not for the management of Allah, the Exalted. And among the living beings existing in nature are good and evil, small and large, beautiful and ugly, and so on; and these are without doubt signs of change and passing away. And whatever bears those accidents in its existence cannot be eternal. C) As for the inference that Maturidi resorted to in order to prove that the world is created, he reminds us in it of the rational method by which he points to the changes occurring in bodies that were mentioned previously. Although Maturidi did not here put forward the statement of substance (jawhar) and accident ('arad) used later to prove the occurrence of the world, it nevertheless plays a pioneering role in the series of his ideas about the proof that refers to what we observe of those changes in bodies. Maturidi used the word 'attribute' (sifa) instead of 'accident' ('arad) and was convinced that this usage is more correct and closer to Islamic terminology, since the word 'arad occurs in the Noble Qur'an in the sense of the goods of this world and what is meant by it is material things. And Maturidi mentioned the sayings of those who claim the pre-eternity of the world as it is in six following points: a) That something comes from something without an origin, as we observe that in the sensible world, i.e., in the witnessed. b) The existence of things necessarily from a wise origin within the limits of the principle of cause and effect. c) The statement of the pre-eternity of the original clay (al-tinat al-asl) and the occurrence of the making, like what occurs in the example of living beings from the sperm and the egg. d) And the occurrence of the clay by accidents that befell it, then it turned into what the natures are upon of moderation and difference. e) The existence of the world and its occurrence by the Creator, glory be to Him. f) The statement of the existence of the origin for the world and calling it 'hyyle' (hyle).
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage presents both Maturidi's argument for the world's creation based on observable change and a list of six opposing views advocating pre-eternity. The argument begins by noting that even a perfect being cannot prevent decay in his own body, and that the same applies to all living and dead things. The diversity of natures in bodies would lead to disintegration without divine management. The variety in nature (good/evil, etc.) proves change and transience. Anything subject to such accidents cannot be eternal. Then the editorial commentary explains Maturidi's method: he uses a rational inference from observable changes, preferring the term 'attribute' (sifa) over 'accident' ('arad) due to Qur'anic usage. Finally, six arguments for the world's pre-eternity are enumerated: (a) something from nothing without origin, (b) necessary existence from a wise cause, (c) pre-eternal matter with temporal form, (d) accidents changing primordial clay, (e) temporal world from an eternal Creator, and (f) an eternal substrate called 'hyle'. Maturidi refutes these by pointing out that they all improperly analogize from the witnessed to the unseen without proper evidence.

Key Terms
العوارض
طبائع
التغير
الزوال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main reason Maturidi gives for why the world cannot be eternal?
A) Because it was created from nothing.
B) Because all observable things undergo change and decay, which cannot apply to the eternal.
C) Because the Qur'an explicitly says so.
D) Because philosophers proved it.

Key Takeaway
Everything we observe is subject to change and decay; therefore, it cannot be eternal and must be created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know your body is decaying and you cannot stop it entirely — not even with all your strength and knowledge. Does this reality drive you to recognize your utter dependence on the Creator? How does your daily routine reflect this truth? What would change if you truly lived as a creature that perishes?
?
Think of someone you admire for their power or health. The passage says even they are unable to fix their own decay. Does their mortality change how you view them? Are you prepared to meet the One who manages all creation and before whom all power fades?
?
You are surrounded by change: good becomes evil, beauty fades, life becomes death. You are part of that change. Are you rushing towards what remains? What concrete step can you take today to prepare for the inevitable end that awaits every created thing?

Allah's Existence
intermediate
4 min
Six Arguments for the Pre-Eternity of the World
Maturidi enumerates six positions that claim the world is pre-eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the six main arguments that Maturidi records as claims for the pre-eternity of the world.

Core Idea
Maturidi compiles six philosophical claims for the world's pre-eternity.

After presenting his own proof, Maturidi lists six alternative views that argue for the world's pre-eternity. These range from spontaneous generation to the idea of an eternal hyle. This lesson presents that list.

Guided Path
1
Read the six points in Arabic and English
Read each point slowly. Note the letters (a) to (f) and the structure of each claim.
Each point is a distinct argument from a different philosophical school.
2
Categorize each point by its type
Try to classify each argument: (a) is chance, (b) is necessary causation, (c) is eternal matter with temporal form, (d) is matter altered by accidents, (e) is creation by God (seemingly contradictory), (f) is the Greek concept of hyle.
Point (e) is ambiguous; it may imply the world is created but still pre-eternal in some sense.
3
Reflect on the diversity of views
Notice that Maturidi is presenting these as claims made by opponents. The list shows the range of pre-eternity arguments in his time.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Checkpoint
How many arguments for pre-eternity does Maturidi list?
A) 4
B) 5
C) 6
D) 7

Key Takeaway
Maturidi catalogs six distinct philosophical claims for the world's pre-eternity, ranging from atheistic to hylomorphic.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Maturidi lists these six arguments without refuting them here. Do you have the courage to study the beliefs of those who disagree with you? Could you articulate their position accurately before answering it? Commit to learning one opposing argument fully this week.
?
Argument (c) asserts that eternal clay exists alongside the Creator. Is there anything in your life—your time, your possessions, your relationships—that you treat as if it is 'eternal' besides Allah? What would change if you truly believed everything is created by Him alone?
?
The mention of 'hyle' shows that even ancient philosophers wrestled with the origin of the world. When you feel overwhelmed by life's complexity, do you tend to attribute things to natural processes or to Allah's direct creation? How can you train your heart to see His hand in everything?

Allah's Existence

Proving God's Existence Through the Created World
Maturidi explains how the world serves as evidence for God, using both occurrence and order.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi proves God's existence and attributes by using the sensible world as evidence for the unseen, combining arguments from occurrence and order.

Core Idea
The created world is proof of its Creator, both in its coming into existence and in its order.

Maturidi argues that the world we perceive points beyond itself to a Creator. He does not rely solely on the argument that the world began to exist; he also sees the intricate order as evidence that its Creator possesses knowledge, power, and will. This lesson introduces his method of reasoning from the witnessed to the unseen.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp Maturidi's key points about al-shahid and al-gha'ib.
Pay attention to the terms 'دليل النظام والغاية' (proof of order and purpose).
2
Identify the two proofs
Notice how Maturidi uses both the occurrence of the world (حدوث العالم) and the argument from order and purpose (دليل النظام والغاية) as evidence.
He considers both as complementary, not alternative.
3
Connect to attributes
Understand that the same reasoning that proves God's existence also leads to affirming His names and attributes like knowledge, power, and will.
Maturidi uses the same methodology for existence and attributes.
4
Reflect on the method
Consider why Maturidi emphasizes continuous observation of the world and its entities as a starting point for theological reasoning.
This is a distinctive feature of his approach.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد استقل الماتريدي في كتابه بفصل خاص يتعلق بكيفية كون العالم المحسوس دليلاً لعالم الغيب، أي دلالة الشاهد على الغائب . وقد تعرّض الماتريدي أيضًا في مناسبات عديدة إلى مسألة حدث العالم وخلقه التي تعتبر نقطة انطلاق لإثبات وجود الله . ومن الجدير بالذكر أن دليل إثبات الواجب عند الماتريدي ليس عبارة فقط عن دليل الحدوث؛ فهو في مناسبات مختلفة يذكر استدلالات تدخل في ساحة دليل النظام والغاية . فإذا وقعع الدليل على خلق العالم، أي أن العالم لم يكن في البداية ثم خرج إلى الوجود تحت تأثير عامل خارجي، هذا يدل بالضرورة إلى القبول بوجود خالقه. وقد رأينا أن الماتريدي في إثبات الواجب يعتمد في الغالب على ملاحظته المستمرة للعالم وأعيانه، فهو بالأسلوب والمنهج نفسه يتبنى موقفه في الاستدلال على وجود أسمائه تعالى وصفاته. فالخلق البديع والنظام الهائل الذي نشاهدهما في العالم يؤدي بنا إلى القول بأن خالقه له العلم والقدرة والإرادة غير أن نسبة تلك الصفات إلى الخالق هل يوجب التشابه بالمخلوقات التي تتصف بالصفات نفسها، فبالتالي تعارض مبدأ عقيدة التوحيد ؟ فقد ذهب الماتريدي إلى القول بأنه احتمال لا نستطيع الابتعاد عنه، فلعله في الظاهر يوجب التشابه؛ ولكن الضرورة أطلقت لنا على نفي المفهوم من الشاهد ليُنفى به الشبه. ونسميه بالذي ذكرت ضرورة، ولو احتمل وسعنا التسمية بما لا يسمّى به غير كنا نسميه» . غير أن التشابه هنا في القول فقط ؛ ألا ترى أن العبارة التي بها نسميه عز وجل عالِمًا وقادرًا في اللغات المتعددة مختلفة من غير أن كان ثمة اختلاف في المعنى. وهذا يعنى أن الأسماء التي نسميه بها فهي ليست إلا عبارات وألفاظ تقرب المعاني اللائقة بالله تعالى إلى الأفهام والأذهان. فقد أصبح بالتالي البيان الإلهي للصفات في قوله ليس كمثله شيء ﴾ [الشورى: ١١/٤٢] مبدءا تبناه الجميع حتى التشبيه في مثل هذا التوصيف لذات الله تعالى
And Maturidi devoted a special chapter in his book regarding how the sensible world is evidence for the unseen world, i.e., the indication of the witnessed (al-shahid) on the absent (al-gha'ib). And Maturidi also addressed on many occasions the issue of the occurrence (hadath) of the world and its creation, which is considered a starting point for proving the existence of God. And it is worth mentioning that the proof of establishing the Necessary Being according to Maturidi is not only the proof of occurrence; rather, on various occasions he mentions inferences that enter the domain of the proof of order and purpose (dalil al-nizam wa al-ghaya). So if the evidence for the creation of the world is established, i.e., that the world was not at the beginning and then came into existence under the influence of an external factor, this necessarily leads to acceptance of the existence of its Creator. And we have seen that Maturidi, in proving the Necessary Being, mostly relies on his continuous observation of the world and its entities; so by the same style and method he adopts his position in inferring the existence of His names, the Exalted, and His attributes. For the wonderful creation and the immense order that we observe in the world lead us to say that its Creator has knowledge, power, and will. However, does attributing those attributes to the Creator necessitate similarity to the creatures that are described with the same attributes, and consequently contradict the principle of the doctrine of tawhid? Maturidi held that it is a possibility we cannot avoid; perhaps it apparently necessitates similarity; but necessity has allowed us to negate the concept from the witnessed in order to negate similarity. And we name Him with what I mentioned as a necessity, and even if it is possible, we have the capacity to name Him with what no other is named, but we name Him. However, similarity here is only in speech; do you not see that the expression by which we call Him, the Mighty and Majestic, 'knowing' and 'powerful' in various languages differs while there is no difference in meaning? This means that the names by which we call Him are only expressions and words that bring meanings befitting Allah the Exalted closer to understandings and minds. Thus, the divine statement of attributes in His saying 'There is nothing like unto Him' (Qur'an 42:11) became a principle that everyone adopted, even similarity in such description of the essence of Allah the Exalted.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage explains Maturidi's method of proving God's existence and attributes. It begins by noting that Maturidi dedicated a chapter to how the sensible world (al-shahid) serves as evidence for the unseen (al-gha'ib). The occurrence of the world is key. However, Maturidi does not rely solely on the argument from occurrence; he also uses the argument from order and purpose (dalil al-nizam wa al-ghaya). The order and creation point to a Creator with knowledge, power, and will. The passage then raises a problem: if we attribute these same qualities (knowledge, power) to God and creatures, does that imply similarity (tashbih) and contradict tawhid? Maturidi answers that necessity forces us to use these terms, but we negate the human concept of these attributes from the witnessed to avoid similarity. The similarity is only in wording, not in reality. Different languages use different expressions for the same meaning. Thus, divine names are approximations for human understanding. The principle from the Qur'an 'There is nothing like unto Him' protects against true anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
دليل النظام والغاية
الشاهد
الغائب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What are the two types of evidence Maturidi uses from the world to prove God's existence?
A) Miracle and prophecy
B) Occurrence and order
C) Reason and revelation
D) Essence and attributes

Key Takeaway
The world's creation and order are sufficient proofs for the existence of God and His attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world around you—the stars, the seasons, your own body—do you live as if it all just happened, or as if a Creator intentionally designed it? What would change in your gratitude, your humility, your sense of purpose if you saw each order as a direct sign from Him?
?
The passage says Maturidi relied on continuous observation of the world to know God's names and attributes. Do you actively observe the world to deepen your knowledge of God—for instance, seeing His mercy in rain, His power in the mountains? If not, what is one thing you can start noticing today to turn your observation into worship?
?
If the world's existence and order compel you to accept a Creator, why do you sometimes act as if you are the master of your own life? Where in your heart do you need to surrender to the fact that you were created by Him and for Him?

intermediate
8 min
Resolving the Problem of Similarity in Divine Attributes
Maturidi addresses the problem of anthropomorphism when attributing attributes to God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi resolves the apparent similarity between God and creatures when using the same attribute names without compromising tawhid.

Core Idea
We describe God with names like 'Knowing' by necessity, but the reality is unlike anything created.

A problem arises: if we call God 'Knowing' and 'Powerful', do we not liken Him to creatures who also know and have power? Maturidi tackles this head-on, arguing that we use these terms by necessity, but we negate the human concept behind them. The similarity is only in wording, not in meaning or essence. This lesson explains his solution and its grounding in the Qur'anic principle 'There is nothing like unto Him'.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and translation, focusing on the question Maturidi poses about tashbih and his answer.
Notice the phrase 'غير أن نسبة تلك الصفات إلى الخالق هل يوجب التشابه'.
2
Understand the tension
Realize that affirming attributes like knowledge and power for God seems to threaten tawhid by implying similarity with creatures.
This is a central theological problem in Islamic thought.
3
Analyze Maturidi's solution
Maturidi acknowledges the apparent similarity but insists that necessity allows us to negate the concept (mafhum) from the witnessed realm to avoid likeness.
The terms are used, but their meanings are not like human attributes.
4
See the role of language
Note that different languages use different words for the same referent, showing that the names are mere expressions approximating the divine reality.
This underscores that similarity is only in wording, not in essence.
5
Conclude with the Qur'anic principle
The verse 'There is nothing like unto Him' becomes the foundational safeguard against anthropomorphism.
Maturidi says this principle is adopted by all.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد استقل الماتريدي في كتابه بفصل خاص يتعلق بكيفية كون العالم المحسوس دليلاً لعالم الغيب، أي دلالة الشاهد على الغائب . وقد تعرّض الماتريدي أيضًا في مناسبات عديدة إلى مسألة حدث العالم وخلقه التي تعتبر نقطة انطلاق لإثبات وجود الله . ومن الجدير بالذكر أن دليل إثبات الواجب عند الماتريدي ليس عبارة فقط عن دليل الحدوث؛ فهو في مناسبات مختلفة يذكر استدلالات تدخل في ساحة دليل النظام والغاية . فإذا وقعع الدليل على خلق العالم، أي أن العالم لم يكن في البداية ثم خرج إلى الوجود تحت تأثير عامل خارجي، هذا يدل بالضرورة إلى القبول بوجود خالقه. وقد رأينا أن الماتريدي في إثبات الواجب يعتمد في الغالب على ملاحظته المستمرة للعالم وأعيانه، فهو بالأسلوب والمنهج نفسه يتبنى موقفه في الاستدلال على وجود أسمائه تعالى وصفاته. فالخلق البديع والنظام الهائل الذي نشاهدهما في العالم يؤدي بنا إلى القول بأن خالقه له العلم والقدرة والإرادة غير أن نسبة تلك الصفات إلى الخالق هل يوجب التشابه بالمخلوقات التي تتصف بالصفات نفسها، فبالتالي تعارض مبدأ عقيدة التوحيد ؟ فقد ذهب الماتريدي إلى القول بأنه احتمال لا نستطيع الابتعاد عنه، فلعله في الظاهر يوجب التشابه؛ ولكن الضرورة أطلقت لنا على نفي المفهوم من الشاهد ليُنفى به الشبه. ونسميه بالذي ذكرت ضرورة، ولو احتمل وسعنا التسمية بما لا يسمّى به غير كنا نسميه» . غير أن التشابه هنا في القول فقط ؛ ألا ترى أن العبارة التي بها نسميه عز وجل عالِمًا وقادرًا في اللغات المتعددة مختلفة من غير أن كان ثمة اختلاف في المعنى. وهذا يعنى أن الأسماء التي نسميه بها فهي ليست إلا عبارات وألفاظ تقرب المعاني اللائقة بالله تعالى إلى الأفهام والأذهان. فقد أصبح بالتالي البيان الإلهي للصفات في قوله ليس كمثله شيء ﴾ [الشورى: ١١/٤٢] مبدءا تبناه الجميع حتى التشبيه في مثل هذا التوصيف لذات الله تعالى
And Maturidi devoted a special chapter in his book regarding how the sensible world is evidence for the unseen world, i.e., the indication of the witnessed (al-shahid) on the absent (al-gha'ib). And Maturidi also addressed on many occasions the issue of the occurrence (hadath) of the world and its creation, which is considered a starting point for proving the existence of God. And it is worth mentioning that the proof of establishing the Necessary Being according to Maturidi is not only the proof of occurrence; rather, on various occasions he mentions inferences that enter the domain of the proof of order and purpose (dalil al-nizam wa al-ghaya). So if the evidence for the creation of the world is established, i.e., that the world was not at the beginning and then came into existence under the influence of an external factor, this necessarily leads to acceptance of the existence of its Creator. And we have seen that Maturidi, in proving the Necessary Being, mostly relies on his continuous observation of the world and its entities; so by the same style and method he adopts his position in inferring the existence of His names, the Exalted, and His attributes. For the wonderful creation and the immense order that we observe in the world lead us to say that its Creator has knowledge, power, and will. However, does attributing those attributes to the Creator necessitate similarity to the creatures that are described with the same attributes, and consequently contradict the principle of the doctrine of tawhid? Maturidi held that it is a possibility we cannot avoid; perhaps it apparently necessitates similarity; but necessity has allowed us to negate the concept from the witnessed in order to negate similarity. And we name Him with what I mentioned as a necessity, and even if it is possible, we have the capacity to name Him with what no other is named, but we name Him. However, similarity here is only in speech; do you not see that the expression by which we call Him, the Mighty and Majestic, 'knowing' and 'powerful' in various languages differs while there is no difference in meaning? This means that the names by which we call Him are only expressions and words that bring meanings befitting Allah the Exalted closer to understandings and minds. Thus, the divine statement of attributes in His saying 'There is nothing like unto Him' (Qur'an 42:11) became a principle that everyone adopted, even similarity in such description of the essence of Allah the Exalted.
Source pages p. 35

Commentary
This passage explains Maturidi's method of proving God's existence and attributes. It begins by noting that Maturidi dedicated a chapter to how the sensible world (al-shahid) serves as evidence for the unseen (al-gha'ib). The occurrence of the world is key. However, Maturidi does not rely solely on the argument from occurrence; he also uses the argument from order and purpose (dalil al-nizam wa al-ghaya). The order and creation point to a Creator with knowledge, power, and will. The passage then raises a problem: if we attribute these same qualities (knowledge, power) to God and creatures, does that imply similarity (tashbih) and contradict tawhid? Maturidi answers that necessity forces us to use these terms, but we negate the human concept of these attributes from the witnessed to avoid similarity. The similarity is only in wording, not in reality. Different languages use different expressions for the same meaning. Thus, divine names are approximations for human understanding. The principle from the Qur'an 'There is nothing like unto Him' protects against true anthropomorphism.

Key Terms
التشبيه
التوحيد
الأسماء والصفات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: To avoid similarity, we should not use the same terms for God and creatures at all.
Correction: Maturidi says we must use these terms by necessity, but we negate the human concept; the terms themselves are not problematic when understood properly.

Checkpoint
How does Maturidi resolve the apparent similarity when using the same attribute names for God and creatures?
A) He says we should not use such names.
B) He says the names are used but the concepts are negated.
C) He says God is like creatures in some ways.
D) He says attributes are metaphorical.

Key Takeaway
Affirming divine attributes does not imply similarity because we negate the limited human understanding while retaining the names as necessary expressions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite Allah's names like Ar-Rahman (The Most Merciful), do you ever catch yourself mentally picturing a human-like mercy? What concrete step can you take today to remind yourself that His mercy is utterly unlike any mercy you have experienced?
?
If you were asked to describe God's knowledge without using human analogies, would you feel lost? What does your discomfort reveal about your reliance on worldly comparisons? Will you commit to sitting in that uncertainty rather than forcing a false image?
?
The lesson says 'similarity is only in speech'. But when you pray, do your words ever feel hollow because you can't truly grasp what you're saying? How can you make your heart present with the reality behind the words, even without full comprehension?

Maturidi's Combined Proof: Huduth and Nizam
Maturidi presents his combined proof for God's existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi combines the proof from temporal origination and the proof from order to demonstrate the existence of God.

Core Idea
Maturidi proves God through both creation and cosmic order.

This lesson presents Maturidi’s unique approach to proving God’s existence. Rather than relying solely on the argument from temporal origination (huduth), he also invokes the argument from order and purpose (nizam wa ghaya). As you read, notice how these two strands work together to point necessarily to a Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get the overall claim that Maturidi uses both proofs.
2
Identify the two proofs
The passage mentions the proof of temporal origination and the proof of order and purpose. Notice how they are presented as complementary.
3
Understand the necessity
The passage states that evidence of creation and order necessarily indicates a Creator. Think about why this implication is 'necessary'.

Source Text

مسألة حدث العالم وخلقه التي تعتبر نقطة انطلاق لإثبات وجود الله . ومن الجدير بالذكر أن دليل إثبات الواجب عند الماتريدي ليس عبارة فقط عن دليل الحدوث؛ فهو في مناسبات مختلفة يذكر استدلالات تدخل في ساحة دليل النظام والغاية . فإذا وقعع الدليل على خلق العالم، أي أن العالم لم يكن في البداية ثم خرج إلى الوجود تحت تأثير عامل خارجي، هذا يدل بالضرورة إلى القبول بوجود خالقه.
The issue of the temporal origination of the world and its creation is considered a starting point for proving the existence of God. And it is worth noting that the proof of the Necessary Being according to al-Maturidi is not only the proof of temporal origination; rather, on various occasions he mentions arguments that enter into the domain of the proof of order and purpose. So if the evidence falls on the creation of the world, meaning that the world did not exist in the beginning and then came into existence under the influence of an external factor, this necessarily indicates acceptance of the existence of its Creator.

Commentary
This passage defines Maturidi's approach to proving God's existence. It states that while the argument from the temporal origination of the world (huduth) is a starting point, Maturidi also employs arguments from order and purpose (nizam wa ghaya). The passage explains that this double evidence—the world's coming into being from nothing and the observed order—points necessarily to a Creator.

Rational Proof for the Purposefulness of Creation
Maturidi argues rationally that creation is purposeful and needs a lawgiver.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational argument that the world cannot have been created for annihilation and must therefore have a purpose that requires a divine lawgiver.

Core Idea
The world cannot be vain because the intellect itself is part of it.

Maturidi begins this passage by grounding his argument in reason itself. He argues that since the intellect is a part of the world, the world cannot be founded on foolishness. From this he concludes that creation is for permanence, and then observes that the conflicting nature of humans requires divine guidance to prevent corruption. This lesson walks through each step of that rational proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the flow of the argument: from premise to conclusion to supporting observations.
The passage is incomplete but presents a complete mini-argument.
2
Identify the main premise
Maturidi starts with reason ('aql) and states that a world created only for annihilation would lack wisdom. What is his basis for saying this?
He uses the fact that the intellect itself is part of the world.
3
Follow the conclusion
From the premise, he concludes the world was created for permanence (baqa'). How does he justify this transition?
If not for annihilation, then for permanence by necessity.
4
Examine human nature
Maturidi describes humans as having conflicting desires and instincts. What would happen if they were left without law?
He argues mutual destruction would occur, nullifying wisdom.
5
Connect to the need for a lawgiver
The passage implies that a divine law is necessary to prevent corruption. Reflect on how this argument supports prophethood.
This is a rational proof for the necessity of guidance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما العقل فهو أن كون هذا العالم للفناء خاصة ليس بحكمة، وخروج كل ذي عقل بفعله عن طريق الحكمة قبيح عنه ؛ فلا يحتمل أن يكون العالم الذي العقل منه جزء مؤسسا على غير الحكمة أو مجعولاً عبثاً. وإذا ثبت ذلك دلّ أن إنشاء العالم للبقاء لا للفناء. ثم كان العالم بأصله مبنيا على طبائع مختلفة ووجوه متضادة، وبخاصة الذي هو مقصود من حيث العقل الذي يجمع بين المجتمع ويفرق بين الذي حقه التفريق ؛ وهو الذي سمته الحكماء العالم الصغير. فهو على أهواء مختلفة وطبائع متشتتة، وشهوات ركبت فيهم غالبة، لو تركوا وما عليه جبلوا لتنازعوا في تجاذب المنافع وأنواع العز والشرف والملك والسلطان، فيعقب ذلك التباغض ثم التقاتل، وفي ذلك التفاني والفساد الذي لو تعلق أمر كون العالم له لبطلت الحكمة في كونه . مع ما جعل البشر وجميع الحيوان غير محتمل للبقاء إلا بالأغذية وما به قوام أبدانهم إلى المدد التي جعلت لهم. فلو لم يُرد بتكوينهم سوى
And as for reason (al-ʿaql), it is that the existence of this world specifically for annihilation is not wisdom, and the departure of every rational being by his action from the path of wisdom is evil (qabīḥ) in his view; so it is not plausible that the world, of which the intellect is a part, is founded upon other than wisdom or made in vain. And when that is established, it indicates that the creation of the world is for permanence, not for annihilation. Then the world in its origin is built upon different natures and opposing aspects, especially that which is intended from the perspective of the intellect that unites what should be united and separates what deserves separation; it is that which the sages called the 'small world'. So it is upon different inclinations and scattered natures, and desires instilled in them are overwhelming; if they were left to what they are naturally disposed to, they would dispute in the pursuit of benefits and types of honor, nobility, kingship, and authority, and that would be followed by hatred then fighting, and in that is annihilation and corruption, which if the matter of the world's existence were attached to it, wisdom would be nullified in its existence. Along with what is placed: human beings and all animals are not capable of survival except by nourishment and that by which their bodies are sustained until the periods appointed for them. So if not intended by their creation except
Source pages p. 65

Commentary
This passage provides a rational argument for the purposefulness of creation and the necessity of a lawgiver. Maturidi begins with a premise: it is not wise for the world to be created only for annihilation. Since the intellect itself is part of the world, the world cannot be founded on foolishness or vanity. Therefore, the world must be created for permanence (al-baqāʾ). He then observes that the world is composed of conflicting natures, especially humans, who have diverse desires and inclinations. If left without guidance, they would fall into conflict and self-destruction, which would nullify the wisdom of creation. Additionally, humans and animals depend on sustenance for survival, implying a designed order. Thus, the creation must have a purpose that requires a divine law to maintain harmony.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The world could have been created without any purpose or design, and human life could be merely incidental.
Correction: Maturidi argues that since the intellect itself is part of the world, it is impossible for the world to be without wisdom or purpose. The very structure of human nature and the dependence on sustenance indicate a designed order.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi argue that the world cannot have been created for annihilation?
A) Because it would be unjust
B) Because it would not be wisdom
C) Because humans desire immortality
D) Because revelation says so

Key Takeaway
Maturidi uses reason itself to prove that the world is created for permanence and that human nature requires divine guidance to avoid corruption.

?

?
This lesson shows that the world is not vain. What part of your life feels meaningless or futile? How will you reorient it toward the permanence that Allah intends?

Proof for Origination of Entities through Report
Maturidi enumerates three ways of knowledge and details the report proof.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses divine report as a proof for the origination of all entities, and why no being can claim pre-eternity.

Core Idea
No living being can truly claim pre-eternity; all have observed beginnings.

Maturidi begins his proof for the origination of the world by establishing three foundational ways of knowledge: sense, reason, and report. In this passage, he focuses on the authority of divine report, arguing that God's own testimony as Creator is beyond human imitation. He then shows that no living being can convincingly claim to be eternal, because everyone witnesses their own beginning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Let the structure of the argument become clear.
Notice the section heading and the mention of 'three ways'.
2
Identify the three ways of knowledge
Maturidi references three ways of knowing things. Identify them from the context (sense, reason, report) and note that only the report proof is elaborated here.
The three ways are a standard framework in Maturidi theology.
3
Understand the report argument
Maturidi argues that divine report is valid because God's statement that He is Creator is in a way humans cannot replicate. This establishes His authority.
The report is from the Qur'an and authentic revelation.
4
Observe the implication for living beings
No living being can claim pre-eternity; any such claim would be immediately known as false. Everyone witnesses their own childhood and beginning.
This observational argument reinforces the report.
5
Conclude the origination of all entities
Since living beings are originated, and non-living things are under their management, all entities must be originated. This completes the proof.
The conclusion is that everything except God is originated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حدث الأعيان. الباب الأول: مسائل الإلهيات. حدث العالم ووجوب محدثه. قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله: الدليل على حدث الأعيان هو شهادة الوجوه الثلاثة التي ذكرنا من سبل العلم بالأشياء. فأما الخبر فما ثبت عن الله تعالى من وجه يعجز البشر عن دليل مثله لأحد: إنه أخبر أنه خالق كل شيء، وبديع السموات والأرض، وأن له ملك ما فيهن. وقد بينا لزوم القول بالخبر. وليس أحد من الأحياء ادعا لنفسه القدم أو أشار إلى معنى يدل على قدمه، بل لو قال لعرف كذبه هو بالضرورة، وكذا كل من حضره بما رأوه صغيرا، ويذكر ابتداؤه أيضا. لذلك لزم القول بحدث الأحياء. ثم الأموات تحت تدبير الأحياء، فهم أحق بالحدث. والله الموفق.
The origination of entities. The first chapter: Matters of Divinity. The origination of the world and the necessity of its Originator. The Shaykh Abu Mansur, may God have mercy on him, said: The proof for the origination of entities is the testimony of the three ways that we mentioned among the paths of knowledge of things. As for the report, it is what has been established from God Almighty in a manner that humans are incapable of producing a similar proof for anyone: that He informed that He is the Creator of everything, the Originator of the heavens and the earth, and that to Him belongs the dominion of whatever is in them. And we have already explained the necessity of accepting the report. And no living being has claimed pre-eternity for himself or pointed to a meaning indicating his pre-eternity; rather, if he claimed it, his lie would be known necessarily, and likewise everyone present, based on what they saw of him as a child, and his beginning is also mentioned. Therefore, the statement of the origination of living beings is necessary. Then the dead are under the management of the living, so they are more deserving of origination. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage begins with a section heading introducing the proof for the origination of entities. Maturidi asserts that the proof relies on three ways of knowledge (report, sense, and reason). He then details the first way: divine report. The report from God that He is the Creator is unimpeachable because humans cannot produce a similar proof. Moreover, no living being can legitimately claim pre-eternity; anyone who does is known to lie. Since living beings are observed to have beginnings (childhood), they must be originated. Non-living things are under the management of the living, so they too are originated.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The 'report' here refers to any human report or hearsay.
Correction: The report is specifically divine revelation, which is unique and authoritative beyond human capability.

Checkpoint

B) No one can claim pre-eternity for themselves and be believed.

D) They are like inanimate objects.

Key Takeaway
The divine report that God is Creator, combined with the observed fact that no being can claim pre-eternity, proves that all entities are originated.

Proof for Origination via Sense and Need
Maturidi uses sense and need/incapacity to prove origination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that perceived entities and their attributes of need and incapacity demonstrate their origination.

Core Idea
Everything perceived shows dependency, not self-sufficiency.

Maturidi presents a second proof for the origination of the world, this time grounded in sense perception and the observable attributes of need and incapacity. By examining the nature of perceived entities and their dependence on others, he demonstrates that they cannot be pre-eternal, thus leading to the conclusion of a Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing; then read the translation to grasp the overall argument.
Notice the use of terms like 'حس' (sense), 'حاجة' (need), 'قدم' (pre-eternity), and 'حدث' (origination).
2
Analyze the first proof: sense and need
Identify how Maturidi uses sense perception to argue that every perceived entity is surrounded by necessity and indicates need, thereby implying origination.
He contrasts pre-eternity (self-sufficiency) with observable dependency.
3
Analyze the second proof: ignorance and incapacity
Examine the second argument: creatures are ignorant of their own beginnings and unable to repair corruption, showing they depend on another.
This reinforces the conclusion that they are originated.

وعلم الحس، وهو أن كل عين من الأعيان يُحَس محاطا بالضرورة منبئا بالحاجة، والقدم هو شرط الغناء؛ لأنه يستغنى بقدمه عن غيره، والضرورة والحاجة يُحوجانه إلى غيره، فلزم به حدثه. وأيضا إن كل شيء جاهل ببدء حاله، عاجز عن إصلاح ما يفسد [عظ] منه في الحال التي هو فيها موصوف بالكمال في القوة والعلم إذا كان ذلك حيا، ولو كان ميتا فسلطان الحي عليه جارٍ، ثبت أنه لم يكن واحد منهما إلا بغيره؛ وإذا ثبت الغير لزم الحدث.
And sense perception: that every entity that is perceived is surrounded by necessity, indicating need. Pre-eternity is the condition of self-sufficiency, because by its pre-eternity it is independent of others, while necessity and need require it to depend on others. Therefore its origination is necessitated. Also, every thing is ignorant of the beginning of its state, incapable of repairing what corrupts [؟] from it in the state in which it is described as perfect in power and knowledge if it is living, and if it is dead then the authority of the living over it prevails. It is established that neither of them exists except through another; and when the other is established, origination follows.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage presents the second way: sense perception. Maturidi argues that every perceived object shows need and dependency, while pre-eternity implies self-sufficiency. Since objects exhibit need, they cannot be pre-eternal. Further, creatures are ignorant of their own beginnings and unable to fix their own corruption, even when they appear perfect. Living things are weak, and dead things are under the control of the living. All this shows that nothing exists independently; everything depends on another, which proves origination.

Key Terms
حس
حاجة
قدم
غنى
حدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about imperfection proving that God exists.
Correction: Maturidi focuses not on imperfection per se, but on demonstrable dependency. The key is that entities cannot be self-sufficient; they rely on another.

Checkpoint
What condition does Maturidi associate with pre-eternity (qidam)?
A) Need (haja)
B) Self-sufficiency (ghina)
C) Ignorance (jahl)
D) Incapacity (ʿajz)

Key Takeaway
Maturidi uses observable need and incapacity in creatures to prove that they are originated and dependent on a Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever truly recognized your own utter dependence, even in moments when you feel most capable and self-sufficient? Today, identify one area of your life where you act as if you are independent—do you realize that your next breath, your health, your ability to fix even a small problem, are all given to you moment by moment?
?
When something goes wrong in your life—a relationship, a health issue, a failure—do you immediately try to fix it using your own power and knowledge, forgetting that you are 'ignorant of the beginning of your state' and 'incapable of repairing what corrupts'? What would change if you turned to your Creator first, acknowledging your need?
?
If every perceived thing around you, including your own body, screams its dependence and need, why do you still live as if you are self-sufficient? What would it look like to shift from a posture of self-reliance to a constant, lived awareness of your need for the Pre-eternal One?

Arguments from Dependence and Inability
Maturidi presents two sensory arguments proving contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses two observations—that all perceived things are inherently needy and that they are ignorant of their origin and unable to repair themselves—to prove their contingency.

Core Idea
Dependence and inability are signs of contingency; the Eternal is independent and powerful.

Maturidi begins his sensory proof with two intertwined arguments. First, every perceived entity is surrounded by need, whereas eternity implies self-sufficiency. Second, every thing is ignorant of its own beginning and incapable of mending its own corruption, indicating it relies on another. Together these establish that all perceived things are contingent—created and dependent on a Creator.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two arguments
The passage contains two distinct arguments: the first from need and self-sufficiency, the second from ignorance and inability. Read carefully to separate them.
2
Understand the reasoning of the first argument
Notice that Maturidi defines eternity as a condition for self-sufficiency. Since perceived things are needy, they cannot be eternal, so they must be contingent.
The key link is between need and lack of self-sufficiency.
3
Understand the reasoning of the second argument
Every thing is ignorant of its origin and unable to repair itself, so it depends on another. That dependence proves contingency because eternity cannot depend on another.
The argument applies to both living and dead things.
4
Reflect on the overall conclusion
Both arguments lead to the same conclusion: all perceived things are contingent, pointing to a necessary Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بــ وعلم الحس، وهو أن كل عين من الأعيان يُحَس محاطا بالضرورة منبئا بالحاجة، والقدم هو شرط الغناء ؛ لأنه يستغنى بقدمه عن غيره، والضرورة والحاجة يُحوجانه إلى غيره، فلزم به حدثه. وأيضاً إن كل شيء جاهل ببدء حاله ، عاجز عن إصلاح ما يفسد منه في الحال التي هو فيها موصوف بالكمال في القوة والعلم إذا كان ذلك حيا، ولو كان ميتا فسلطان الحي عليه جارٍ ، ثبت أنه لم يكن واحد منهما إلا بغيره ؛ وإذا ثبت الغير لزم الحدث، إذ القدم يمنع الكون لغيره. وأيضا إن كل محسوس لا يخلو عن اجتماع طبائع مختلفة ومتضادة فيه مما حقها التنافر والتباعد لأنفسها، ثبت اجتماعها بغيرها، وفي ذلك حدثه. والله الموفق. وأيضا إن العالم ذو أجزاء وأبعاض ، ويُعلم أكثر أبعاضه أنه حادث بعد أن لم يكن، ويُعْلَم نماؤه واتساعه وكبره؛ لزم ذلك في كلّه، إذ لا يصير اجتماع أجزاء متناهية غير متناهية ! وأيضاً إن منه طيّبًا وخبينًا، وصغيرًا وكبيرا، وحسنًا وقبيحا، ونورا وظلمة، وهذه آيات التغير والزوال، وفي التغير والزوال فناء وهلاك؛ إذ معلوم أن الاجتماع يؤكد ويقوى ويُعظم؛ دليله النَّشَر ، وأنه إذا تفرق بطل ذلك. فثبت أن ذلك آية الفناء، وما احتمل الفناء لم يجز كونه بنفسه. ويلزم أيضاً احتمال الابتداء . وليس لقول مَنْ يقول : يغيب عن الأبصار ولا يفنى معنى، لما علم العالم بالبصر لا بالدلائل، وبها يُدعى القدم، فقد زال ذلك. مع ما أنا بينا من وهنه، ولا فرق بين حياة جسم تفنى وبين ذاته . ولا قوة إلا بالله .
B: And sensory knowledge: it is that every individual entity that is perceived is surrounded necessarily by need, indicating need. Eternity is the condition of self-sufficiency, because it is self-sufficient by its eternity from other than itself. Necessity and need make it need other than itself, so its contingency is necessary. Also, every thing is ignorant of the beginning of its state, incapable of repairing what becomes corrupted from it in the state in which it is described as perfect in power and knowledge if it is alive; and if it is dead, then the authority of the living is over it. It is established that neither of them exists except by another; and when the other is established, contingency is necessary, for eternity prevents existence for other than itself. Also, every perceived thing is not free from the combination of different and opposing natures in it, while their nature is repulsion and separation. Their combination is established by another, and in that is its contingency. And God is the Grantor of success. Also, the world has parts and members; most of its members are known to be contingent after not having been, and its growth, expansion, and size are known; that is necessary for the whole, for the combination of finite parts cannot become infinite! Also, from it are good and bad, small and large, beautiful and ugly, light and darkness; these are signs of change and passing away. In change and passing away are annihilation and destruction, for it is known that combination strengthens, confirms, and magnifies; its evidence is diffusion: when it disperses, that ceases. So it is established that that is a sign of annihilation, and whatever admits annihilation cannot be by itself. Also, the possibility of beginning is necessary. And the saying of one who says 'It disappears from sight but does not perish' has no meaning, because the world is known by sight, not by proofs; and by proofs eternity is claimed, so that has vanished. Moreover, we have shown its weakness; and there is no difference between the life of a body that perishes and its essence. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 75

Commentary
This passage presents the second way of knowledge: sensory knowledge (ʿilm al-ḥiss). Maturidi gives multiple arguments, each building on observable features of the world. First, every perceived entity is marked by need and dependence, whereas eternity implies self-sufficiency; thus need proves contingency. Second, every thing is ignorant of its own origin and unable to repair itself, showing it depends on another. Third, every composite object contains opposing natures that should repel each other, so their combination requires an external cause. Fourth, the world has parts that grow and change, and finite parts cannot yield an infinite whole. Fifth, the world contains opposites (good/bad, light/darkness) which are signs of change and perishing; what perishes cannot be self-existent. He refutes the claim that something can disappear without perishing, arguing that perception does not prove eternity. The passage ends with a declaration of human helplessness.

Key Terms
علم الحس
القدم
الغناء
الحدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That need is not a sufficient proof for contingency because something could be eternal yet still have needs.
Correction: Maturidi defines eternity as the condition of self-sufficiency (شرط الغناء), so an eternal being cannot have needs by definition. Therefore, need directly disproves eternity.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does ignorance of its own beginning prove about a thing?
A) It is eternal
B) It is contingent
C) It is self-sufficient
D) It is compound

Key Takeaway
The observed need and incompetence of all perceived things demonstrate that they are contingent and depend on a Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You observe your own body and mind: you do not know how you began, and you cannot fully repair yourself when you fall ill. How does this undeniable inability humble you before Allah? Will you today, in a moment of honesty, admit to yourself that you are completely dependent on Him, and let that change how you pray?
?
Maturidi says that even when you feel perfect in power and knowledge, you are still ignorant of your own beginning. Think of a time you felt self-sufficient or proud of your abilities. How does this lesson expose that feeling as a delusion? What specific step can you take this week to replace pride with gratitude for your Creator?
?
If eternity means self-sufficiency and you are not self-sufficient, then you are not eternal. Do you truly live as if you are a contingent being, or do you act as if you control your own life? What one habitual thought or action would change if you truly internalized that you exist only because of Allah?

Arguments from Composition and Growth
Maturidi argues from composition and growth to contingency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the combination of opposing natures and the growth of the world's parts to argue for the contingency of all beings.

Core Idea
Opposites that naturally repel cannot unite without a cause; finite parts cannot yield an infinite whole.

Maturidi presents two further sensory arguments. The first observes that every perceived object contains opposing natures that naturally repel each other, so their combination must be caused by an external agent. The second notes that the world has finite parts that grow and change, and since the whole is composed of finite parts, it cannot be infinite or eternal. These arguments reinforce the contingency of all creation.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the logic of opposing natures
Notice that Maturidi states that opposing natures naturally repel and separate, so their combination requires an external cause. This cause proves the object is contingent.
2
Understand the parts-to-whole argument
The world has parts that are observed to be contingent and finite. The whole, being composed of such parts, cannot be infinite or eternal.
The argument uses the principle that the whole shares the nature of its parts.
3
Connect both arguments to contingency
Both arguments show that the world depends on an external cause, so it is contingent and not eternal.

Source Text

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the combination of opposites could be natural, but Maturidi argues that their nature is repulsion, so union requires an external cause.
Correction: The natural tendency of opposites is repulsion, not attraction. Therefore, their combination cannot be self-caused; it must be due to something else.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what is the nature of opposing natures in themselves?
A) Attraction
B) Repulsion
C) Indifference
D) Combination

Key Takeaway
The observed composition of opposing natures and the finite growth of the world's parts both point to their contingency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your life is filled with opposing forces—desire for comfort vs. responsibility, love of this world vs. the next. You try to balance them yourself, but the argument shows that only an external unifier can truly bring them together. In what area of your life are you still trying to be your own unifier, ignoring your absolute need for Allah? Will you turn to Him tonight and ask Him to bring peace to that battle?
?
Everything you have—your body, your wealth, your relationships—has grown from small beginnings. The lesson says that growth itself proves contingency: you did not create yourself from nothing. When you look at what you have built, do you feel proud of your own effort or humbled by the One who enabled every step? Spend three minutes listing five things that grew in your life and thank the Originator for each.
?
The argument from parts proves that the finite cannot become infinite. Your days are numbered, your strength limited. Yet you often act as if you have infinite time or energy. If you truly believed that every moment is a gift from the Creator who holds you in existence, how would you spend the next hour differently? Commit now to one action that acknowledges your total contingency.

Arguments from Opposites and Annihilation
Maturidi argues from change and annihilation to contingency, and refutes an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the presence of opposites and change as signs of annihilation, and why this proves contingency, including his refutation of the claim that things can disappear without perishing.

Core Idea
If something is subject to change, it perishes; what perishes cannot be self-existent.

In his final sensory argument, Maturidi points to the existence of opposites in the world (good/bad, light/darkness) as signs of change and passing away. Change leads to annihilation, and anything that admits annihilation cannot be self-existent, so must be contingent. He also refutes the objection that something could disappear from sight without perishing, arguing that perception does not prove eternity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposites as signs
Maturidi lists pairs of opposites (good/bad, small/large, beautiful/ugly, light/darkness) and calls them signs of change and passing away.
2
Understand the connection to annihilation
Change leads to annihilation (فناء). Since combination strengthens and dispersion destroys, the presence of change shows the world is perishable.
3
Draw the conclusion
What perishes cannot be self-existent, so the world must be contingent. Necessarily, it had a beginning (احتمال الابتداء).
4
Examine the refutation
Maturidi rejects the idea that things can disappear from sight without perishing, arguing that perception itself does not prove eternity; only proofs could, and they fail.

Fourth, the world has parts that grow and change, and finite parts cannot yield an infinite whole. Fifth, the world contains opposites (good/bad, light/darkness) which are signs of change and perishing; what perishes cannot be self-existent. He refutes the claim that something can disappear without perishing, arguing that perception does not prove eternity. The passage ends with a declaration of human helplessness.

Key Terms
آيات
التغير
الزوال
الفناء
النشر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that things can simply cease to be seen without ceasing to exist, but Maturidi argues that change and dispersion lead to real annihilation.
Correction: The observable signs of change (opposites, growth, decay) indicate genuine perishing, not mere disappearance. Therefore, such things cannot be self-existent.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude from the presence of opposites in the world?
A) The world is eternal
B) The world is subject to annihilation and thus contingent
C) The world has no beginning
D) The world is self-sufficient

Key Takeaway
The changing and opposite nature of the world shows it is perishable and therefore contingent, depending on an eternal Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own life—your changing moods, successes and failures, health and sickness—do you see them as signs that you are not self-sufficient? How does this awareness affect your trust in the eternal Creator every moment?
?
If everything that changes must perish, what does that mean for your soul? Are you living as if your soul is perishable or as if it is held by the Eternal? What would change if you truly believed that your very existence depends on God from moment to moment?
?
The world's opposites (good and bad, light and darkness) are signs of annihilation. What attachments in your life are built on things that are changing and will pass away? Will you repent and anchor your heart in what does not perish?

The Argument from Motion and Rest
Maturidi demonstrates contingency from the alternation of motion and rest.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the alternation between motion and rest to prove that every body is contingent and points to a Creator.

Core Idea
Alternating states prove the body is not eternal.

Maturidi begins a logical argument based on a simple observation: every body is either in motion or at rest, and these two states cannot exist simultaneously. He then shows that this alternation forces us to conclude that the body itself is contingent. This lesson unpacks the precise reasoning, step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and English translation once to get the flow of the argument. Notice that it begins by stating the inferential method and then lays out premises.
The passage is dense; read slowly.
2
Identify the key premises
List the three main premises: (1) Every body is always either in motion or at rest. (2) Motion and rest cannot coexist. (3) Each occupies a finite portion of time.
The conclusion is that the body itself must be contingent.
3
Work through the logic
Follow the author's reasoning: if motion and rest cannot both be eternal, then one must have a beginning. Since the body is never without one of these states, the body cannot be eternal either.
This is a classic kalam contingency argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جـ وعلى ذلك طريق علم الاستدلال. مع ما أنه لا يخلو الجسم من حركة أو سكون، وليس لهما الاجتماع. فيزول من جملة أوقاته نصف الحركة ونصف السكون، وكل ذي نصف متناه. على أنهما إذ لا يجتمعان في القِدَم لزم حدث أحد الوجهين، وببطلانه أن يكون محدثًا في الأزل لزم في الآخر، وفي ذلك حدث ما لا يخلو عنه وأيضا إن كل جسم لا يخلو عن سكون دائم أو حركة دائمة أُوهِمَا، وما هو عليه منهما مدفوع إليه مُستخر به ومجعول لمنافع غيره. وإذا كان ذلك وصف جواهر العالم التي لا
C: And upon that is the way of inferential knowledge. Also, no body is free from motion or rest, and they cannot coexist. So half of its times are taken by motion and half by rest, and every half is finite. Since they cannot coexist in eternity, the contingency of one of the two states is necessary, and by its nullity [i.e., if it were eternal] that it be originated in pre-eternity, then the other [state] is necessary, and in that is the contingency of that which is never free from them. Also, every body is not free from perpetual rest or perpetual motion—these are assumed—and what it is upon from them is forced upon it, subjugated, and made for the benefits of others. And if that is the description of the substances of the world which do not...
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage presents the third way: inferential knowledge (ʿilm al-istidlāl). Maturidi first argues from the alternation of motion and rest in bodies. Since a body cannot be simultaneously in motion and at rest, and each state occupies finite portions of time, the body must be contingent—it is subject to change. If motion and rest cannot both be eternal, then one must have a beginning, and since the body cannot exist without either, the body itself is contingent. The second argument: every body is either perpetually in motion or perpetually at rest, but these are assumed states; moreover, the body is compelled into its state and made to serve others. Such subjugation and being for another's benefit prove the body is created. The passage cuts off before completion, but the conclusion is clear: the world's substances are contingent.

Key Terms
الحركة
السكون
القدم
الحدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that the argument from motion is only about living things or that it proves motion is eternal.
Correction: Maturidi applies the argument to all bodies, and the conclusion is that the body is contingent, not that motion is eternal.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi argue from the fact that motion and rest cannot coexist in a body?
A) The body must be eternal
B) The body must be contingent
C) The body must be composed of atoms
D) The body must be alive

Key Takeaway
The alternation of motion and rest in every body proves that the body is contingent and depends on a Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your life is a constant alternation between activity and stillness. If neither can be eternal, what does that say about your claim to any moment as your own? How will you use the knowledge that your very existence is borrowed to orient your next action?
?
The argument shows that you are never truly self-sufficient—every state you experience points to a source beyond you. When you feel self-reliant, which of your abilities can you honestly attribute to yourself? Will you today acknowledge your dependence on Allah in every movement and every pause?
?
If the alternation of motion and rest proves that the body is contingent, then your physical existence is a sign of Allah's power. What is one concrete change in your daily routine that will remind you that your next breath is a gift, not a right? Commit to it today.

The Argument from Subjugation
Maturidi proves contingency through the subjugation of bodies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the subjugation of bodies to serve others as evidence for their being created and not eternal.

Core Idea
Forced to serve others, bodies cannot be eternal.

Maturidi adds a second argument: every body is compelled into its state of motion or rest and is subjugated to benefit others. This subjugation (taskhīr) indicates that the body is not self-sufficient but is created by a wise Maker. This lesson explores the implications of subjugation.

Guided Path
1
Read the argument

The passage is incomplete, but the reasoning is clear.

Understand key terms

These terms imply lack of self-determination.
3
Reflect on implications
Ask yourself: if a body is forced into its state and serves purposes beyond itself, can it be eternal? What does this say about its origin?
The answer is that it must be created.

Maturidi's Argument Against the Eternity of Bodies
Maturidi argues that a body's dependence on accidents prevents its eternity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical argument that a body cannot be eternal because it is always dependent on temporally originated accidents.

Core Idea
A body's dependence on temporary accidents proves it cannot be eternal.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents a concise but powerful argument against the eternity of bodies. He begins by noting that a body is always accompanied by accidents—non-essential attributes that come into being. The key move is to show that even the body's permanence depends on a continuous succession of accidents, making the body inherently temporal. This argument is foundational for establishing that only Allah is eternal.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
First, read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Notice the logical connectors like 'على أن' and 'فصار'.
The bracketed word [بالعدم] is uncertain; it does not affect the main argument.
2
Identify the two main claims
Identify the general claim about a body always having an accident, and the specific claim about the accident of permanence (بقاء) being the cause of the body's preservation.
3
Follow the logical chain
Trace the reasoning: if permanence is an accident that occurs temporally, and the body's existence continues only through a succession of such accidents, then the body cannot precede (be eternal) because it is always accompanied by temporal events.
The key insight is that the accident of permanence is itself temporally originated.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Some might think that because a body always has accidents, it could still be eternal. Consider why Maturidi's argument refutes this: the accidents themselves are temporal, so their presence necessitates the body's temporality.
5
Test your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you have grasped the core idea. Then try the activity to solidify your learning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يوصف جسم [بالعدم] مع حدث واحد لا يخلوا عنه ، فكذلك ما كثر منه. على أن حدوث البقاء في الجسم هو سبب إبقائه، ويدوم بقاؤه على دوام تتابع البقاء فيه . ولا يجوز أن يكون سبب قدم الجسم حدوث عرض فيه فصار هو عديله وقرينه؛ فلذلك لا يتقدمه. والله الموفق.
A body is described [as non-existent?] with a single accident that it does not lack, and likewise what is more than it. Because the temporal origination of permanence in the body is the cause of its preservation, and its permanence continues with the continuity of the succession of permanence in it. And it is not permissible that the cause of the body's eternity be the occurrence of an accident in it, so that accident becomes its equal and companion; therefore it does not precede it. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage presents an argument against the eternity of bodies. Maturidi begins by stating that a body is described by an accident (a non-essential attribute) that it never lacks. If a single accident is necessary, then multiple accidents are also necessary. Specifically, the accident of 'coming into being' (huduth) of permanence (baqa') in the body is the cause of its being preserved. The permanence of the body continues through the continuous succession of the accident of permanence. Therefore, the body is dependent on accidents. If the body were eternal, then the accident that causes its permanence would also be eternal, but that accident is a temporal occurrence (hadith) because it is an attribute that comes into being. Thus, a temporal accident cannot be the cause of an eternal body. So the body cannot be eternal because it is never free from temporally originated accidents.

Key Terms
جسم (body)
حدث (temporal origination/huduth)
بقاء (permanence/baqa')
عرض (accident)
قدم (eternity/qidam)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A body could be eternal even if it always has accidents.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the accidents themselves are temporally originated, so the body's dependence on them necessitates its temporality. An eternal being cannot be dependent on temporal causes.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi in this passage, why can a body not be eternal?
A) Because bodies are composed of atoms.
B) Because bodies are never free from temporally originated accidents.
C) Because bodies are created by God.
D) Because bodies have colors.

Key Takeaway
A body's dependence on temporally originated accidents, including permanence, proves that it cannot be eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Is there any area of your life where you act as if something will last forever—your health, wealth, or relationships? How does Maturidi's argument that even permanence is continuously renewed by Allah challenge that assumption, and what practical change can you make today to realign your heart with this reality?
?
If your very existence is being re-created moment by moment through the accident of permanence, what does that say about your independence from Allah? Are you living with the awareness that you need Him every instant? What would change if you truly internalized this?
?
Maturidi argues that a body cannot be eternal because its permanence is caused by a temporal accident. How does this truth affect your understanding of your own mortality and your reliance on Allah? What is one concrete way you can remind yourself daily of your complete dependence on Him?

Refutation of the objection from color analogy
Maturidi refutes the objection from color analogy

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the objection that a body could be eternal despite being inseparable from its accidents, using the example of color.

Core Idea
A body’s temporality is tied to the accident of existence, not to any specific accident like color.

In this passage, Maturidi addresses a clever objection: if a body can exist without color, why can't it exist without accidents altogether, and thus be eternal? He shows that the objector confuses one type of accident with another. The key is the accident of 'event' (existence after non-existence) which is inseparable from a temporal body.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the English translation. Let the argument's structure come through.
The passage contains an objection followed by Maturidi's response.
2
Identify the objector's point
The objector argues: a body is never found without color, but that does not mean it is color itself. Similarly, a body might not need to have accidents.
The objector is trying to use color as an analogy to deny the necessity of accidents.
3
Understand Maturidi's distinction
Maturidi distinguishes between the accident of 'event' (existence after non-existence) and other accidents like color. The accident of existence is inseparable from a temporal body.
Color is a quality that inheres in a colored thing, while 'event' describes the body's very coming into being.
4
See how color is not analogous
The body as a whole is never free from colors; it does not precede them but occurs simultaneously. Similarly, origination (temporality) is simultaneous with the body.
Therefore, the objection fails: the analogy with color does not prove that a body could be without all accidents.
5
Conclude the refutation
Maturidi has shown that the temporality of the body is proven by its inseparable accident of existence. The objector's analogy is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وعارض بعض مَن يقول بهذا : بما لا يوجد الشيء بلا لون، ثم لا يجب أن يكون لونًا. وذلك لا معنى له، لما أن الحدث هو وصف الكون بعد أن لم يكن، فإذا وُجِدَ غيره غير مفارقة - وفيه هذا الوصف - لزمَهُ هذا الحكم ; وليس اللون بلون المعنى يوجد في المتلون به، لذلك اختلفا ; لكن جملة الجسم إذ لا يخلو من الألوان فلا يسبقها ولكن يسبق واحدا فواحدا، وكذلك أمر الإحداث.
And someone who says this objected: that a thing is not found without color, yet it is not necessary that it be color. But that has no meaning, because 'event' is the description of existence after it was not. So if something other than it is found without separation—and it has this description—this judgment necessarily applies to it. And color is not like the meaning that exists in the thing colored; therefore they differ. But the entirety of the body, since it is not free from colors, does not precede them, but precedes them one by one. And likewise the matter of origination.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage addresses an objection to the previous argument. Someone objects: 'A thing can exist without color, but that does not mean it must be color.' The objector is trying to say that a body can exist without accidents, just as it exists without color, so it could be eternal. Maturidi refutes this: the accident of 'event' (existence after non-existence) is a description of the body's coming into being. If another accident accompanies the body without separation, then the same judgment (temporality) applies to that accident. Color is different because it is a quality that inheres in a colored thing, not the same as the accident of existence. The body is never free from colors (or other accidents), so it cannot precede them; rather, it occurs simultaneously with them. Similarly, the origination of the body is tied to its accidents.

Key Terms
لون (color)
حدث (event/temporal origination)
جسم (body)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: If a body can exist without one accident (like color), it could exist without all accidents, thus being eternal.
Correction: Maturidi explains that the accident of 'event' (existence after non-existence) is inseparable from a temporal body, unlike color which is a separable attribute.

Checkpoint
What is the point of the objector's analogy involving color?
A) That bodies are colored.
B) That a body can exist without an accident, so it could be eternal.
C) That color is an eternal attribute.
D) That accidents are necessary for bodies.

Key Takeaway
The temporality of a body is proven by its inseparable accident of existence, not by any particular property like color.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself thinking that some part of your life or existence is 'just there' without needing Allah? This lesson shows that even the accident of existence itself is inseparable from dependence. Where do you need to personally acknowledge your utter dependence on Allah in a specific area you've been neglecting?
?
The objector in this lesson assumed that because a body can lack one accident (color), it could lack them all. Where in your beliefs have you made a similar assumption—taking an isolated experience or blessing as evidence that you don't need Allah's constant support? What would it look like to repent from that mindset today?
?
Maturidi's refutation hinges on the fact that the accident of 'existence after non-existence' is inescapable for every created thing. When you reflect on your own existence—your breath, your heartbeat, your next moment—are you truly living with the awareness that you are being brought into existence at every instant? What will you do to keep this reality at the forefront of your heart for the rest of this day?
