Refuting Sensory Empiricism
Maturidi refutes the sensory empiricist objection to creation ex nihilo.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the claim that creation from nothing cannot be known because it is not sensed, and why rational cognition transcends sensory limits.

Core Idea
Rational truths are known by reason, not by the senses.

In this passage, Maturidi confronts an objection: some deny that something can be made from nothing because we never observe it. He argues that this objection is based on a flawed epistemology that limits knowledge to sensation. Instead, many rational concepts—like permissibility and impermissibility—are known without being sensed. Therefore, the objection is invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the first line: 'Whoever says: The making of something from nothing is not known'. This is the view Maturidi refutes.
2
Understand Maturidi's counter
He says the objector is judging by sensory existence. But rational cognitions are beyond sensation. List the examples: permissibility, impermissibility, separation not annihilation.
These are all non-sensory concepts.
3
Apply the principle
Maturidi says our case (creation ex nihilo) is similar: it involves intellect, hearing, sight, soul—things whose making is not known by sensation. So the objection fails.
The reasoning is consistent: if you accept non-sensory knowledge in other cases, you must accept it here.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن قال: لا يُعلم صُنع شيء لا من شيء فهو المقدر بالموجود حساً. والمعارف نفسها خارجة عن الحس، وكذلك القول بيجوز ولا يجوز ، وكذلك ما يدعى من التفرق لا الهلاك لا نعرفه بالحس، ثم قال به، فمثله ما نحن فيه مع ما كان فيه عقل وسمع وبصر وروح وغير ذلك مما لا يدري ممّ صُنْعَ، فذلك يمنعه القول بما قال. وأيضاً إنه إذا لم يكن أحد يخبرنا عن قِدَمه أو كان أزليا من جوهر العالم فلا وجه للعلم به إلا بالاستدلال. ثم لا نعلم كتابة بلا كاتب، ولا تفرّقا إلا بمفرق ، وكذلك الاجتماع، وكذلك السكون والحركة، فيلزم في جملة العالم ذلك؛ إذ هو مؤلف مفرق، بل الأعجوبة في تأليف العالم أرفع، فهو أحق أن لا يتفرق ولا يجتمع إلا بغيره. ثم كل ما في الشاهد من التأليف والكتابة يكون أحدث ممن به كان، فمثله جميع العالم، إذ هو في معنى ما ذكرت . وبالله التوفيق. ولو تكلّف الاستقصاء في مثل هذا لَيَخرج عن طوق البشر ; إذ ما من شيء مما يُحس أو يُسْمَع به من أجزاء العالم إلا ودلالة حدثه ظاهرة مِنْ جَهْله بابتداء حاله وبإصلاح ما يفسد أو بما يُنشئ مثله، وعجزه عما به حفظ نفسه، أو تقليبه عن جوهره; مع ما فيه الخبيث والقبيح والذليل المهان الذي لولا تدبير غيره فيه ما احتمل أن يكون كذلك. وبالله التوفيق.
And whoever says: 'The making of something from nothing is not known' — he is judging by sensory existence. But rational cognitions themselves are beyond sensation, and similarly the statements of 'permissible' and 'impermissible', and similarly what is claimed of separation not annihilation — we do not know it by sensation, yet he asserts it. So the like of that is our case here, with what contains intellect, hearing, sight, soul, and other things whose making is not known — that prevents him from saying what he said. Moreover, if no one informs us of its eternity, or if it is eternal from the substance of the world, then there is no way to know it except by inference. Then we do not know writing without a writer, nor separation without a separator, nor composition, nor rest and motion. So that necessarily applies to the whole world, for it is composed and separated. But the wonder in the composition of the world is higher, so it is more deserving that it does not separate or come together except by another. Then everything in the observable world of composition and writing is more recent than the one through whom it is, and likewise the whole world, for it is in the meaning of what I mentioned. And God is the Grantor of success. And if one were to exhaustively investigate such matters, it would exceed human capacity, for there is nothing of the sensory or audible parts of the world except that the evidence of its temporal origin is manifest from ignorance of its initial state, and from fixing what is corrupted, or from creating its like, and its incapacity for self-preservation, or its alteration from its essence — with what is in it of the vile, the ugly, and the contemptible lowly, which, without another's management, would not bear to be as such. And God is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This is a comprehensive argument for the temporal creation of the world. Maturidi refutes those who deny creation ex nihilo, claiming that it is not known by sensation. He points out that many rational concepts (like permissibility) are not sensible, yet we accept them. So the claim that something cannot be made from nothing is baseless because it is a rational judgment. He then gives an analogy: we do not know writing without a writer, separation without a separator, composition, rest, motion without an agent. The world is composed and separated, so it must have an agent. The world's composition is even more wondrous, so it is even more dependent on an external cause. Then he cites empirical evidence: every part of the world shows signs of its temporal origin — from the fact that we do not know its beginning, from the need to repair what is corrupted, from its inability to produce its like, from its inability to preserve itself, and from its containing lowly and vile things that require an external manager. All these indicate that the world is created and dependent on a Creator.

Key Terms
صنع (making/sun')
حس (sensation)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Creation from nothing is impossible because we never observe it happening.
Correction: Maturidi shows that many rational truths are not known by sensation, so sensory limitation does not disprove creation ex nihilo.

Checkpoint
What is Maturidi's main argument against those who deny creation from nothing?
A) They rely on sensation, but many rational truths are beyond sensation.
B) They are atheists.
C) They reject revelation.
D) They misunderstand the term 'nothing'.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that the objection to creation ex nihilo based on sensory experience is self-defeating because rational concepts are not limited to sensation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When has your own reliance on the senses caused you to doubt a truth of faith that is only known by reason? How does this lesson expose the weakness of that doubt? Will you now consciously submit your intellect to rational proofs of the unseen?
?
You accept that some actions are permissible and others impermissible without ever having seen a sign on them. Do you also accept that God created the universe from nothing without having seen it? What prevents your heart from affirming this as readily?
?
Think of a specific instance where you dismissed a belief because it wasn't 'scientific' or 'observable'. How does Maturidi's argument challenge that mindset? What is one step you will take this week to prioritize rational conviction over sensory evidence in your faith?

The Analogy of Composition and Writing
Maturidi uses analogy from writing and composition to argue for a creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument from analogy: that the world's composition requires a creator, just as writing requires a writer.

Core Idea
The composition of the world is even more wondrous than human composition, so it must have an external agent.

Maturidi now presents a positive argument for creation. He draws an analogy from everyday experience: we know that writing requires a writer, and separation requires a separator. The world is composed and separated, so it must have an agent. Moreover, the composition of the world is far more impressive than any human work, so it is even more dependent on an external cause.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the inference
Maturidi says if we have no direct report of the world's eternity, we must use inference (istidlal). This sets up the need for reasoning.
2
List the analogies
He lists: writing without writer, separation without separator, composition, rest and motion — all require an agent. These are known from the observable world.
These are basic causal principles.
3
Apply to the world
The whole world is composed and separated, so it must have an agent. He emphasizes that the world's composition is even more wondrous, so it is even more dependent.
The argument is a fortiori: if human artifacts need makers, then the world needs a maker even more.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن قال: لا يُعلم صُنع شيء لا من شيء فهو المقدر بالموجود حساً. والمعارف نفسها خارجة عن الحس، وكذلك القول بيجوز ولا يجوز ، وكذلك ما يدعى من التفرق لا الهلاك لا نعرفه بالحس، ثم قال به، فمثله ما نحن فيه مع ما كان فيه عقل وسمع وبصر وروح وغير ذلك مما لا يدري ممّ صُنْعَ، فذلك يمنعه القول بما قال. وأيضاً إنه إذا لم يكن أحد يخبرنا عن قِدَمه أو كان أزليا من جوهر العالم فلا وجه للعلم به إلا بالاستدلال. ثم لا نعلم كتابة بلا كاتب، ولا تفرّقا إلا بمفرق ، وكذلك الاجتماع، وكذلك السكون والحركة، فيلزم في جملة العالم ذلك؛ إذ هو مؤلف مفرق، بل الأعجوبة في تأليف العالم أرفع، فهو أحق أن لا يتفرق ولا يجتمع إلا بغيره. ثم كل ما في الشاهد من التأليف والكتابة يكون أحدث ممن به كان، فمثله جميع العالم، إذ هو في معنى ما ذكرت . وبالله التوفيق. ولو تكلّف الاستقصاء في مثل هذا لَيَخرج عن طوق البشر ; إذ ما من شيء مما يُحس أو يُسْمَع به من أجزاء العالم إلا ودلالة حدثه ظاهرة مِنْ جَهْله بابتداء حاله وبإصلاح ما يفسد أو بما يُنشئ مثله، وعجزه عما به حفظ نفسه، أو تقليبه عن جوهره; مع ما فيه الخبيث والقبيح والذليل المهان الذي لولا تدبير غيره فيه ما احتمل أن يكون كذلك. وبالله التوفيق.

?
If you were to treat your own existence as a piece of writing that requires a Writer, how would that change the way you live today? What would you do differently knowing that you are not self-sufficient but created?

Empirical Evidence of Temporal Origin
Maturidi enumerates empirical signs of the world's temporal origin.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the empirical signs Maturidi lists that indicate the world's temporal creation and dependence on a Creator.

Core Idea
Every part of the world shows signs of being created and dependent.

Maturidi concludes his argument by pointing to empirical evidence from the world itself. He says that a full investigation is beyond human capacity, but every sensory part of the world manifests evidence of its temporal origin. These signs include our ignorance of its beginning, the need for repair, inability to reproduce itself, inability to preserve itself, and the presence of vile and ugly things that require external management.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening statement
Maturidi says that exhaustive investigation is beyond human capacity, but the evidence is manifest in every part.
This sets the scope: we don't need to prove everything, just point to clear signs.
2
List the signs
Identify each sign: ignorance of initial state, fixing corruption, creating its like, incapacity for self-preservation, alteration from essence, and the presence of vile things.
These are all properties that point to contingency.
3
Understand the conclusion
All these indicate that the world cannot exist by itself but requires external management. This supports the argument for a Creator.
The argument is inductive: from many signs, we infer a cause.

Source Text

beginner

The First Proof: Contingency and Self-Composition
Maturidi states the first proof: contingency and lack of self-composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first proof that the world has a creator based on the established contingency and the fact that nothing in the observable world composes or separates by itself.

Core Idea
If nothing in the world creates itself, the world must be created.

Maturidi begins his proofs for a creator by building on the previously established fact that the world is contingent (ḥudūth). He then presents a direct observable argument: in our experience, no object in the world brings itself into existence or organizes itself without an external cause. This forms the foundation of the first proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic once to get the rhythm. Notice that Maturidi first restates the contingency of the world, then adds a new point about self-composition.
The phrase 'يجتمع بنفسه ويفترق' is key: nothing composes itself.
2
Understand the logical structure
Maturidi is arguing: (1) The world is contingent. (2) In the observable world (shāhid), no object composes or separates by itself. (3) Therefore, the world must have been brought into being by another (i.e., a creator).
The word 'ثبت' (it is established) is used twice to indicate certainty.
3
Connect to the bigger picture
This proof uses both a priori knowledge (contingency) and empirical observation (nothing self-composes). It is a blend of rational and empirical reasoning.
This is the first of three proofs; each builds on the idea of dependence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم الدليل على أن للعالَم مُحدثًا أنه ثبت حدثه بما بينا، وبما لا يُوجد شيء منه في الشاهد يجتمع بنفسه ويفترق، ثبت أن ذلك كان بغيره. والله الموفق. والثاني أن العالم لو كان بنفسه لم يكن وقت أحق به من وقت، ولا حال أولى به من حال، ولا صفة أليق به من صفة؛ وإذا كان على أوقات وأحوال وصفات مختلفة ثبت أنه لم يكن به، ولو كان الحاز كل شيء لنفسه أحوالاً هي أحسن الأحوال والصفات وخيرها، فيبطل به الشرور والقبائح؛ فدل وجود ذلك على كونه بغيره. والله الموفق. وأيضاً إن العالم نوعان: حي وميت. وكل حي جاهل بابتدائه، عاجز عن إنشاء مثله وإصلاح ما فسد منه وقت قوته وكماله فثبت أنه كان بغيره. والميت أحق بذلك.
Abū Manṣūr said: Then the proof that the world has a creator is that its contingency has been established by what we have explained, and [by the fact] that nothing of it is found in the observable world that composes itself and separates; so it is established that that was by another. And God is the grantor of success. The second [proof] is that if the world were by itself, no time would be more fitting for it than any time, no state more appropriate for it than any state, and no attribute more suitable for it than any attribute; but since it exists in different times, states, and attributes, it is established that it was not by itself. And if everything had for itself states that are the best of states and attributes and the best of them, then evil and ugly things would be negated; so the existence of [evil and ugly things] indicates that it [the world] is by another. And God is the grantor of success. And also, the world is of two types: living and dead. Every living [being] is ignorant of its beginning, incapable of creating its like or repairing what becomes corrupt from it at the time of its strength and perfection, so it is established that it was by another. And the dead [being] is even more deserving of that.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs for the existence of a creator (muḥdith) of the world. The first proof: the world's contingency (ḥudūth) has already been established, and since nothing in the observable world composes or separates by itself, it must have been made by another. The second proof: if the world were self-sufficient, it would not have specific times, states, or attributes that are more fitting than others. Its actual variation in times and states shows it is not self-existent. Moreover, if everything self-existent could choose its best state, evil and ugly things would not exist; their existence shows the world is not self-sufficient. The third proof: the world contains living and dead things. Every living being is ignorant of its origin and incapable of creating its like or repairing itself at its peak, proving it depends on another; the dead are even more in need. Thus, the world has a creator.

Key Terms
محدث
حدوث
شاهد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that things can arise spontaneously or organize themselves without a cause (e.g., via natural randomness).
Correction: Maturidi observes that in our experience, nothing in the world composes or separates by itself. This empirical fact refutes spontaneous self-creation; every composite thing depends on something else.

Checkpoint
According to the first proof, why must the world have a creator?
A) Because the world is eternal and unchangeable.
B) Because the world is contingent and nothing in it composes itself.
C) Because the world has a beautiful design.
D) Because the world is made of atoms.

Key Takeaway
The world's contingency combined with the observable fact that nothing composes itself proves it was brought into being by a creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe the universe in its order and complexity, do you truly see it as dependent on God every moment, or do you tend to think of it as self-sustaining? What specific habit can you adopt today to remind yourself that your very existence is 'by another'?
?
Maturidi says 'nothing composes itself.' In what area of your life do you act as though your own efforts are self-sufficient, forgetting that every success comes from God? Write down one concrete way you will acknowledge God's role in your accomplishments this week.
?
The proof begins with the reality that the world is contingent. Have you deeply internalized that you could cease to exist at any moment? If not, what holds you back from that awareness, and how would your priorities change right now if you truly believed it?

The Second Proof: Variation in Time, State, and Attribute
Maturidi gives the second proof: variation and evil show the world is not self-sufficient.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second proof that the world has a creator based on the variation of times, states, and attributes, and the presence of evil.

Core Idea
If the world were self-sufficient, it would not vary across times, states, and attributes; its variation proves dependence.

Maturidi presents a second argument: if the world were self-existing (bil-nafsihi), it would not have specific times, states, or attributes that are more fitting than others. But the world clearly shows variation and also contains evil and ugliness, which would not exist if everything could choose its best state. Therefore, the world is not self-sufficient but depends on another.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the second proof carefully. Notice the conditional structure: 'if the world were by itself... then no time would be more fitting... but it is different, so it is not by itself.'
The phrase 'الحاز' may be a corruption; treat it as 'الحال' (state) as per the commentary.
2
Understand the two arguments within the proof
Maturidi uses two sub-arguments: (a) variation of times, states, and attributes shows it is not self-sufficient; (b) if everything could choose its best state, evil would not exist, but evil exists, so it is not self-sufficient.
The second sub-argument is a reductio ad absurdum.
3
Reflect on the theological implication
This proof not only establishes a creator but also indirectly addresses the problem of evil: the presence of evil indicates that the world is not self-determining but is subject to a creator who allows both good and evil.
Maturidi uses the existence of evil as evidence for a creator, not against one.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : ثم الدليل على أن للعالَم مُحدثًا أنه ثبت حدثه بما بينا، وبما لا يُوجد شيء منه في الشاهد يجتمع بنفسه ويفترق، ثبت أن ذلك كان بغيره. والله الموفق. والثاني أن العالم لو كان بنفسه لم يكن وقت أحق به من وقت، ولا حال أولى به من حال، ولا صفة أليق به من صفة؛ وإذا كان على أوقات وأحوال وصفات مختلفة ثبت أنه لم يكن به، ولو كان الحاز كل شيء لنفسه أحوالاً هي أحسن الأحوال والصفات وخيرها، فيبطل به الشرور والقبائح؛ فدل وجود ذلك على كونه بغيره. والله الموفق. وأيضاً إن العالم نوعان: حي وميت. وكل حي جاهل بابتدائه، عاجز عن إنشاء مثله وإصلاح ما فسد منه وقت قوته وكماله فثبت أنه كان بغيره. والميت أحق بذلك.
Abū Manṣūr said: Then the proof that the world has a creator is that its contingency has been established by what we have explained, and [by the fact] that nothing of it is found in the observable world that composes itself and separates; so it is established that that was by another. And God is the grantor of success. The second [proof] is that if the world were by itself, no time would be more fitting for it than any time, no state more appropriate for it than any state, and no attribute more suitable for it than any attribute; but since it exists in different times, states, and attributes, it is established that it was not by itself. And if everything had for itself states that are the best of states and attributes and the best of them, then evil and ugly things would be negated; so the existence of [evil and ugly things] indicates that it [the world] is by another. And God is the grantor of success. And also, the world is of two types: living and dead. Every living [being] is ignorant of its beginning, incapable of creating its like or repairing what becomes corrupt from it at the time of its strength and perfection, so it is established that it was by another. And the dead [being] is even more deserving of that.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents three proofs for the existence of a creator (muḥdith) of the world. The first proof: the world's contingency (ḥudūth) has already been established, and since nothing in the observable world composes or separates by itself, it must have been made by another. The second proof: if the world were self-sufficient, it would not have specific times, states, or attributes that are more fitting than others. Its actual variation in times and states shows it is not self-existent. Moreover, if everything self-existent could choose its best state, evil and ugly things would not exist; their existence shows the world is not self-sufficient. The third proof: the world contains living and dead things. Every living being is ignorant of its origin and incapable of creating its like or repairing itself at its peak, proving it depends on another; the dead are even more in need. Thus, the world has a creator.

Key Terms
وقت
حال
صفة
شر
قبيح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the world's suitability for life shows it is self-sufficient or designed by itself.
Correction: Maturidi argues the opposite: the fact that the world has specific times and states that are more fitting than others actually proves it is not self-sufficient but depends on a chooser.

Checkpoint
According to the second proof, what does the existence of evil and ugly things indicate?
A) That the world is self-sufficient.
B) That the world is not by itself but by another.
C) That evil is an illusion.
D) That the world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
The world's variation in times, states, and attributes, along with the presence of evil, proves it is not self-sufficient but depends on a creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider a time when you felt in control of your life, as if you were self-sufficient. How does this proof expose the illusion of self-sufficiency? Can you identify concrete dependencies you forgot?
?
Evil and suffering are real. When you encounter evil, do you instinctively blame God or question His goodness? How does Maturidi's proof redirect your focus from doubt to seeing evil as a sign of your own need for a wise Creator?
?
If everything depended on itself, it would choose only the best for itself. Look at your own choices—how often do you choose what is truly best? What does that reveal about your dependence on Allah for guidance and success?

The Third Proof: Incapacity of Living and Dead Things
Maturidi presents the third proof: living and dead things show incapacity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third proof that the world has a creator based on the incapacity of living beings to know their origin or produce their like, and the even greater incapacity of dead matter.

Core Idea
Living things cannot create themselves or repair their decay; dead matter is even more helpless, proving a creator.

Maturidi's third proof examines the nature of beings in the world. He divides the world into living and dead. Every living being is ignorant of its own origin, unable to create another like itself, and unable to repair itself at the peak of its strength. Dead matter is even more clearly dependent. Thus, all things point to a creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the third proof. Notice the classification: 'العالم نوعان: حي وميت'. Then Maturidi describes the characteristics of each.
The proof is simple but powerful: living things show their limitation, and dead things are even more limited.
2
Analyze the characteristics of living beings
Maturidi lists three limitations: (1) ignorance of their beginning, (2) inability to create their like, (3) inability to repair corruption at their peak. This establishes their need for a creator.
The phrase 'جاهل بابتدائه' means even humans don't know how they began.
3
Extend the argument to dead things
Maturidi then states 'والميت أحق بذلك', meaning if living things are needy, non-living matter is even more so. This completes the proof.
The argument is cumulative: from the highest (living) to the lowest (dead).

?

Proof from Combination and Separation
Maturidi's proof from combination and separation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the combination and separation of worldly things require an external originator.

Core Idea
Nothing in the world comes together or falls apart by itself—it all points to a Creator.

Maturidi presents a concise yet powerful proof for the existence of a Creator based on everyday observation. He argues that since nothing in the witnessed world combines or separates without an external cause, the world itself must be contingent. This lesson unpacks that reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi refers to a prior proof that the world is temporal.
2
Identify the key premise
Identify the statement: 'nothing of it is found in the witnessed world that combines or separates by itself.' This is the empirical observation that drives the argument.
Think of examples: a clay pot doesn't shape itself; clouds don't form without atmospheric processes.
3
Follow the conclusion
Maturidi concludes that because nothing combines or separates by itself, the world's existence must be due to another (bighayrihi). That 'other' is the Creator.
This is an argument from contingency: the world's features depend on an external cause.
4
Reflect on the proof's strength
Consider why this proof is accessible: it relies on common experience, not abstract metaphysics. It resonates with everyday observation.
This contrasts with proofs that require specialized philosophical concepts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: ثم الدليل على أن للعالم محدثًا أنه ثبت حدثه بما بينا، وبما لا يوجد شيء منه في الشاهد يجتمع بنفسه ويفترق، ثبت أن ذلك كان بغيره. والله الموفق.
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: Then the proof that the world has an originator is that its temporality has been established by what we have explained, and because nothing of it is found in the witnessed world that combines or separates by itself; thus it is established that that was by another. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents a proof for the existence of a creator (muhdith) based on the observation that in the witnessed world (al-shahid), nothing comes together or falls apart by itself. Maturidi first references a prior proof that the world is temporal (hadith). Then he adds that in our experience, no object combines or separates without an external agent. Therefore, the combination and separation observed in the world must be due to something else, i.e., a creator. The conclusion is that the world exists through another (bighayrihi). The proof is concise and relies on empirical observation.

Key Terms
محدث
حدث
شاهد
اجتماع
افتراق
بنفسه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof requires accepting unseen metaphysical principles or prior theological commitments.
Correction: The proof is based solely on ordinary observation of the physical world. It does not assume any unseen causes; it simply notes that objects do not combine or separate by themselves, pointing to an external agent.

Checkpoint
What is the basis of this proof?
A) The world is eternal.
B) Nothing in the world combines or separates by itself.
C) All things are created by chance.
D) The world is composed of atoms.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's proof shows that the observed need for an external cause in combination and separation points to a Creator of the world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness the intricate combination of cells in your own body, do you pause to acknowledge that it did not happen by itself? How would your awareness of Allah change if you saw His hand in every biological process?
?
Have you ever attributed a success or a failure to 'coincidence' or 'luck'—essentially saying it happened by itself? Will you commit to replacing those words with 'by the will of Allah' from now on?
?
If you truly believed that nothing in the universe combines or separates except by Allah's permission, would you still fear failure or the loss of anything? What would you dare to do for His sake if that belief were absolute in your heart?

Second Proof: From Indifference to Time and State
Maturidi argues that if the world were self-sufficient, no time or state would be more appropriate.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's modal argument that the world's variation in times, states, and attributes proves it is not self-sufficient but caused by another.

Core Idea
If the world were self-sufficient, it would have no privileged time or state.

This lesson examines Maturidi's second proof for the world's dependence on a creator. He argues that if the world existed by itself, no specific time, state, or attribute would be more fitting than another. But we observe variation and imperfection, which shows the world is not self-caused. This is a modal argument, not a design argument, focusing on the logic of indifference.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the logical structure: premise, observation, conclusion, and a subsidiary argument about evil.
Pay attention to the conditional 'if the world were by itself'.
2
Identify the main argument
Find the core premise: if the world were self-sufficient, no time, state, or attribute would be more appropriate. Then note the observation of variation and the conclusion that the world is not by itself.
This is the backbone of the proof.
3
Examine the subsidiary argument
Notice the additional point: if everything by itself had the best states, evil and abominations would be impossible. Their existence confirms dependence.
This strengthens the case against self-sufficiency.
4
Understand the type of argument
Recognize that this is a modal argument about indifference, not a design argument. It doesn't invoke complexity or purpose, but logical necessity.
Contrast with common misconceptions.
5
Reflect on the implication
Consider how this proof leads to affirming a creator (the Grantor of success). Ask yourself: what does this argument assume about causality?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والثاني أن العالم لو كان بنفسه لم يكن وقت أحق به من وقت، ولا حال أولى به من حال، ولا صفة أليق به من صفة؛ وإذا كان على أوقات وأحوال وصفات مختلفة ثبت أنه لم يكن به. ولو كان الجائز أن يكون كل شيء لنفسه أحوالاً هي أحسن الأحوال والصفات وخيرها، فيبطل به الشرور والقبائح؛ فدل وجود ذلك على كونه بغيره. والله الموفق.
And the second: that if the world were by itself, no time would be more deserving of it than any other time, nor any state more appropriate for it than any other state, nor any attribute more suitable for it than any other attribute. But since it is upon different times, states, and attributes, it is established that it is not by itself. And if it were possible that everything by itself has states that are the best of states, attributes, and the best of them, then evil and abominations would be invalidated by it. Thus, the existence of that indicates that it is by another. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage contains a second proof for the world's need for a creator. Maturidi argues that if the world were self-sufficient (by itself), then no specific time of existence, state, or attribute would be more appropriate than another. But we observe the world in different times, states, and attributes, which shows it is not self-caused. He adds that if everything had the best possible states by itself, evil and bad things would not exist; their existence indicates that the world is from another. Thus, the variety and imperfection in the world point to an external cause.

Key Terms
بنفسه
وقت
حال
صفة
شرور
قبائح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This is an argument from design, similar to Paley's watchmaker analogy.
Correction: It is a modal argument about indifference and necessity, not about complexity, order, or purpose. It does not rely on observed design but on logical impossibility of self-sufficiency given variation.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does Maturidi draw from the world having different times and states?
A) The world is eternal.
B) The world is by itself.
C) The world is not by itself.
D) The world has no creator.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's second proof uses the observed variation in times, states, and attributes to show that the world is not self-sufficient, but dependent on a creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your life do you still act as if you are self-sufficient, ignoring the evidence of your dependence on God? What would it look like to humbly acknowledge that dependence today?
?
This proof shows that the existence of evil points to our need for God. When you experience suffering, do you feel resentment, or does it drive you to rely on Him? How can you change your response next time?
?
Look at the changing states in your own life—your moods, health, circumstances. Do you take these as mere facts, or do they remind you that you are not self-sufficient? What specific step will you take today to turn these variations into moments of turning to God?

Third Proof: From Living and Dead
Maturidi argues that living beings are initially ignorant and unable to create themselves.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's third proof for the existence of a creator based on the initial ignorance and inability of living beings.

Core Idea
Every living being starts ignorant and incapable—proof it owes its existence to another.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents a simple yet profound argument from observation. He notes that every living being begins in a state of ignorance and is unable to create a copy of itself or even repair its own body at its peak. This points to a dependency on an external cause, and dead things are even more in need.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, carefully read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the concise structure of the argument.
The Arabic uses 'وأيضاً' to connect to previous proofs.
2
Identify the two categories
Maturidi divides the world into living and dead. Identify why this classification is important for the proof.
3
Analyze the reasoning about the living
Examine the statement: every living being is initially ignorant and unable to create its like or repair itself. Ask: what does this tell us about its independence?
Note the phrase 'وقت قوته وكماله' (at the time of its strength and perfection) to emphasize the inability even at peak.
4
Apply the a fortiori argument
Consider why the dead are 'more worthy' of being by another. This strengthens the conclusion.
This is a legal principle (qiyas awwalawi) applied to theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً إن العالم نوعان: حي وميت. وكل حي جاهل بابتدائه، عاجز عن إنشاء مثله وإصلاح ما فسد منه وقت قوته وكماله فثبت أنه كان بغيره. والميت أحق بذلك.
Also, indeed the world is of two types: living and dead. Every living thing is ignorant at its beginning, incapable of creating its like or repairing what is corrupted from it at the time of its strength and perfection. So it is established that it is by another. And the dead is more worthy of that.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage presents a third proof: the world consists of living and dead beings. Every living being begins in ignorance and is unable to create another like itself or repair its own corruption. This inability even at the peak of its strength shows it is not self-sufficient. Therefore, it must be by another. The same applies a fortiori to dead things, which have even less ability. This proof relies on the observed weakness and dependency of life.

Key Terms

ميت
جاهل

إصلاح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof is about biological evolution or the origin of life from non-life.
Correction: Maturidi's argument is not about how life emerges but about the dependence of living beings on an external cause. It is a metaphysical contingency argument.

Checkpoint

B) Because dead things have even less ability than living things.
C) Because dead things are simpler.
D) Because death is a proof of creation.

Key Takeaway
The inherent initial ignorance and inability of living beings demonstrate their dependency on a creator, and this applies even more to dead things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You are a living being. When you reflect on your own beginning, do you truly know how you came to be? Have you ever honestly admitted that your existence is not from yourself? What changes if you live as though your life is a gift rather than a right?
?
The passage points out that even at your peak strength and perfection, you are incapable of repairing what is corrupted in yourself. Think of a weakness or sin you cannot fix on your own. In what specific area are you still trying to be self-sufficient rather than turning to your Creator?
?
If dead things are even more worthy of being dependent, then every rock and star around you is screaming its need for a Creator. How does this transform the way you look at the world? Will you let the silent objects around you remind you of your own need for Allah today?

Fourth Proof: From Accidents and Substance
Maturidi argues that every substance is subject to accidents, and accidents need substances.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's fourth proof for the existence of God based on the mutual dependence of substances and accidents.

Core Idea
Substances and accidents need each other; neither is self-sufficient, so the world needs a Creator.

In this proof, Imam Maturidi examines the relationship between substances (jawāhir) and accidents (aʻrāḍ). He argues that every entity in the world is subject to accidents, and accidents cannot exist without a substance. This mutual dependence shows that nothing in the world can be self-caused, pointing to a necessary external cause.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Notice the logical flow from observation to conclusion.
The Arabic is concise; pay attention to key terms.
2
Understanding key terms
Identify the Arabic terms: 'ayn (entity), a'rād (accidents), qiyām (subsistence), wujūd (existence), hājah (need). Understand their roles in the argument.
3
Follow the logical chain
Trace Maturidi's reasoning: every entity undergoes accidents → accidents depend on the entity → mutual dependence → neither can be self-sufficient → the world needs an external cause.
Consider why mutual dependence prevents self-sufficiency.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the broader implication: if everything in the world is interdependent, then the world as a whole cannot be self-caused. This points to Allah as the necessary being.
This proof complements other proofs for God's existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن العالم لا يخلو كل عين منه مما يحتمله من الأعراض قهرا، وما اعترضه من الأعراض لا قيام لها ولا وجود دونه، فثبت بذلك دخول كل واحد منهما تحت حاجة الآخر. فيبطل أن يكون بنفسه، حال كونه محتاجا إلى غير به يوجد ويقوم. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Also, indeed the world: every entity in it is not free from the accidents that it necessarily undergoes, and the accidents that befall it have no subsistence or existence without it. So it is established by that that each of the two falls under the need of the other. Thus it is invalid that it be by itself while being in need of another for its existence and subsistence. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This proof examines the relationship between substances (jawahir) and accidents (a'rad). Every entity in the world is necessarily subject to accidents (such as color, motion, etc.), and accidents cannot subsist without a substance. Therefore, each depends on the other—substances require accidents for their states, and accidents require substances for existence. This mutual need shows that neither is self-sufficient. Consequently, the world as a whole cannot be self-caused; it must be by another. The proof highlights the interdependence of all created things.

Key Terms
عين
أعراض
قيام
وجود
حاجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This proof is about atomism or that substances are eternal.
Correction: The proof is not about atomism; it shows contingency through the mutual dependence of substances and accidents, proving the world needs a Creator.

Checkpoint
What does the mutual dependence of substance and accident prove?
A) That both are eternal.
B) That neither is self-sufficient.
C) That accidents are illusory.
D) That substances are the only reality.

Key Takeaway
The mutual dependence of substance and accident proves that the world is contingent and requires a Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if you are self-sufficient, forgetting your constant need for Allah in every moment? What would change if you truly internalized that even your very existence and the existence of everything around you depends on Him every second?
?
This proof shows that accidents depend on substances, but ultimately all depend on Allah. In what areas of your life do you rely on created things as if they are independent, ignoring the Creator behind them?
?
The phrase 'لا قوة إلا بالله' concludes the proof. When was the last time you felt your own powerlessness and turned to Allah with that phrase? What keeps you from living in that state of reliance?

Fifth Proof: Opposing Natures Require a Unifier
Maturidi argues that opposing natures in a single entity require an external unifier.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's proof from opposing natures and how it necessitates an external unifier.

Core Idea
Opposing natures cannot unite themselves; they need a unifier.

In this fifth proof, Imam Maturidi draws attention to a fundamental observation: within the world, we find entities composed of elements that naturally repel each other. How can such opposing natures come together in a single thing unless something external compels their union? This argument points to a necessary unifier, which is Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Let the logic sink in.
The Arabic is concise; pay attention to the phrase 'لم يجز أن يكون بنفسه يجتمع'.
2
Understand the premise
Identify the premise: opposing natures are naturally repulsive to each other. They do not come together willingly.
Think of examples: heat and cold, fire and water.
3
Recognize the conclusion
The conclusion is that such an entity cannot have come together by itself. It must have a unifier.
This is a logical necessity, not a probabilistic guess.
4
Reflect on the implication
The unifier must be external and powerful enough to overcome the repulsion. This points to Allah as the creator.
This proof is distinct from design arguments; it's based on the impossibility of self-union of opposites.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن كل عين مما اجتمع فيه الطبائع المتضادة التي من طبعها التنافر لم يجز أن يكون بنفسه يجتمع، ثبت أن له جامعا. والله الموفق.
Also, indeed every entity in which opposing natures that are by nature repulsive have come together cannot be that it came together by itself; it is established that it has a unifier. And Allah is the Grantor of success.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This proof focuses on the composition of things from opposing natures. For example, a living body combines heat and moisture with cold and dryness, which tend to repel each other. Since these natures naturally oppose each other, they cannot come together by themselves. Therefore, there must be an external unifier that brought them together. This points to a creator who combines the disparate elements. The proof is concise and relies on the observed repulsion between contrary properties.

Proof from Need for Nourishment and Wisdom
Maturidi argues that every entity needs external nourishment beyond its knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that creatures' dependence on nourishment beyond their knowledge points to an All-Knowing, Wise Creator.

Core Idea
Your need for what you do not know points to a Provider Who does.

Maturidi presents his sixth proof for a Creator by examining the universal need for sustenance. Every being requires external nourishments and other things to continue existing, yet no being fully comprehends how to acquire or produce these necessities. This mismatch between need and knowledge reveals that the world's preservation comes from an All-Knowing, Wise Source, not from itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the logical flow: need → inability to know → conclusion.
The passage is brief but dense; read carefully.
2
Identify the key premises

3
Understand the conclusion
See how Maturidi concludes that the entity is 'by an All-Knowing, Wise One, not by itself.' Reflect on why this conclusion follows from the premises.
The conclusion is not merely 'God exists' but specifies His attributes.
4
Contrast with common misunderstanding
This argument is not simply 'design implies designer.' It focuses on the creature's inherent ignorance of its own sustenance, highlighting its utter dependence and need for a wise provider.

Seventh and Eighth Proofs: Persistence and Self-Existence
Maturidi presents two logical proofs against self-causation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two rational proofs for the existence of Allah based on the impossibility of self-causation.

Core Idea
If the world were self-caused, it would be unchanging and self-existence is absurd.

Imam Maturidi presents two short but powerful arguments. The first argues from the world's changeability; the second exposes the logical impossibility of something causing itself. These proofs rely on reason and common experience.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very compressed style with two separate proofs.
2
Identify the two proofs
The passage contains two distinct arguments. Find each one and notice how each ends with a conclusion about the world being 'by another'.
The first proof uses 'لو كان بنفسه لكان يبقى به' and the second uses 'لا يخلو كونه بنفسه'.
3
Reflect on the absurdity
Focus on the second proof: why is a non-existent agent impossible? Maturidi uses analogies from building, writing, and weaving to reinforce the point.
This is a key insight: an effect requires an existing cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إنه لو كان بنفسه لكان يبقى به ويكون على حدّ واحد، فلما لم يكن دل على أنه كان بغيره. وأيضا لا يخلو كونه بنفسه: من أن كان بعد الوجود، فيبطل كونه به لما كان موجودًا بغيره؛ أو قبله، وما هو قبله كيف يوجد نفسه؟ مع ما لو كان به قبل الوجود لتوهم أن لا يوجد فيكون عديمًا فاعلاً، وذلك محال. ويشهد لما ذكرنا أمر البناء والكتابة والشفن أنه لا يجوز كونها إلا بفاعل موجود فمثله ما نحن فيه. وبالله التوفيق.
Also, if it were by itself, it would persist by itself and be upon one single limit; but since it is not, it indicates that it is by another. Also, its being by itself cannot be free from either: that it is after existence, which would invalidate its being by itself because it already existed by another; or before existence, and how can what is before existence cause itself to exist? Moreover, if it were by itself before existence, it could be imagined that it would not exist, so it would be a non-existent agent, and that is impossible. And what we have mentioned is witnessed by the matter of building, writing, and weaving: they are not possible except by an existent agent. The same applies to what we are discussing. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage combines two short proofs. The first: if the world were self-caused, it would remain in one state perpetually, but it changes, so it is not self-caused. The second: the concept of self-causation leads to absurdity. If something causes itself, it must either be after its own existence (then it already exists, so no need) or before its existence (then it is non-existent and cannot act). A non-existent agent is impossible. Maturidi analogizes to human artifacts like building, writing, and weaving, which require an existent agent. Thus, the world must have an existent creator. The proof uses logic and common experience.

Key Terms
بنفسه
حد واحد
عديم فاعل
بناء
كتابة
شفن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument is a version of the cosmological argument (contingency).
Correction: Maturidi's proof is a logical analysis of self-causation, not a general contingency argument based on contingency of the world.

Checkpoint
What is the absurd consequence of something causing itself before existence?
A) It would be eternal.
B) It would be non-existent and agent, which is impossible.
C) It would create itself perfectly.
D) It would be temporal.

Key Takeaway
The world cannot cause itself because change proves external causality and self-causation leads to logical absurdity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the world cannot cause itself and requires a Creator, why do you live as though your own efforts are the ultimate cause of your success? In which area of your life do you need to consciously acknowledge your absolute dependence on Allah?
?
The passage proves that a non-existent agent cannot act. Yet how often do you procrastinate or make excuses, acting as if you need no external help? What specific step can you take today to break that pattern and rely on the One who truly sustains you?
?
The world is constantly changing, proving it is caused. Is your faith also changing and growing, or have you become stagnant? What change must you make in your heart or actions to align with the truth that you are always in need of Allah's continuous cause (Himself)?

The Ninth Proof: Wisdom in Creation as Evidence of a Wise Creator
Maturidi argues that the incomprehensible wisdom in creation points to a wise originator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the existence of profound wisdom in creation, and our inability to fully grasp it, as a proof for a wise Creator.

Core Idea
The more we realize our ignorance of creation's wisdom, the clearer the sign of its Wise Maker.

Maturidi presents a subtle proof that does not merely point to design but highlights the very fact that even the greatest sages cannot fully comprehend the wisdom in any created thing. This inherent limitation becomes a powerful indication that the source of that wisdom must be beyond human intellect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and observe key phrases
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice the phrases 'حكمة عجيبة' (wonderful wisdom) and 'دلالة بديعة' (marvelous indication). Reflect on the repetition of 'عجز' (incapacity).
The passage is dense; let it sink in.
2
Reflect on the argument
Maturidi argues that every creature contains a wisdom so profound that even the wisest humans cannot grasp its essence or how it came to be. Their awareness of this limitation is itself evidence of a Creator whose wisdom surpasses all.
This is not a simple design argument; it's about the incomprehensibility of the wisdom.
3
Connect to the larger theology
Consider how this proof differs from merely noting order in the universe. Maturidi emphasizes that our very inability to fully understand is a sign pointing to a transcendent, wise Originator.
This aligns with the Maturidi emphasis on human limitation and divine transcendence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: وأصل ذلك أنه لا يعاين منه شيء إلا وفيه حكمة عجيبة ودلالة بديعة مما يعجز الحكماء عن إدراك مائيته وكيفية خروجه على ما خرج؛ وعلم كل أحد منهم بقصور عقله على ما عنده من الحكمة والعلم عن إدراك كنه ذلك؛ ففي هذه الضرورة وغيرها دلالة حكمة مبدعها وخالقها. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Abu Mansur (may Allah have mercy on him) said: The basis of that is that nothing is observed from it except that in it there is a wonderful wisdom and a marvelous indication, such that the wise are incapable of fathoming its quiddity and the modality of its emergence as it emerged. And each one of them knows, due to the insufficiency of his intellect despite his wisdom and knowledge, that he is unable to grasp the reality of that. So in this necessity and other things is an indication of the wisdom of its Originator and Creator. And there is no power except through Allah.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage presents a proof from the manifest wisdom in creation. Maturidi states that every observable thing in the world contains a wonderful wisdom and a clear sign, yet even the most learned sages (hukama) are incapable of fully understanding the essence or the manner in which things came to be. Their awareness of their own intellectual limitation, despite their knowledge, serves as evidence that the world must have a wise originator and creator. The very fact that we recognize our inability to fathom the complete wisdom points to a beyond-human source of that wisdom. The proof is teleological and based on wonder and humility.

عجز الحكماء
مبدع
خالق

Key Takeaway
The fact that even the wisest cannot fully grasp the wisdom in creation is itself a sign that the Creator is infinitely wise.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The lesson states that even the wisest sages cannot fully grasp the wisdom in any created thing. ?
Maturidi argues that a person's awareness of their own intellectual insufficiency is itself a sign. When was the last time you felt genuinely humbled by your lack of understanding of Allah's creation? Did that humility translate into deeper trust in Him, or did it lead to frustration? What would change in your daily life if you actively sought out such moments of intellectual humility as signs of His wisdom?
?
If every created thing contains a wisdom that even the best of human minds cannot fully reach, then your own ignorance is not a failure but a proof. Do you live as if your ignorance is a flaw to be fixed, or as a pointer to the Infinitely Wise? Starting tomorrow, when you encounter something you cannot figure out—whether in science, relationships, or your own life—will you consciously stop and say, 'This is a sign of my Creator's wisdom, not a problem to solve'? Commit now to that practice.

The Argument from Accidents and Opposing Natures
Maturidi presents two arguments from accidents and opposing natures.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two early arguments used by Maturidi to prove that the world cannot exist by itself and therefore requires a creator.

Core Idea
Accidents and opposing natures show the world's dependence.

Imam Maturidi begins his case against the self-subsistence of the world by examining its internal composition. He observes that every entity is subject to accidents and that opposing natures are found together—both of which point to an external cause. This lesson covers these two foundational arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the original Arabic passage and its English translation carefully. Notice the repeated 'وأيضا' signaling a new argument.
The first argument deals with accidents, the second with opposing natures.
2
Understand the accident argument
Maturidi argues that every entity undergoes accidents, and accidents depend on the entity. Since both need each other, neither can be self-subsisting. The entity cannot be by itself because it needs something else to exist.
This is an argument from mutual dependency.
3
Understand the opposing natures argument
Entities often combine opposite qualities (e.g., heat and cold) that naturally repel. Their coexistence must be due to an external gatherer, not the entity itself.
This is an argument from composition of opposites.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Both arguments lead to the conclusion that the world needs a creator. Think about how these arguments complement each other.
The conclusion is that the world is not self-subsisting.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن العالم لا يخلو كل عين منه مما يحتمله من الأعراض قهرا، وما اعترضه من الأعراض لا قيام لها ولا وجود دونه، فثبت بذلك دخول كل واحد منهما تحت حاجة الآخر. فيبطل أن يكون بنفسه، حال كونه محتاجا إلى غير به يوجد ويقوم. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأيضا إن كل عين مما اجتمع فيه الطبائع المتضادة التي من طبعها التنافر لم يجز أن يكون بنفسه يجتمع، ثبت أن له جامعا. والله الموفق. وأيضاً إن كل عين محتاج إلى آخر به يقوم ويبقى من الأغذية وغيرها، مما لا يحتمل أن يبلغ علمه ما به بقاؤه، أو كيف يستخرج ذلك ويكتسب؛ فثبت أنه بعليم حكيم لا بنفسه. وبالله النجاة والعصمة. وأيضا إنه لو كان بنفسه لكان يبقى به ويكون على حد واحد، فلما لم يكن دل على أنه كان بغيره. وأيضا لا يخلو كونه بنفسه: من أن كان بعد الوجود، فيبطل كونه به لما كان موجودا بغيره؛ أو قبله، وما هو قبله كيف يوجد نفسه. مع ما لو كان به قبل الوجود لتوهم أن لا يوجد فيكون عدما فاعلا، وذلك محال. ويشهد لما ذكرنا أمر البناء والكتابة والشفن أنه لا يجوز كونها إلا بفاعل موجود فمثله ما نحن فيه. وبالله التوفيق.
And also: The world—every entity in it—is not free from the accidents that it necessarily undergoes, and the accidents that befall it have no subsistence and no existence apart from it. Thus it is established that each of them falls under the need of the other. Therefore it is invalid that it be by itself, while it is in need of another by which it exists and subsists. And there is no power except with God. And also: Every entity in which opposing natures that are by their nature repellant have come together cannot be by itself in coming together; it is established that it has a gatherer. And God is the grantor of success. And also: Every entity is in need of another by which it subsists and endures from food and other things, such that it is inconceivable that its knowledge reaches what its subsistence is by, or how to extract that and acquire it. So it is established that it is by a Knowing, Wise (one), not by itself. And with God is deliverance and protection. And also: If it were by itself, it would subsist by itself and be upon a single state. Since it was not (such), it indicates that it was by another. And also: Its being by itself is not free from either being after existence—so its being by it is invalidated because it existed by another—or before it, and what is before it, how does it cause itself to exist? Considering that if it were by it before existence, it would be imagined that it does not exist, so it would be a non-existent doer, and that is impossible. And what we have mentioned is witnessed to by the matter of building, writing, and weaving: they cannot exist except by an existent agent, and similarly what we are discussing. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage presents a series of arguments demonstrating that the world cannot be self-subsisting and therefore must have a creator. The first argument notes that every physical entity necessarily undergoes accidents, and these accidents have no independent existence; the entity depends on accidents and accidents depend on the entity, so each requires the other, proving the entity cannot exist by itself. The second argument points to entities composed of opposing natures that naturally repel each other; their composition requires an external gatherer. The third argument observes that every entity needs external sustenance, which it cannot fully comprehend or acquire on its own, implying a Knowing, Wise provider. The fourth argument states that if something were self-subsisting it would remain unchanged, but since things change, they must depend on another. The fifth argument examines the logical possibilities: if something were self-subsisting after it already exists, then its existence by itself is invalid because it already existed by another; if it were self-subsisting before existence, then it would have to cause itself to exist while being non-existent, which is absurd. Finally, analogies from human artifacts (building, writing, weaving) show that such ordered products require an agent, and the world is similar. All these arguments converge to prove the world's dependence on a creator.

Key Terms
العالم
الأعراض
الطبائع المتضادة
جامع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the accident argument and opposing natures argument are redundant or the same.
Correction: They address different aspects: the accident argument focuses on dependency through accidents, while the opposing natures argument focuses on the need for an external cause to unite opposites.

Checkpoint
What does the argument from opposing natures (الطبائع المتضادة) prove?
A) The world is eternal.
B) The world has a gatherer (جامع).
C) The world is self-subsisting.
D) The world is composed of accidents.

Key Takeaway
The world's dependence on a creator is evident from its composition of accidents and opposing natures.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just read that every entity is subject to accidents and depends on something else for its existence. In what area of your life are you arrogantly pretending to be self-sufficient, and will you consciously acknowledge your dependence on God today?
?
The argument from opposing natures shows that when opposites come together, a gatherer is needed. Look at the conflicting desires within your own heart – love of this world and love of the next. Are you letting the true Gatherer order them, or are you trying to reconcile them on your own terms?
?
Maturidi ends with 'there is no power except with God.' In one specific situation this coming week, how will you remind yourself that even your ability to act is a loan from Allah, not your own?

The Argument from Need and Change
Maturidi argues from the need for sustenance and the fact of change.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's arguments from the world's need for sustenance and from its changeability.

Core Idea
Need and change prove the world is not self-subsisting.

Maturidi continues by observing that every living thing depends on food and other resources, and it cannot fully comprehend its own sustenance—pointing to a Wise Provider. Additionally, if something were self-sufficient, it would never change; but the world clearly changes, so it depends on another.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the passage containing two arguments. Notice the logical flow: need for food, then change.
2
Understand the argument from need
Every entity requires external food and other things to survive, and its knowledge cannot grasp how to obtain them. This implies a knowing and wise provider.
This argument points to the attributes of the creator.
3
Understand the argument from change
If something were self-subsisting, it would remain in one state. Since entities change, they must depend on something else.
This is a classic argument from contingency.
4
Combine both conclusions
Both arguments show that the world is not self-subsisting and requires a creator who is Knowing, Wise, and unchanging.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن العالم لا يخلو كل عين منه مما يحتمله من الأعراض قهرا، وما اعترضه من الأعراض لا قيام لها ولا وجود دونه، فثبت بذلك دخول كل واحد منهما تحت حاجة الآخر. فيبطل أن يكون بنفسه، حال كونه محتاجا إلى غير به يوجد ويقوم. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وأيضا إن كل عين مما اجتمع فيه الطبائع المتضادة التي من طبعها التنافر لم يجز أن يكون بنفسه يجتمع، ثبت أن له جامعا. والله الموفق. وأيضاً إن كل عين محتاج إلى آخر به يقوم ويبقى من الأغذية وغيرها، مما لا يحتمل أن يبلغ علمه ما به بقاؤه، أو كيف يستخرج ذلك ويكتسب؛ فثبت أنه بعليم حكيم لا بنفسه. وبالله النجاة والعصمة. وأيضا إنه لو كان بنفسه لكان يبقى به ويكون على حد واحد، فلما لم يكن دل على أنه كان بغيره. وأيضا لا يخلو كونه بنفسه: من أن كان بعد الوجود، فيبطل كونه به لما كان موجودا بغيره؛ أو قبله، وما هو قبله كيف يوجد نفسه. مع ما لو كان به قبل الوجود لتوهم أن لا يوجد فيكون عدما فاعلا، وذلك محال. ويشهد لما ذكرنا أمر البناء والكتابة والشفن أنه لا يجوز كونها إلا بفاعل موجود فمثله ما نحن فيه. وبالله التوفيق.
And also: The world—every entity in it—is not free from the accidents that it necessarily undergoes, and the accidents that befall it have no subsistence and no existence apart from it. Thus it is established that each of them falls under the need of the other. Therefore it is invalid that it be by itself, while it is in need of another by which it exists and subsists. And there is no power except with God. And also: Every entity in which opposing natures that are by their nature repellant have come together cannot be by itself in coming together; it is established that it has a gatherer. And God is the grantor of success. And also: Every entity is in need of another by which it subsists and endures from food and other things, such that it is inconceivable that its knowledge reaches what its subsistence is by, or how to extract that and acquire it. So it is established that it is by a Knowing, Wise (one), not by itself. And with God is deliverance and protection. And also: If it were by itself, it would subsist by itself and be upon a single state. Since it was not (such), it indicates that it was by another. And also: Its being by itself is not free from either being after existence—so its being by it is invalidated because it existed by another—or before it, and what is before it, how does it cause itself to exist? Considering that if it were by it before existence, it would be imagined that it does not exist, so it would be a non-existent doer, and that is impossible. And what we have mentioned is witnessed to by the matter of building, writing, and weaving: they cannot exist except by an existent agent, and similarly what we are discussing. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage presents a series of arguments demonstrating that the world cannot be self-subsisting and therefore must have a creator. The first argument notes that every physical entity necessarily undergoes accidents, and these accidents have no independent existence; the entity depends on accidents and accidents depend on the entity, so each requires the other, proving the entity cannot exist by itself. The second argument points to entities composed of opposing natures that naturally repel each other; their composition requires an external gatherer. The third argument observes that every entity needs external sustenance, which it cannot fully comprehend or acquire on its own, implying a Knowing, Wise provider. The fourth argument states that if something were self-subsisting it would remain unchanged, but since things change, they must depend on another. The fifth argument examines the logical possibilities: if something were self-subsisting after it already exists, then its existence by itself is invalid because it already existed by another; if it were self-subsisting before existence, then it would have to cause itself to exist while being non-existent, which is absurd. Finally, analogies from human artifacts (building, writing, weaving) show that such ordered products require an agent, and the world is similar. All these arguments converge to prove the world's dependence on a creator.

Key Terms
الأغذية
بعليم حكيم
حد واحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that change is just a natural process and does not imply dependence.
Correction: Maturidi argues that real, substantial change indicates that an entity is not self-sufficient; if it were, it would remain unchanged.

Checkpoint
According to the argument from change, if the world were self-subsisting, it would:
A) be in constant change
B) remain in one state
C) need food
D) be composed of accidents

Key Takeaway
The world's need for sustenance and its changeability show it is not self-sufficient and requires a creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you woke up this morning, did you assume your next breath was guaranteed? How does this lesson's claim that you cannot even know the source of your own sustenance confront your sense of self-sufficiency?
?
Consider a recent change in your life that you resisted. Can you now see that change itself is proof you are not self-sustaining? What would it mean for you to accept that you are entirely dependent on Another?
?
The passage says every entity cannot comprehend what its subsistence is by. Has your own ignorance about how your body or livelihood works ever humbled you enough to actually rely on God rather than your own plans? If not, what keeps you from that reliance?

The Logical Argument and the Analogy
Maturidi presents a logical dilemma and an analogy from crafts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's logical argument about the impossibility of self-subsistence before or after existence and the supporting analogy from human crafts.

Core Idea
Self-subsistence is impossible both before and after existence.

Maturidi presents a sophisticated logical argument that examines the very notion of something being 'by itself.' He shows that self-subsistence leads to contradictions whether considered before or after existence. He then reinforces this with a simple analogy from everyday human activities like building and writing.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the final argument carefully. It has two parts: a logical disjunction and an analogy.
2
Understand the logical disjunction
Maturidi considers two possibilities: either the world is self-subsisting after it already exists (impossible because it already depends on another) or before it exists (impossible because a non-existent cannot act).
This is a dilemma that rules out self-subsistence.
3
Understand the analogy
Just as building, writing, and weaving require an existent agent, the ordered world must have a creator.
This is a popular argument from design.
4
Reflect on the cumulative case
This argument, combined with the previous ones, provides a comprehensive proof that the world is not self-subsisting.
All arguments point to the existence of a creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage presents a series of arguments demonstrating that the world cannot be self-subsisting and therefore must have a creator. The first argument notes that every physical entity necessarily undergoes accidents, and these accidents have no independent existence; the entity depends on accidents and accidents depend on the entity, so each requires the other, proving the entity cannot exist by itself. ?

?
Maturidi uses the analogy of building, writing, and weaving to show that every product requires a maker. If your life is like a building or a book, who is its maker? Are you living in a way that acknowledges that your existence depends entirely on God, or do you sometimes feel you are the author of your own life? What one practical step can you take today to consciously remember your dependency on Him?

The Wondrous Wisdom in Creation
Maturidi argues that the world's intricate design proves a wise Maker.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the inability of the wisest humans to fully comprehend creation as a proof for the wisdom of the Creator.

Core Idea
The very things that baffle the wise point to an infinitely wiser Maker.

This passage from Maturidi presents a unique argument: not from the order of the world, but from the fact that even the most learned cannot fully grasp the essence of any created thing. That limitation itself is a sign. Let's read his words.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the rhythm and key terms.
2
Identify key terms
Focus on 'حكمة عجيبة' (wondrous wisdom), 'دلالة بديعة' (novel indication), and 'مائية' (quiddity). These carry the argument.
3
Understand the argument chain
The passage moves from observation (nothing without wisdom) to incapacity of the wise to grasp it, to concluding that this shows the Creator's wisdom.
4
Reflect on the rational nature
Consider why this is a rational argument, not just aesthetic. The wise person's awareness of their own limitation is key.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله: وأصل ذلك أنه لا يعاين منه شيء إلا وفيه حكمة عجيبة ودلالة بديعة مما يعجز الحكماء عن إدراك مائيته وكيفية خروجه على ما خرج؛ وعلم كل أحد منهم بقصور عقله على ما عنده من الحكمة والعلم عن إدراك كنه ذلك؛ ففي هذه الضرورة وغيرها دلالة حكمة مبدعها وخالقها. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: And the foundation of that is that nothing is observed from it except that in it is a wondrous wisdom and a novel indication, such that the wise are incapable of grasping its quiddity and how it emerged as it did; and each one of them knows, by the deficiency of his intellect—given what he has of wisdom and knowledge—his inability to grasp the inner reality of that. So in this necessity and others is an indication of the wisdom of its inventor and creator. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage introduces a different kind of argument: the foundation of the preceding proofs is that every observable thing in the world contains a 'wondrous wisdom' and 'novel indication' that the wisest people cannot fully comprehend—neither the essence (مائية) of a thing nor the precise manner of its coming into existence. Everyone, despite their own wisdom and knowledge, is aware of their intellect's limitation in grasping the ultimate reality of creation. This very fact—the necessity of such wondrous design and the incapacity of the wise—serves as a clear sign of the wisdom of the Maker and Creator. Thus, the passage shifts from logical arguments to an argument from the sheer marvel of creation.

Key Terms
أبو منصور
حكمة عجيبة
دلالة بديعة
الحكماء
مائية
مبدعها وخالقها

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is merely aesthetic or emotional.
Correction: It is a rational argument based on the incapacity of even the wisest to fully grasp creation.

Checkpoint
What does the passage say the wise are incapable of?
A) Observing the world.
B) Grasping the quiddity (مائية) and manner of existence of things.
C) Denying the existence of God.
D) Creating similar things.

Key Takeaway
The inability of the wisest humans to fully comprehend any created thing is itself a compelling sign of the Creator's infinite wisdom.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Can you honestly name one thing in your life—a trial, a talent, a relationship—whose inner reality you fully comprehend? How does your inability to fully grasp it reveal your need for a Creator who knows it completely?
?
The wisest people admit they cannot understand the quiddity of a single created thing. When was the last time you admitted to yourself that you don't truly understand something about God's creation? Why is that admission so hard for you?
?
If even the most knowledgeable are humbled by the complexity of creation, then what right do you have to demand full clarity from God about your own circumstances? Will you today embrace the mystery as a sign of His infinite wisdom rather than a reason for complaint?
