The Argument from Gradual Change and External Causes
Maturidi argues from gradual change and external causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that gradual changes in the world prove it depends on external causes, requiring a knowing and powerful creator.

Core Idea
Gradual change proves the world cannot be self-caused; it needs an external maker.

Maturidi presents a second argument based on the observable fact that things in the world change gradually and through external influences. If the world were self-originating, it should have the power to annihilate itself instantly, but it doesn't—instead, changes occur through external causes (أغيار). This points to a creator who is knowing and powerful.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and English translation. Note how Maturidi builds his argument.
Focus on the logical flow from hypothesis to conclusion.
2
Identify the key analogy
Maturidi uses analogies of cloth color and ship movement. Understand why these analogies support his point.
The analogies show that self-change is impossible in observable reality.
3
Analyze the logical structure
This is a reductio ad absurdum: if self-origination were possible, absurd consequences follow. Trace the logic.
The argument proceeds: if self-origination → instant annihilation possible → but we see gradual change → therefore not self-origination.
4
Connect to the conclusion
Recognize that the existence of gradual change necessitates a creator who is 'Aliim (Knowing) and Qadir (Powerful).
The final sentence explicitly states the necessity of such a creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إنه لو جاز أن يكون العالم يبدأ من قبل نفسه بمرة لجاز أن يذهب كله بمرة. فإذ لم يكن بل كان على الاختلاف، حتى لم يكن تختلف عليه الأحوال إلا بالأغيار، نحو حي يموت، ومتفرق يجتمع، وصغير يكبر، وخبيث يطيب، أبدا يتغير بأغيار تحدث. فعلى ذلك جملته لا يحتمل أن يكون لا بغيره. ولو جاز ذا لجاز أن تتغير ألوان الثوب بنفسه لا بأصباغ، أو السفينة تسير على ما هي عليه بذاتها. فإذ لم تكن ولا بد من عليم ينشئها، قدير به تكون فكذلك ما نحن فيه. وبالله التوفيق. ويبعد أيضا كون العالم بنفسه بما فيه من دلالة العلم بما هو عليه والقدرة عليه، ومحال وجود مثله بعاجز جاهل، فكيف بالمعدوم الفاني. وبالله التوفيق. ودلالة كون العالم لا من شيء هي حدوثها، وقد بينا ذلك.
And also: If it were permissible for the world to begin from itself all at once, then it would be permissible for it all to vanish all at once. But it did not (do so); rather it was in a state of variation, such that states change over it only by (external) changes (أغيار), like a living thing that dies, a scattered thing that comes together, a small thing that grows, a bad thing that becomes good—it continually changes by (external) changes that occur. Thus, in its entirety, it cannot be without another. And if this were permissible, then it would be permissible for the colors of a cloth to change by themselves without dyes, or for a ship to move by itself as it is. But they do not (do so), and there must be a Knowing (one) who brings them into being, a Powerful one by whom they are; similarly what we are discussing. And with God is success. And it is also remote that the world is by itself, given that it contains an indication of knowledge of what it is and power over it; and the existence of something like it by an incapable, ignorant is impossible—so how about by a non-existent, perishing? And with God is success. And the indication that the world is from nothing (لا من شيء) is its temporality (حدوثها), and we have already explained that.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage contains three additional arguments. First, if the world had the power to originate itself instantaneously, it could also annihilate itself instantaneously. But the world does not do so; instead, it undergoes gradual changes (life to death, dispersion to gathering, small to large, bad to good) that are caused by external factors (الأغيار). Thus the whole world cannot exist without another. An analogy: if self-change were possible, cloth colors would change without dye, and a ship would move on its own; but they do not, so a Knowing, Powerful creator is necessary. Second, the world displays marks of knowledge and power (design), so it cannot be produced by an ignorant, powerless being, let alone by a non-existent, perishing one. Third, the very fact that the world is temporally originated (حادث) indicates it came from nothing (لا من شيء), which was already explained. These arguments reinforce the conclusion that the world requires a creator who is knowing and powerful.

Key Terms
أغيار
عليم
قدير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that gradual change (like evolution) shows the world can develop on its own without a creator.
Correction: Maturidi uses gradual change to argue the opposite: because change always depends on external causes, the world itself cannot be self-subsisting.

Checkpoint
What analogy does Maturidi use to argue against the world being self-subsisting?
A) A cloth changing color by itself without dye
B) A plant growing from a seed
C) The sun rising and setting
D) A human building a house

Key Takeaway
The world's gradual change through external causes proves it cannot be self-caused and requires a knowing, powerful creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You witness your own aging, your changing moods, your shifting circumstances. Do you habitually acknowledge the Hand that changes you, or do you live as if you are self-made? What would it look like to attribute every change—small or large—to Allah’s power and wisdom?
?
If a cloth cannot change color without dye, and a ship cannot move without an external force, then your very existence and every alteration in you are not self-produced. How does this confront the pride or self-sufficiency you might feel? Will you let this realization humble you today?
?
The argument uses everyday examples to point to a Knowing, Powerful creator. What pattern in your life—perhaps a recent difficulty or blessing—most clearly reminds you that you are not self-caused? Will you pause now to thank the One who causes it, or will you let the lesson slip away?

Arguments from Design and Temporality
Maturidi presents arguments from design and temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's arguments from design (knowledge and power in the world) and from temporality (the world's originated nature) that together prove the world is created by a knowing, powerful agent.

Core Idea
The world's design and its temporal origin demand a creator with knowledge and power, not chance or nothing.

Maturidi continues with two additional proofs. First, the world displays clear marks of knowledge and power—its order and precision indicate a wise designer, not an ignorant or powerless cause. Second, the very fact that the world is temporally originated (hadith) points to its creation from nothing, a point already established. These reinforce the necessity of a creator who is 'Aliim (Knowing) and Qadir (Powerful).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice it contains two distinct arguments.
The first argument starts with 'ويبعد أيضا', the second with 'ودلالة'.
2
Analyze the design argument
Maturidi says the world contains evidence of knowledge and power (dilālah al-ʿilm wa al-qudra). Therefore, its cause must possess these attributes. An ignorant or powerless cause cannot produce such a world.
This is a classic teleological argument from order and complexity.
3
Consider the temporality argument
The world's temporality (ḥudūth) indicates it came from nothing (lā min shay'). This is a reference to a previously established proof.
This argument is concise; Maturidi assumes the reader already knows the proof of temporality.
4
Reflect on the overall conclusion
Both arguments converge on the same point: the world cannot be self-subsisting; it must have a creator who is knowing and powerful.
The phrase 'وبالله التوفيق' appears twice, marking the end of each argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إنه لو جاز أن يكون العالم يبدأ من قبل نفسه بمرة لجاز أن يذهب كله بمرة. فإذ لم يكن بل كان على الاختلاف، حتى لم يكن تختلف عليه الأحوال إلا بالأغيار، نحو حي يموت، ومتفرق يجتمع، وصغير يكبر، وخبيث يطيب، أبدا يتغير بأغيار تحدث. فعلى ذلك جملته لا يحتمل أن يكون لا بغيره. ولو جاز ذا لجاز أن تتغير ألوان الثوب بنفسه لا بأصباغ، أو السفينة تسير على ما هي عليه بذاتها. فإذ لم تكن ولا بد من عليم ينشئها، قدير به تكون فكذلك ما نحن فيه. وبالله التوفيق. ويبعد أيضا كون العالم بنفسه بما فيه من دلالة العلم بما هو عليه والقدرة عليه، ومحال وجود مثله بعاجز جاهل، فكيف بالمعدوم الفاني. وبالله التوفيق. ودلالة كون العالم لا من شيء هي حدوثها، وقد بينا ذلك.
And also: If it were permissible for the world to begin from itself all at once, then it would be permissible for it all to vanish all at once. But it did not (do so); rather it was in a state of variation, such that states change over it only by (external) changes (أغيار), like a living thing that dies, a scattered thing that comes together, a small thing that grows, a bad thing that becomes good—it continually changes by (external) changes that occur. Thus, in its entirety, it cannot be without another. And if this were permissible, then it would be permissible for the colors of a cloth to change by themselves without dyes, or for a ship to move by itself as it is. But they do not (do so), and there must be a Knowing (one) who brings them into being, a Powerful one by whom they are; similarly what we are discussing. And with God is success. And it is also remote that the world is by itself, given that it contains an indication of knowledge of what it is and power over it; and the existence of something like it by an incapable, ignorant is impossible—so how about by a non-existent, perishing? And with God is success. And the indication that the world is from nothing (لا من شيء) is its temporality (حدوثها), and we have already explained that.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This passage contains three additional arguments. First, if the world had the power to originate itself instantaneously, it could also annihilate itself instantaneously. But the world does not do so; instead, it undergoes gradual changes (life to death, dispersion to gathering, small to large, bad to good) that are caused by external factors (الأغيار). Thus the whole world cannot exist without another. An analogy: if self-change were possible, cloth colors would change without dye, and a ship would move on its own; but they do not, so a Knowing, Powerful creator is necessary. Second, the world displays marks of knowledge and power (design), so it cannot be produced by an ignorant, powerless being, let alone by a non-existent, perishing one. Third, the very fact that the world is temporally originated (حادث) indicates it came from nothing (لا من شيء), which was already explained. These arguments reinforce the conclusion that the world requires a creator who is knowing and powerful.

قدير

حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the world's complexity could arise by chance or from nothing without a creator.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the world's design requires a knowing, powerful cause, and its temporality requires a cause from nothing.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what indicates that the world came from nothing (لا من شيء)?
A) Its colors
B) Its temporality (حدوثها)
C) Its gradual change
D) Its ships

Key Takeaway
The world's design shows its creator knows and can do all, and its temporal origin proves it was created from nothing.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the intricate design of the world around you—your own body, the cosmos, the order of day and night—do you truly pause to see it as evidence of Allah's knowledge and power, or do you pass by it as ordinary? What would change in your heart if you saw every sign as a direct proof of His attributes?
?
The world's temporality means you yourself did not exist and now do. How does that truth confront the way you live—do you live as if you are self-sufficient, or do you constantly acknowledge that your very existence is a gift from nothing? What one habit can you adopt today to make this dependence real in your daily life?
?
Maturidi argues that it is impossible for the world to come from an ignorant or incapable source. Yet you sometimes attribute your own successes or failures to pure chance, your own effort, or other people. In what areas of your life are you still living as if the outcomes are not entirely in Allah's hands, and how can you repent from that subtle shirk of independence?

Maturidi's Argument for a Creator
Maturidi argues that the world cannot be self-caused, using analogies of cloth and ship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational argument that the world cannot exist without a creator, based on analogies from everyday experience.

Core Idea
The world is like a cloth needing a dyer; it cannot exist without a creator.

In this passage, Imam Maturidi presents a concise rational argument for the necessity of a creator. He uses simple analogies from everyday experience to show that the composite world cannot be self-sufficient. Read on to see how he draws a clear conclusion about the existence of Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its literal English translation. Pay attention to the flow of the argument and the key phrases.
Notice the conditional 'لو جاز' which sets up a hypothetical.
2
Identify the main claim
What is Maturidi's central assertion? Find the sentence that states the world cannot be without something else.
3
Examine the analogies
Consider the two analogies: cloth colors changing without dye, and a ship sailing by itself. Why does Maturidi choose these examples?
They are everyday examples of things that clearly require an external cause.
4
Understand the logical inference
Maturidi argues that since these analogies are impossible in reality, the world too must depend on a creator. Follow how he connects the analogies to the conclusion.
The phrase 'فإذ لم تكن' (since that is not the case) introduces the inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذلك جملته لا يحتمل أن يكون لا بغيره. ولو جاز ذا الجاز أن تتغير ألوان الثوب بنفسه لا بأصباغ، أو السفينة تسير على ما هي عليه بذاتها. فإذ لم تكن ولا بد من عليم ينشئها، قدير به تكون فكذلك ما نحن فيه. وبالله التوفيق.
That, its entirety, cannot be without something else. And if it were permissible that the colors of a garment change by themselves without dyes, or that a ship sails as it is by itself, then since that is not the case, there must be a knowing creator who brings it into existence, a powerful one through whom it exists. So likewise what we are discussing. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a brief argument for the necessity of a creator. Maturidi begins by stating that the composite totality of the world cannot exist independently. He then offers two analogies: if it were possible for cloth colors to change without an external dye, or for a ship to sail by itself, then perhaps the world might be self-sufficient. But since such things are not observed, it follows that the world requires a knowing and powerful creator. The conclusion is that the same reasoning applies to the current discussion. The passage ends with a formulaic invocation.

Key Terms
عليم
قدير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the analogies are proofs of atheism or that they imply the world is like a cloth or ship in all respects.
Correction: The analogies are not literal descriptions; they are tools to show that self-causation is empirically impossible. Their purpose is to demonstrate the logical need for an external cause.

Checkpoint

A) Colors would change without dye
B) Ships would need oars
C) Garments would fade on their own
D) The sea would be calm

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's analogies demonstrate that the order and change in the world require a knowing, powerful creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you look at the order in your own life—your health, your relationships, your provision—do you instinctively attribute it to your own efforts or to a Knowing, Powerful Creator? Which of these attributions gives you more humility and gratitude?
?
Maturidi uses a simple cloth and ship to argue. What is one object or event in your life that you take for granted as 'just happening'? How would you use it as evidence for a Creator starting today?

The World's Attributes Require a Knowing, Powerful Creator
Maturidi argues that the world's signs require a knowing, powerful creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the world's order and power cannot originate from an ignorant or nonexistent being, thereby necessitating a knowing and powerful Creator.

Core Idea
The world's order cannot emerge from ignorance or nothingness.

Maturidi continues his proof for a Creator by examining the world itself. He points to the intricate signs of knowledge and power within creation, arguing that such a world cannot be the product of an ignorant or incapable being. In fact, even a mere incapable being cannot produce this world, so how could nothingness? This passage builds on the earlier proof of temporal origination (hadath).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation to grasp Maturidi's concise argument.
2
Identify the main argument
Note that Maturidi first states the world contains signs of knowledge and power. Then he argues it is impossible for such a world to come from an incapable ignorant being.
This is an a fortiori (all the more) argument.
3
Understand the a fortiori reasoning
If an incapable ignorant being cannot produce the world, how could something nonexistent and perishable produce it? This strengthens the case for a Creator.
4
Connect to temporal origination
Maturidi links this argument to the proof from hadath (temporal origination), which shows the world came into existence from nothing.
5
Reflect on the implication
Ask yourself: What attributes must the Creator have? The world's order implies knowledge, and its control implies power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويبعد أيضاً كون العالم بنفسه بما فيه من دلالة العلم بما هو عليه والقدرة عليه، ومحال وجود مثله بعاجز جاهل، فكيف بالمعدوم الفاني. وبالله التوفيق. ودلالة كون العالم لا من شيء هي حدوثها، وقد بينا ذلك.
And it is also unlikely that the world by itself [contains] what in it indicates the knowledge of its state and the power over it, and it is impossible for the like of it to exist from an incapable ignorant [being], so how from the nonexistent perishable? And with God is success. And the indication that the world is from nothing is its temporal origination, and we have explained that.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a continuation of the argument for a creator. Maturidi presents a second proof: the world itself displays signs of precise knowledge (scientific order) and power (control). Such a world cannot have come from an incapable or ignorant source. He then argues a fortiori: if it cannot come from a mere incapable being, how could it come from something that is itself nonexistent and perishable? Finally, he references the earlier proof that the world's temporality (hadath) indicates creation ex nihilo.

Key Terms

القدرة
عاجز جاهل
حدوث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'معدوم فاني' refers to matter or pre-existing substance.
Correction: Maturidi uses 'nonexistent perishable' to emphasize absolute nothingness, not pre-existing matter. The world's origin is ex nihilo, as shown by temporal origination.

Checkpoint

B) Because the world is finite
C) Because the world is composed of elements
D) Because the world is eternal

Key Takeaway
The world's attributes of knowledge and power point to a Creator who possesses knowledge and power, and cannot be the product of ignorance or nonexistence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The world's order testifies to knowledge and power. When you observe the precision of your own body or the universe, do you ever pause to see it as a direct sign from a knowing Creator? Or do you let the routine of life make you blind to His fingerprints? What would change if, starting today, you treated every intricate detail as a personal message from the All-Knowing?
?
Maturidi argues that it is impossible for such order to come from ignorance. Yet in your own life, do you act as if your choices and their outcomes are random or unaccountable? Where specifically are you living as if your actions are not seen by a Creator who is fully aware and powerful? Commit to one area where you will realign your actions with the reality that He knows and sees.
?
The a fortiori argument shames the intellect: if an incapable being cannot produce order, how much less can nothingness? Yet we sometimes treat God as distant or limited, forgetting that He is active in every moment. When you pray or seek guidance, do you truly believe you are addressing the One whose knowledge and power fill all of existence? What would it take to approach Him with the awe and certainty that this passage demands?

The Three Evidences and the Meaning of 'One'
Maturidi introduces the three evidences and the dual meaning of 'one'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three sources of evidence for tawhid that Maturidi identifies and how the term 'one' carries both a numerical and a qualitative sense.

Core Idea
The term 'one' points to both number and greatness; only the latter applies to God.

Maturidi begins by laying out the full range of evidence for God's oneness: scripture, reason, and the testimony of creation. He then focuses on the scriptural evidence, showing that even those who believe in multiple gods still use the word 'one'—revealing that 'one' has two distinct meanings. This lesson unpacks that foundational distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the rhythm, then read the translation. Notice how Maturidi lists three sources before focusing on scripture.
2
Identify the three evidences
Locate the three sources: sam‘ (scripture), ‘aql (reason), and shahādat al-‘ālam bi-l-khalqah (the world's testimony by creation). Note that only scripture is elaborated here.
Reason and creation are taken up in later lessons.
3
Understand the two meanings of 'one'
Maturidi explains that 'one' (wāḥid) means both 'the beginning of number' and 'greatness, authority, elevation'. The example of 'one of the age' shows the second meaning.
This duality is the key to the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : والدلالة على أن محدث العالم واحد لا أكثر السمع، والعقل، وشهادة العالم بالخلقة. أ ـ فأما السمع فهو اتفاق القول - على اختلافهم - على الواحد، إذ مَنْ يقول بالأكثر يقول به. على أن الواحد اسم لابتداء العدد، واسم للعظمة والسلطان والرفعة والفضل، كما يُقال: فلان واحد الزمان ومنقطع القرين في الرفعة والفضل والجلال. وما جاوز ذلك لا يحتمل غير العدد، والأعداد لانهاية لها من حيث العدد؛ وفي تحقيق ما يُعدّ يخرج عن النهاية بالعدد، فيجب أن يكون العالم غير متناه؛ إذ لو كان من كل منها شيء واحد، ليخرج الجملة عن التناهي بخروج المحدثين، وذلك بعيد. ثم ما من عدد يشار إليه إلا وأمكن من الدعوى أن يزاد عليه أو ينقص منه، فلم يجب القول بشيء ـ لما لا حقيقة لذلك بحق العدد - لا يُشارك فيه غيره؛ لذلك بطل القول به. وبالله التوفيق.
Abu Mansur said: The evidence that the creator of the world is one, not more, is scripture, reason, and the testimony of the world by creation. As for scripture, it is the agreement of speech — despite their differences — upon the one, for whoever says 'more' says it with the one. Indeed, 'one' is a name for the beginning of number, and a name for greatness, authority, elevation and excellence, just as it is said: So-and-so is the one of the age and the peerless in elevation, excellence and majesty. And what goes beyond that admits nothing except number, and numbers have no end in terms of number; and in the realization of what is counted, it exceeds finitude by number, so the world must be unlimited; for if from each of them there were one thing, the totality would go out of finitude by the going out of the originated things, and that is unlikely. Then, there is no number pointed to except that it is possible to claim increase or decrease from it, so one should not affirm anything — since there is no reality for that by the right of number — that is not shared by another; therefore the assertion of it is invalid. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument for the oneness of the creator. Maturidi first states the three sources of evidence: scripture (sam'), reason ('aql), and the testimony of the world. He then elaborates on the scriptural argument. He notes that all groups agree on the term 'one', but 'one' has two senses: numerical commencement and greatness. If 'one' is taken numerically, then positing multiple creators leads to an infinite regress because numbers are unlimited—if each creator were a distinct number, the world would be infinite. Moreover, any specific number can be increased or decreased arbitrarily, so no particular number of gods can be rationally preferred. Thus, belief in multiple creators is baseless. The argument concludes that the only valid concept of 'one' in divinity is the non-numerical sense of uniqueness.

Key Terms
السمع
العقل
الواحد
اتفاق القول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the term 'one' (wāḥid) signify besides the beginning of number?
A) Unity of essence
B) Greatness, authority, elevation
C) Simplicity
D) Eternity

Key Takeaway
The term 'one' has two distinct meanings: numerical and qualitative; only the qualitative sense applies to God.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Allah is one', do you reduce Him to a number in your mind, or do you truly feel His absolute uniqueness and unmatched greatness? How does your understanding affect your reliance on Him alone?
?
Which of the three evidences—scripture, reason, or creation—do you personally find weakest in your own faith? What step will you take this week to strengthen that evidence in your heart?
?
If someone told you that believing in one God is just a matter of counting, how would you respond using the distinction from this passage? Are you ready to defend the deeper meaning of tawḥīd in your conversations?

The Numerical Argument Against Multiple Gods
Maturidi uses numerical reasoning to prove the creator must be one.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the numerical sense of 'one' to show that belief in multiple creators leads to logical absurdities involving infinite numbers.

Core Idea
If 'one' meant only number, any number of gods could be claimed, leading to an absurd infinite world.

Building on the duality of the word 'one', Maturidi now examines what happens if we take 'one' in its numerical sense. He shows that numbers are unlimited, so if each creator were a distinct number, the world would have to be infinite — an impossible consequence. Furthermore, any specific number can be arbitrarily increased or decreased, so no specific number of gods can be rationally maintained. This powerful argument refutes polytheism from within the concept of number itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the second part of the passage
Read the Arabic from 'وما جاوز ذلك' to the end, then read the translation. Focus on the logical steps.
2
Follow the absurdity argument
Maturidi argues: if 'one' is purely numerical, then numbers go to infinity; if each creator corresponds to a number, the world would be infinite. Since the world is finite, multiple creators cannot be numerical.
The phrase 'يخرج عن النهاية بالعدد' is key.
3
Understand the arbitrariness objection
Even if we ignore infinity, any proposed number of gods can be increased or decreased without a rational basis, so no number is privileged.
This refutes any specific number of gods.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : والدلالة على أن محدث العالم واحد لا أكثر السمع، والعقل، وشهادة العالم بالخلقة. أ ـ فأما السمع فهو اتفاق القول - على اختلافهم - على الواحد، إذ مَنْ يقول بالأكثر يقول به. على أن الواحد اسم لابتداء العدد، واسم للعظمة والسلطان والرفعة والفضل، كما يُقال: فلان واحد الزمان ومنقطع القرين في الرفعة والفضل والجلال. وما جاوز ذلك لا يحتمل غير العدد، والأعداد لانهاية لها من حيث العدد؛ وفي تحقيق ما يُعدّ يخرج عن النهاية بالعدد، فيجب أن يكون العالم غير متناه؛ إذ لو كان من كل منها شيء واحد، ليخرج الجملة عن التناهي بخروج المحدثين، وذلك بعيد. ثم ما من عدد يشار إليه إلا وأمكن من الدعوى أن يزاد عليه أو ينقص منه، فلم يجب القول بشيء ـ لما لا حقيقة لذلك بحق العدد - لا يُشارك فيه غيره؛ لذلك بطل القول به. وبالله التوفيق.
Abu Mansur said: The evidence that the creator of the world is one, not more, is scripture, reason, and the testimony of the world by creation. As for scripture, it is the agreement of speech — despite their differences — upon the one, for whoever says 'more' says it with the one. Indeed, 'one' is a name for the beginning of number, and a name for greatness, authority, elevation and excellence, just as it is said: So-and-so is the one of the age and the peerless in elevation, excellence and majesty. And what goes beyond that admits nothing except number, and numbers have no end in terms of number; and in the realization of what is counted, it exceeds finitude by number, so the world must be unlimited; for if from each of them there were one thing, the totality would go out of finitude by the going out of the originated things, and that is unlikely. Then, there is no number pointed to except that it is possible to claim increase or decrease from it, so one should not affirm anything — since there is no reality for that by the right of number — that is not shared by another; therefore the assertion of it is invalid. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a multi-part argument for the oneness of the creator. Maturidi first states the three sources of evidence: scripture (sam'), reason ('aql), and the testimony of the world. He then elaborates on the scriptural argument. He notes that all groups agree on the term 'one', but 'one' has two senses: numerical commencement and greatness. If 'one' is taken numerically, then positing multiple creators leads to an infinite regress because numbers are unlimited—if each creator were a distinct number, the world would be infinite. Moreover, any specific number can be increased or decreased arbitrarily, so no particular number of gods can be rationally preferred. Thus, belief in multiple creators is baseless. النهاية
الزيادة
النقصان

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidi rejects the numerical sense of 'one' for God entirely.
Correction: He does not reject it; he uses it to show that if applied to God, it leads to absurdities, so God's oneness must be understood in the qualitative sense.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi claim would happen if the numerical sense of 'one' were applied to the creator?

?

?

Prophetic Signs as Proof of Tawhid
Maturidi argues that no rival god has produced evidence, and prophets' miracles prove one true God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the success of the prophets' miracles as empirical evidence for the oneness of God.

Core Idea
No rival god ever stopped a prophet's miracle, proving there is only one true God.

Maturidi presents a powerful evidence for tawhid that is based on observable history: the prophets came with undeniable signs, yet no rival deity ever prevented them. This lesson examines his argument that the absence of such prevention proves the singularity of the true God.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the literal translation. Notice Maturidi's use of 'falam yu'jadu' (it was not found) and 'fathabata' (so it is established).
The passage is a continuous argument; try to follow its logical flow.
2
Identify the two main premises
First, Maturidi states that no claimed god has presented evidence. Second, he argues that if there were another god, it would have prevented the prophets' signs. Find these two parts.
These premises lead to the conclusion that the prophets' success proves oneness.
3
Reflect on the reasoning
Why does the absence of prevention prove oneness? Think about what a rival god would want to do if it existed. The commentary explains this step.
This is a historical argument, not a purely rational one.
4
Test your understanding with the checkpoint
Answer the multiple-choice question to see if you grasped the key inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإنه لم يُذكر عن غير الإله الذي يعرفه أهل التوحيد دعوى الإلهية والأشارة إلى أثر فعل منه يدل على ربوبيته، ولا وجد في شيء معنى أمكن إخراجه عن جملته، ولا بَعَثَ رُسُلاً بالآيات التي تقهر العقول وتبهر لها، فثبت أن القول بذلك خيال ووسواس. وأيضاً مجيء الرسل بالآيات التي يُضطر من شهدها أنها فعل من لو كان معه شريك ليمنعهم عن إظهارها، إذ بذلك إبطال ربوبيتهم وألوهيتهم. فإذ لم يوجد ولا منعوا عن ذلك، مع كثرة المكابرين لهم والمعاندين ممن لو أحبوا وجود الأنصار لهم في إظهار آياتهم لوجدوا، فثبت أن ذلك إنما سَلِم للرسل؛ لما لم يكن الإله الحق والخالق للخلق غير الواحد القهار الذي قهر كل متعنّت مكابر عن التمويه فضلا عن التحقيق ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And moreover, no claim of divinity has been reported from anyone other than the God known to the people of tawhid, nor an indication of an effect of an act of His indicating His lordship, nor was there found in anything a meaning that could be removed from its totality, nor did they send messengers with signs that overwhelm intellects and dazzle them. So it is established that the assertion of that is fantasy and delusion. And also, the coming of the messengers with signs that compel whoever witnesses them [to admit] that they are the act of one such that if He had a partner, they would have prevented them from manifesting them, for by that their lordship and divinity would be nullified. Since no prevention occurred, despite the abundance of their obstinate opponents and adversaries, some of whom would have loved to find supporters for manifesting their signs, it is established that that [success] was secured for the messengers only because the true God and Creator of creation is none other than the One, the Subduer, who subdued every stubborn obstinate one from false claim, let alone verification. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is an empirical and historical proof for tawhid. Maturidi argues that no rival deity has ever produced a genuine claim or miracle. The prophets, however, brought undeniable signs. If there had been another god, that god would have prevented the prophets from displaying such signs to protect its own lordship. Since no prevention occurred, despite many enemies who would have liked to support a rival, it proves that the prophets' signs came from the one true God alone. The passage ends by affirming God's unique power.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think this argument is circular, assuming the existence of God to prove oneness.
Correction: The argument is not circular; it uses the observable fact that prophets succeeded in performing miracles despite opposition as evidence that there is no other god who could have stopped them.

Checkpoint

D) Because a rival god would be less powerful

Key Takeaway
The absence of any divine rival interfering with the prophets' miracles is a strong historical proof that the only true God is Allah, the One and Subduer.

Rational Argument Against Multiple Gods
Maturidi gives a rational demonstration that two gods would inevitably conflict, ruining creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational argument for the oneness of God, grounded in the logical impossibility of two gods with conflicting wills leading to a consistent creation.

Core Idea
Two gods with conflicting wills make an orderly world impossible.

Maturidi presents a straightforward rational argument that the existence of a consistent, orderly world proves there can only be one God. He uses two interconnected lines of reasoning: the impossibility of divine agreement and the inevitability of conflicting wills. This lesson walks through the logic step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation. Focus on the overall flow of the argument from premise to conclusion.
Notice the two 'meanings' (arguments) Maturidi presents before the conclusion.
2
Identify the two premises
Maturidi gives two reasons why multiple gods are impossible: (1) they would need to agree, which corrupts lordship; (2) they would have conflicting wills. Underline each in the text.
The second premise includes examples of willing creation vs. annihilation.
3
Connect to the conclusion
See how the existence of a coherent, orderly world (consistent management) directly proves that the Creator is one. Reflect on why conflict would necessarily produce disorder.
This is a classic 'argument from harmony'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم دلالة العقل أنه لو كان أكثر من واحد ما احتمل وجود العالم إلا بالاصطلاح، وفي ذلك فساد الربوبية. ومعنى آخر أنّ كل شيء يُريد أحد ممن يُنسب إليه الإلهية إثباته يريد الآخر نفيه، وما يريد أحدهما إيجاده يريد الآخر إعدامه؛ وكذلك في الإبقاء والإفناء؛ وفي ذلك تناقض وتناف. فدل الوجود على أن مُحْدِث العالم واحد، فاتسق تدبيره.
Then the evidence of reason is that if there were more than one, the existence of the world would not be possible except by agreement, and in that is the corruption of lordship. And another meaning: every thing that one of those attributed with divinity wills to affirm, the other wills to negate, and what one of them wills to bring into existence, the other wills to annihilate; likewise in preserving and destroying; and in that is contradiction and opposition. So existence indicates that the creator of the world is one, so its management is consistent.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage is a concise rational proof for the oneness of God. Maturidi argues that two or more gods would require agreement to create, which would compromise their lordship. More fundamentally, if two gods had conflicting wills—one wanting to create, the other to destroy—the world could not exist in an orderly fashion. Since the world does exist with consistency, its maker must be one, and its governance is coherent. The proof hinges on the observable harmony of the universe.

Key Terms
دلالة العقل
الاصطلاح
فساد الربوبية
تناقض
تناف
تدبير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that multiple gods could simply agree to cooperate and thus avoid conflict.
Correction: Maturidi's argument shows that requiring agreement itself corrupts lordship, because a truly independent god should not need consent. Moreover, even if they agreed, their wills are independent and could diverge at any moment, making the world's consistency only accidental, not necessary.

Checkpoint

A) They would compromise
B) The world would be destroyed or not exist
C) They would share power
D) The world would be divided into domains

Key Takeaway
The observable harmony and consistency of the world rationally requires a single Creator with a coherent will.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The passage says that two gods would inevitably contradict each other. In your own life, where do you experience internal contradiction—wanting two things that cannot coexist (e.g., worldly pleasure and spiritual purity)? How does the oneness of God call you to resolve that contradiction and unify your heart's direction?
?
The consistency of creation points to one Creator. But is your own life consistent with that truth? Where have you allowed inconsistency, hypocrisy, or divided loyalty to creep in? What step will you take today to align your actions more fully with the belief that there is no god but Allah?

The Numerical Argument Against Multiple Gods
Maturidi refutes polytheism by showing the impossibility of a definite number of gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's numerical argument against polytheism and why any specific number of gods is rationally arbitrary.

Core Idea
No specific number of gods can be rationally justified.

Imam Maturidi presents a concise rational argument against the possibility of multiple gods. He argues that if there were multiple gods, there would have to be a specific number, but any such number can be arbitrarily increased or decreased, making the claim groundless. Let's examine his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the two parts of the argument
First, Maturidi dismisses the idea that the number of gods could be infinite due to new creations. Second, he focuses on any finite number, showing it can be arbitrarily increased or decreased.
3
Understand why arbitrariness invalidates the claim
If no specific number is rationally necessary, then the claim that there are exactly N gods is baseless. The arbitrariness shows the claim has no reality.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument concludes that the statement of multiple gods is invalid. Ask yourself: does this argument prove that only one God exists? How?
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وليخرج الجملة عن التناهي بخروج المحدثين، وذلك بعيد. ثم ما من عدد يشار إليه إلا وأمكن من الدعوى أن يزاد عليه أو ينقص منه، فلم يجب القول بشيء ـ لما لا حقيقة لذلك بحق العدد - لا يشارك فيه غيره؛ لذلك بطل القول به. وبالله التوفيق.
And that the total (of gods) should go beyond infinity due to the emergence of new things is far-fetched. Then, there is no number that can be pointed to except that it is possible in claim to be increased or decreased; so it is not necessary to assert anything — since there is no reality to that with respect to the true number — that is not shared by another; therefore the statement (of multiple gods) is invalid. And with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage presents a rational argument against polytheism. The author begins by stating that the idea that the total number of gods could extend to infinity due to the emergence of new created things is unlikely. Then he argues that for any specific number that one might claim for the gods, it is always possible in imagination to add or subtract from that number. Because there is no intrinsic reason to pick one number over another, the claim is arbitrary and thus invalid. Therefore, the belief in multiple gods is false.

Key Terms
تناهي
عدد
دعوى

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about the impossibility of an infinite number of gods.
Correction: The argument actually focuses on the arbitrariness of any finite number. The infinite case is only briefly dismissed as far-fetched.

Checkpoint

A) That an infinite number of gods is impossible
B) That any specific number of gods can be arbitrarily increased or decreased, so no specific number is valid
C) That there is only one god because numbers are finite
D) That the number of gods must be odd

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's numerical argument shows that any claimed number of gods is rationally arbitrary, thus only the belief in one God is logically sound.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

The Royal Analogy and Scriptural Proofs for Oneness
Maturidi uses an analogy and Quranic verses to prove God's oneness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the behavior of earthly kings and Quranic verses to establish that Allah alone is divine.

Core Idea
If a rival god existed, he would have shown his power—but he didn't, proving oneness.

In this passage, Maturidi combines an analogy from human kings with several Quranic verses to prove that there can be only one God. He observes that earthly kings exert great effort to subdue rivals and secure sole sovereignty. By applying this principle to the divine realm, he argues that if another god existed, he would have attempted to assert his authority. Since no such attempt is observed, the sole sovereignty of Allah is established. The Quranic verses are presented as confirmations of this rational conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Notice the sequence: the king analogy, then three Quranic arguments.
The passage is dense; reading twice helps.
2
Understand the royal analogy
Maturidi describes how earthly kings strive to subdue peers to become the sole ruler. This sets up the expectation that any rival would act similarly.
This is a rational argument from observed human behavior.
3
Analyze the Quranic verses
The passage cites four verses: al-Isra 17:42, al-Anbiya 21:22, al-Mu'minun 23:91, and al-Ra'd 13:16. Each verse reinforces the impossibility of multiple gods.
These verses are scriptural confirmations of the rational argument.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Since no rival god ever manifested his power, it becomes clear that Allah is unique in divinity and exclusive in lordship.
The conclusion is both rational and scriptural.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مع ما كان الأمر المعتاد بين الملوك بذل الوسع منهم في قهر أشكالهم ليكون الملك للقاهر، ومنع كل منهم غيره عن إنفاذ حكمه وإظهار سلطانه ما استطاع. فإذ لم يكن، بل نفذ سلطان العزيز الحكيم، ثبت أنه الواحد وهذا تأويل قوله: {قل لو كان معه آلهة كما يقولون إذا لا بتغوا إلى ذي العرش سبيلا} [الإسراء، 42/17]؛ والأول على ما أودع قوله: {لو كان فيهما آلهة إلا الله لفسدتان} [الأنبياء، 22/21]. وأيضا إنه لو كان مع الله إله لأظهر الآخر حكمته وفصل فعله من فعل الله الحق ليعلن به قدرته وسلطانه، فإذ لم يفعل بان أن الله هو المتوحد بالإلهية والمتفرد بالربوبية؛ وذلك معنى قوله: {وما كان معه من إله إذًا لذهب كل إله بما خلق} [المؤمنون، 91/23]، وكذلك قوله: {أم جعلوا لله شركاء خلقوا كخلقه} [الرعد، 16/13] الآية.
Along with what is customary among kings — they exert their effort to subdue their peers so that sovereignty belongs to the subduer, and each prevents the other from executing his judgment and manifesting his authority as much as he can. Since that did not happen (with God), but rather the authority of the Mighty, the Wise was executed, it is established that He is the One, and this is the interpretation of His words: 'Say: If there were with Him gods as they say, then they would surely seek a way to the Owner of the Throne' [al-Isra 17:42]. And the first (argument) is based on His words: 'If there were in them (the heavens and the earth) gods besides Allah, they would both be corrupted' [al-Anbiya 21:22]. Also, if there were a god with Allah, the other would manifest his wisdom and distinguish his action from the action of Allah the True to make known his power and authority. Since he did not do so, it became clear that Allah is unique in divinity and exclusive in lordship. And that is the meaning of His words: 'And there is no god with Him; otherwise each god would have taken away what he created' [al-Mu'minun 23:91], and likewise His words: 'Or have they ascribed to Allah partners who created the like of His creation?' [al-Ra'd 13:16] — the verse.
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage combines an analogy from human kings with Quranic verses. Maturidi notes that earthly kings strive to subdue rivals to secure sole sovereignty. If there were another god, he would have acted to assert his authority, but since no such action occurred, the sole sovereignty of Allah is proven. He then cites several Quranic verses that support oneness, including the impossibility of multiple gods in the heavens and earth, and the absurdity of each god taking his creation separately. The verses are presented as confirmations of the rational arguments.

Correction: Maturidi is drawing an analogy about how rivals behave, not equating God's essence with human rulers.

The Argument from Power: Proving Tawhid Through Logical Contradiction
Maturidi's logical argument from power against multiple gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational proof that the existence of two gods leads to logical contradictions in the concepts of power and knowledge, thereby proving the necessity of one God.

Core Idea
If two gods existed, neither could have absolute power—contradicting the very definition of divinity.

Maturidi now deploys a powerful logical argument to demonstrate the impossibility of multiple gods. By examining what would happen if two gods possessed power relative to each other—whether they can hide actions from each other or prevent each other's intentions—he shows that every scenario except one leads to an imperfection incompatible with divinity. This argument further solidifies the core Islamic belief in Tawhid.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Notice that Maturidi uses two parallel scenarios: the ability to conceal actions and the ability to prevent actions.
The word 'يُسِرّ' means to hide or conceal.
2
Follow the first scenario: hiding actions from one another
Maturidi presents three possibilities regarding whether one god can hide his actions from the other. Trace each possibility and its consequence: both able, both unable, one able.
If both can hide, each makes the other ignorant—knowledge deficiency removes lordship.
3
Follow the second scenario: preventing each other's actions
Now Maturidi examines the ability to prevent the other's intended actions. Again, he presents possibilities: both able or only one able.
If both can prevent, each can potentially be overpowered—impotence contradicts divinity.
4
Reflect on the overall logical proof
In all scenarios except one (a single God with exclusive power), multiple gods lead to a contradiction or imperfection. This logical elimination proves tawhid.
This argument is known as 'burhan al-tamani' (proof of mutual hindrance) in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم وجه الاصطلاح يدل على العجز والجهل، مع ما لو كان كذلك لم يكن الأمر لواحد في نسبة الخلق وتحقيقه له إلا من حيث يقهرهم ويدخلهم تحت قدرته وصنعه. والله الموفق. وأيضا إنه لو كان مع الله إله لا يخلو من أن يقدر على فعل يسرّه من الآخر أو لا، وكذلك الله سبحانه منه. فإن قدرا جميعا ملك كل واحد منهما تجهيل الآخر، وفي ذلك زوال الربوبية. وإن لم يقدرا عجز كل واحد منهما، والعجز يسقط الألوهية؛ أو قدر أحدهما دون الآخر فهو الرب والآخر مربوب مع ما كان علم الغيب علم الربوبية، فمن ليس له فهو مربوب. ثم لا يخلو أيضا من قدرة كل واحد منهما على غيره في منع ما يروم الفعل بغيره ويريده أو لا؛ فيكون فيهما إمكان خروج كل عن القدرة وتحقيق عجز، وذلك يسقط الربوبية؛ أو يقدر الواحد خاصة فيكون هو الرب سبحانه.
Then, the aspect of mutual agreement indicates impotence and ignorance, along with the fact that if it were so, the matter would not be for one in attributing creation and its realization to him except by way of subduing them and bringing them under his power and making. And Allah is the Grantor of success. Also, if there were a god with Allah, it is unavoidable that he is either able to hide an action from the other or not, and likewise Allah, exalted, with respect to him. If both are able, then each one has the power to make the other ignorant, and in that is the removal of lordship. If neither is able, then each is impotent, and impotence nullifies divinity. Or if one is able and the other is not, then the able one is the Lord and the other is a subject, especially since knowledge of the unseen is the knowledge of lordship, so whoever does not possess it is a subject. Then also, it is unavoidable that either each has power over the other to prevent what the other intends to do or not; so in them is the possibility of each coming out of power and the realization of impotence, and that nullifies lordship; or one has power specifically, so he is the Lord, glorified be He.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed logical analysis of the relationship between two hypothetical gods. Maturidi systematically examines all possibilities regarding their power over each other's actions. If both can conceal actions from each other, that implies deficiency in knowledge. If both are unable, they are weak. If one is able and the other not, the able one is truly divine. Similarly, regarding the ability to prevent each other's actions, either both or only one has that power. In all cases except the last (one having exclusive power), the concept of multiple gods leads to contradiction or imperfection, thus proving only the One True God exists.

Key Terms
قدرة
عجز
ألوهية
ربوبية
علم الغيب
تجهيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Two gods could coexist if they never interfere with each other and remain in perfect harmony.
Correction: Maturidi's argument shows that non-interference would require either mutual ignorance (if actions are hidden) or impotence (if actions can be prevented). Both are deficiencies, so coexistence is impossible.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, if two gods both have the power to prevent each other's actions, what is the logical consequence?
A) They are both still divine because they still have power.
B) Each can potentially be overcome, leading to impotence, which negates divinity.
C) They must cooperate to maintain divinity.
D) One will inevitably dominate the other.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's argument from power logically proves that the existence of more than one God leads to contradictions in the concepts of power and knowledge, thus affirming the oneness of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
After understanding that only Allah possesses absolute power and knowledge, in what areas of your life do you still act as if you have ultimate control? Can you identify a specific situation where you need to surrender that illusion of control to Allah today?
?
If the logical impossibility of multiple gods is so clear, why do you still find yourself relying on created beings as if they have independent power? What is one practical step you can take to transfer that reliance to Allah alone?
?
Maturidi's argument shows that any deficiency in power or knowledge negates divinity. Reflect on your understanding of Allah's attributes. Is there any doubt in your heart about Allah's absolute power over your circumstances? How can you strengthen that certainty today?

The Argument from the Singular Order of Creation
Maturidi presents the argument from the singular order of creation as proof of tawhid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the consistent, unified patterns in creation provide rational proof for the oneness of God (tawhid) and why multiple gods would contradict this observed order.

Core Idea
A single, coherent order in creation demands a single Creator.

Maturidi presents an argument rooted in everyday observation: the natural world displays a singular, harmonious system. From the changing seasons to the movements of the sun, moon, and stars, everything follows one consistent pattern. He argues that such unity of administration would be impossible if there were multiple gods, thus affirming tawhid. This lesson walks through his examples and logical conclusion.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Pay attention to the examples Maturidi lists and the logical structure he builds.
Note that the text cuts off at the end; we focus on the complete argument that is present.
2
Identify the examples of consistency
List the phenomena Maturidi mentions that show a single pattern: seasons, crops, sky and earth, sun/moon/stars, sustenance of creatures, and animal livelihoods.
These are all different domains, yet they follow one coordinated system.
3
Understand the logical argument
The argument is: If there were more than one god, administration would fluctuate (disagree/conflict). But we see all these phenomena proceeding on a single course (مسلك واحد) and a single pattern (سنن واحد). Therefore, it cannot be from multiple administrators—so there is only one God.
This is a classic argument from the unity of design (not just design).
4
Learn the key terms
The three technical terms are: استدلال بالخلق (inference from creation), تدبير (administration/management), مسلك واحد (single course), سنن واحد (single pattern). Understanding these is essential for later kalam discussions.
مسلك and سنن both emphasize unity and consistency.
5
Reflect on the common misunderstanding
Some might think this is merely an argument from design (complexity). But Maturidi's point is more precise: it is the unity and non-contradiction of the system that proves one Creator, not just that the world appears designed.
Multiple designers could produce complexity, but they would inevitably conflict in their plans.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جـ ـ وأما دلالة الاستدلال بالخلق فهو أنه لو كان أكثر من واحد لتقلب فيهم التدبير، نحو تحول الأزمنة من نحو الشتاء والصيف، أو تحول خروج الأنزال وينعها، أو تقدير السماء والأرض، أو تسيير الشمس والقمر والنجوم، أو أغذية الخلق، أو تدبير معاش جواهر الحيوان. فإذا دار كله على مسلك واحد ونوع من التدبير وانساق ذلك على سنن واحد فقد ثبت أنه لا يتم بمدبرين؛ لذلك لزم القول بالواحد. وبالله التوفيق. والثاني أن الأجناس على اختلافها وتباعد ما بينها من نحو السماء والأرض، وأطراف الأرض، وبعد أرزاق أهلها جعلت متصلة بالمنافع، حتى كان كل أنواع الخارج من الأرض...
C. As for the proof by way of inference from creation, it is that if there were more than one, then the administration would fluctuate among them, like the transformation of seasons from winter to summer, or the transformation of the emergence of crops and their ripening, or the measurement of the sky and the earth, or the movement of the sun, moon, and stars, or the sustenance of creatures, or the management of the livelihoods of animal essences. So when all of that proceeds on a single course and a single kind of administration and flows on a single pattern, it is established that it cannot be accomplished by two administrators; therefore it is necessary to affirm the One. And with Allah is success. The second (point) is that the species, despite their differences and the distances between them — like the sky and the earth, the ends of the earth, and the far distances of their inhabitants' sustenance — are made connected by benefits, until every type of what comes out of the earth...
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage uses the consistent order in nature as a proof of tawhid. Maturidi lists several phenomena: the changing seasons, the growth and ripening of plants, the measurement of heavens and earth, the movement of celestial bodies, and the sustenance of all creatures. All these follow a single, coherent pattern. If there were multiple gods, the administration would be inconsistent. The fact that the entire system operates on one unified plan proves there is only one Creator. He then begins a second point about the interconnectedness of diverse species for mutual benefit, but the passage ends abruptly.

Key Terms
استدلال بالخلق
تدبير
مسلك واحد
سنن واحد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument from the singular order of creation is just another version of the argument from design, focusing on complexity.
Correction: Maturidi's argument is specifically about the unity and non-contradiction of the natural order, not merely that it appears designed. Multiple designers could produce complexity, but they would inevitably produce inconsistencies. The observed harmony points to a single will.

Key Takeaway

Proof from the Inability of a Second God to Manifest His Divinity
Maturidi presents the first proof for tawhid from the impossibility of a second god manifesting his divinity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's rational and scriptural argument that a second god would necessarily manifest his divinity, and his failure to do so proves God's oneness.

Core Idea
A second god would have to show his power—but he didn't, so only the One exists.

Imam Maturidi begins his case for tawhid by combining Quranic testimony with a rational principle. The verses warn of cosmic chaos if multiple gods existed, but he goes further: a rival god would have to distinguish himself by separate acts. Because no such act occurred, the unique divinity of Allah is evident. This passage shows how Kalam thinkers weave reason and revelation into a single fabric.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
First, read the Arabic text carefully, then its literal translation. Let Maturidi's own words set the stage before you analyze.
Note the Quranic verses embedded in the passage.
2
Identify the Quranic citations
Find the three Quranic verses Maturidi uses. What do they argue about multiple gods?
Verses are from al-Isra, al-Anbiya, al-Mu'minun, and al-Ra'd.
3
Trace the rational argument
Follow Maturidi's logic: if a second god existed, he would have to manifest his wisdom and separate his actions. Since he did not, God alone is divine.
This is the core rational proof.
4
Understand 'istilah' (convention)
Maturidi argues that the very concept of convention implies incapacity and ignorance. Consider how this reinforces the proof.
This nuance shows the depth of kalam reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سلطان العزيز الحكيم، ثبت أنه الواحد وهذا تأويل قوله : قل لو كان معه آلهة كما يقولون إذا لا بتغوا إلى ذي العرش سبيلا [الإسراء، ٤٢/١٧]؛ والأول على ما أودع قوله: لو كان فيهما آلهة إلا الله لفسدتان [الأنبياء، ٢٢/٢١]. وأيضاً إنه لو كان مع الله إله لأظهر الآخر حكمته و فصل فعله من فعل الله الحق ليُعلَم به قدرته وسلطانه، فإذ لم يفعل بَانَ أنّ الله هو المتوحد بالإلهية والمتفرد بالربوبية؛ وذلك معنى قوله : وما كان معه من إله إذًا لذهب كل إله بما خلق ﴾ [المؤمنون، ۹۱/۲۳]، وكذلك قوله: وأم جعلوا الله " شركاء خلقوا كخلقه ﴾ [الرعد، ١٦/١٣]، الآية. ثم وجه الاصطلاح يدل على العجز والجهل، مع ما لو كان كذلك لم يكن الأمر لواحد في نسبة الخلق وتحقيقه له إلا من حيث يَقْهَرَهُم ويُدخلهم تحت قدرته وصنعه. والله الموفق.
The authority of the Mighty, the Wise, it is established that He is the One, and this is the interpretation of His saying: 'Say: If there were with Him gods as they say, then they would seek a way to the Owner of the Throne' [al-Isra 17:42]; and the first according to what His saying contains: 'If there were in them gods besides Allah, they would have corrupted' [al-Anbiya 21:22]. And also if there were with God a god, the other would have manifested his wisdom and separated his action from the action of the True God so that his power and authority would be known. Then when he did not act, it became clear that God is unique in divinity and singular in lordship. And that is the meaning of His saying: 'And there was no god with Him, for then each god would have gone off with what he created' [al-Mu'minun 23:91], and likewise His saying: 'Or have they assigned to God partners who created as He created' [al-Ra'd 13:16], the verse. Then the aspect of convention indicates incapacity and ignorance, along with the fact that if it were otherwise, the matter would not belong to one in attributing creation and its realization to him except by overpowering them and bringing them under his power and making. And God is the grantor of success.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the oneness of God (tawhid) using both scriptural and rational arguments. Maturidi begins by stating that the authority of God (al-Aziz al-Hakim) establishes His oneness, then he provides the interpretation of two Quranic verses. The first verse from Surat al-Isra indicates that if there were other gods, they would seek a way to the Owner of the Throne (i.e., they would try to overthrow Him). The second verse from al-Anbiya states that if there were other gods, the heavens and earth would be corrupted. Then he gives a rational argument: if there were another god alongside God, that other god would have to manifest his wisdom and separate his actions from God's actions so that his power and authority could be known. Since he did not do so, it is clear that God alone is divine and unique in lordship. This is supported by the verse from al-Mu'minun: each god would go off with his creation, leading to celestial chaos, and the verse from al-Ra'd about false partners. Then Maturidi adds a point about the term 'al-istilah' (convention): the very concept of convention implies incapacity and ignorance, because if the matter were otherwise, the attribution of creation to one being would only be possible through coercion, not through wisdom. This confirms that God is the sole creator. The passage ends with a prayer for success.

Key Terms
التوحيد
الإلهية
الربوبية
الاصطلاح
العجز
الجهل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Maturidi merely quotes Quran without rational proof, but he integrates rational reasoning with scripture.
Correction: Maturidi presents a rational argument alongside Quranic verses: a second god would have to manifest his divinity, and since he did not, God is one.

Checkpoint

B) The universe is too complex to have been created by one god.
C) The term 'al-istilah' means 'convention' and proves multiple gods.
D) The verses cited only serve as decoration, not as proof.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi proves tawhid by arguing that a second god would have to demonstrate his divinity through distinct actions, and the absence of such actions confirms that Allah alone is divine.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

When you look at your life—your responsibilities, your fears, your hopes—do you truly act as if only Allah has ultimate power over all things, or do you sometimes rely on others as if they had independent power? What would it take for you to shift that reliance fully to Him?
?
The argument says that a true god must manifest his divinity through his actions. How does your belief in Allah's oneness manifest in your daily actions? Are there areas where your actions contradict His uniqueness—such as fearing people more than Him, or seeking approval from others as if they hold ultimate control?
?
The passage ends with 'And God is the grantor of success.' In what specific areas of your faith and practice are you relying on your own strength rather than asking Allah for success? What is one concrete step you can take today to depend on Him alone?

The Three Cases of Hidden Acts
Maturidi enumerates three cases of hidden acts between two supposed gods.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's proof that if there were two gods, three possibilities for hiding acts lead to the conclusion that only one can be truly divine.

Core Idea
Equal power to hide acts would destroy lordship.

Maturidi uses a rational argument to prove tawhid by examining the logical consequences of multiple gods. In this passage, he considers whether a second god could perform an act hidden from the first. This simple question forces us to confront the impossibility of two fully divine beings.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation. Let the argument's structure emerge.
Notice the three scenarios introduced by 'either... or...'
2
Identify the three scenarios
Find the three possibilities: both can hide acts, both cannot, or only one can. Maturidi draws a conclusion for each.
The third scenario is the only viable one for lordship.
3
Understand why each fails
Reflect on why the first two scenarios negate divinity: mutual ignorance or incapacity. The third scenario shows one is Lord, the other subservient.
Knowledge of the unseen is linked to lordship.
4
Conclude the proof
Recognize that the only consistent outcome is a single Lord. Any second god would be either inferior or impossible.
This argument is a cornerstone of tawhid reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً إنه لو كان مع الله إله لا يخلو من أن يقدر على فعل يُسره من الآخر أو لا، وكذلك الله سبحانه منه . فإن قدرًا جميعا مَلَكَ كل واحد منهما تجهيل الآخر، وفي ذلك زوال الربوبية. وإن لم يقدرا عجز كل واحد منهما، والعجز يُسقط الألوهية ؛ أو قَدَرَ أحدهما دون الآخر فهو الرب والآخر مربوب مع ما كان علم الغيب علم الربوبية ، فمن ليس له فهو مربوب. ثم لا يخلو أيضاً من قدرة كل واحد منهما على غيره في منع ما يروم الفعل بغيره ويريده أو لا ؛ فيكون فيهما إمكان خروج كُلّ عن القدرة وتحقيق عجز، وذلك يُسقط الربوبية؛ أو يقدر الواحد خاصة فيكون هو الرب سبحانه .
And also if there were with God a god, it cannot be free from that one of them is able to do an action hidden from the other or not, and likewise God the Exalted from him. If both are able, then each of them would have the ability to make the other ignorant, and in that is the removal of lordship. And if both are unable, then each of them is incapable, and incapacity negates divinity. Or if one of them is able and the other not, then that one is the Lord and the other is lorded (marbub), along with the fact that knowledge of the unseen is the knowledge of lordship, so whoever does not have it is lorded. Then also it cannot be free from that each of them has power over the other in preventing what the other intends to do and wills, or not. Thus in them there is the possibility of each exiting from power and the realization of incapacity, and that negates lordship; or only one has power specifically, and he is the Lord, the Exalted.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage presents a rational proof for tawhid by examining the hypothetical scenario of two gods. It is a classification of possibilities regarding the power of two supposed gods. Maturidi argues that if there were another god, we must consider whether each god can perform an act hidden from the other. There are three possibilities: (1) both can hide acts from each other — this would allow each to make the other ignorant, which undermines lordship; (2) both cannot hide acts — this shows incapacity, which negates divinity; (3) only one can hide acts — that one is the true Lord, and the other is subservient. Then he adds another consideration: whether each god has power to prevent the other from acting. If both have such power, then each can potentially be overpowered, leading to incapacity and removal of lordship; if only one has such power, that one is the Lord. The proof follows a logical division of cases, all of which lead to the conclusion that only one God can possess full power and lordship.

Key Terms
القدرة
العجز
الربوبية
المربوب
علم الغيب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is merely a logical exercise with no real theological weight.
Correction: It is a serious proof that a second god would necessarily be inferior or impossible, affirming tawhid.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, if two gods both have the ability to hide acts from each other, what is the consequence?
A) Each would make the other ignorant, removing lordship.
B) They would cooperate harmoniously.
C) One would defeat the other.
D) Both would be fully divine.

Key Takeaway
Only one god can possess full knowledge and power; any other would be either incapable or subordinate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If you truly believe that only Allah has full knowledge of all things, how does that affect your attitude when you are deceived by people who claim to know the future? Are you willing to reject all claims to hidden knowledge, even from those you respect?
?
Consider the logic of this passage: any being that lacks perfect knowledge is not worthy of worship. Have you ever placed your trust in something or someone that cannot possibly know what is best for your unseen future? What would it cost you to withdraw that trust and give it solely to Allah?
?
Maturidi argues that a second god would necessarily be either ignorant, incapable, or subordinate. In your daily life, do you sometimes act as if there are other 'lords'—such as money, status, or people—that have independent power over your affairs? How does this rational proof challenge you to reorient your heart's allegiance?

The Power to Prevent: Completing the Proof
Maturidi considers two gods' power to prevent each other's actions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's additional proof that if two gods existed, they would either both be able to prevent each other's actions or not, again leading to the conclusion of one God.

Core Idea
Mutual power to prevent is self-defeating; only one can have absolute power.

Maturidi extends his argument by considering whether two gods could prevent each other from acting. This second line of reasoning reinforces the impossibility of multiple omnipotent beings from a different angle.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the short passage. It presents two possibilities: both can prevent, or only one can.
This argument complements the earlier one about hidden acts.
2
Analyze the two cases
If both can prevent, each could potentially be prevented, showing incapacity. If only one can prevent, that one is the true Lord.
The logic mirrors the previous tripartite division.
3
Connect to the overall proof
See how this second argument eliminates any remaining possibility of two gods. The only consistent conclusion is tawhid.
Maturidi uses exhaustive case analysis to leave no doubt.
4
Summarize the proof
Combine both arguments: no scenario allows two fully divine beings. Therefore, Allah is one.
This is a powerful rational proof for monotheism.

Proof from Consistent Management of the Universe
Maturidi argues for the unity of God through the consistent management of creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the consistent order of natural phenomena logically leads to the conclusion that there is only one Creator.

Core Idea
Unified management of the universe proves a single Manager.

Imam al-Maturidi presents a powerful argument from the observable consistency in creation. If multiple gods existed, we would expect fluctuation and disorder in natural cycles. Instead, we see a single, coherent system—pointing to one Divine Administrator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its flow, then read the English translation. Notice how Maturidi lists several natural phenomena before drawing a conclusion.
The passage is a single continuous argument.
2
Identify the listed phenomena
Make a mental or written list of the examples Maturidi gives: seasons, crops, heaven and earth, sun/moon/stars, nourishment, animal livelihood. Observe that they all follow a consistent pattern.
These are diverse but all operate on one system.
3
Trace the logical conclusion
Reflect on why this consistency rules out multiple managers. If there were more than one, management would fluctuate. Since we see consistency, it must be one manager.
This is the core of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جـ ـ وأما دلالة الاستدلال بالخلق فهو أنه لو كان أكثر من واحد لتقلب فيهم التدبير، نحو تحول الأزمنة من نحو الشتاء والصيف، أو تحول خروج الأنزال ويَنْعها، أو تقدير السماء والأرض، أو تسيير الشمس والقمر والنجوم، أو أغذية الخلق، أو تدبير معاش جواهر الحيوان. فإذا دار كله على مسلك واحد ونوع من التدبير وانساق ذلك على سنن واحد فقد ثبت أنه لا يتم بمدبرين؛ لذلك لزم القول بالواحد. وبالله التوفيق.
The third: as for the indication of inference by [way of] creation, it is that if there were more than one, management would fluctuate among them, such as the transformation of seasons from winter to summer, or the transformation of the emergence of crops and their ripening, or the measuring of the heaven and earth, or the course of the sun, moon, and stars, or the nourishment of creatures, or the management of the livelihood of animal substances. So when all of that runs on one path and one type of management, and it proceeds on a single course, it has been established that it cannot be accomplished by two administrators; therefore the assertion of the One is necessary. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a proof from the order and consistency observed in creation. Maturidi lists various natural phenomena: the change of seasons, the growth of crops, the measurement of heaven and earth, the movement of celestial bodies, the provision of sustenance, and the management of animal life. All these phenomena follow a single, unified pattern. If there were multiple gods, there would be fluctuation and inconsistency in these systems. Since the universe operates on one consistent course, it proves that there is only one Manager. This is a classic kalam argument from design and order.

Key Terms
الاستدلال بالخلق
التدبير
الواحد
المدبرين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this argument is merely about the design or complexity of the universe, not specifically about consistency and uniformity.
Correction: Maturidi's argument focuses on the consistent management across diverse phenomena. It is the uniformity, not complexity, that points to a single manager.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The consistent and unified management of all natural phenomena is a clear sign that there is only one Creator and Manager.
