The Writing Analogy and Divine Transcendence
Uses the analogy of writing to show the world indicates a Maker without revealing His nature.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the writing analogy demonstrates that the world indicates a Creator without revealing His nature or modality.

Core Idea
Like writing indicates a writer but not his nature, the world indicates a Maker but not His quiddity.

Māturīdī extends his argument with a powerful analogy from the witnessed world: writing proves a writer exists but tells us nothing about his physical form or nature. Similarly, the world and its wonders point to a wise, knowing Creator, but we cannot deduce His modality (kayfiyya) or quiddity (māhiyya). This lesson explores that analogy and its implications for divine transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Focus on the writing analogy and its application to the world. Note the examples of hearing, sight, etc., that are affirmed without being perceived.
The passage starts with 'على أنه جاز في الشاهد'.
2
Analyze the writing analogy
Writing indicates a writer but not his modality (kayfiyya) or quiddity (māhiyya). It only proves existence, not nature. Apply this to the world.
The writer could be human, angel, or jinn – the writing itself doesn't reveal which.
3
Connect to the world and its Maker
The world's wonders require a wise, knowing originator. But like writing, this only proves His existence and attributes of wisdom and knowledge, not His essence or modality.
This preserves tanzīh (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما زعم من الأصل والفرع فمقلوب؛ لما كان القديم والقدم ولم يكن ما به استدل، فلذلك لم يجب جعله فرعا لهذا، بل هو الأصل لكون هذا به. ثم كل كائن بغيره، من طريق العقل، خارج عن جوهره في الشاهد، كالبناء والكتابة وكل أنواع الأفعال والأقوال التي هي أغيار لمن كنّ بهم، لم يجز أن يلحقهم بالجوهر والصفة، فمثله الذي به العالم. على أنه جاز في الشاهد إثبات ما لا يُدرك ولا يُحاط به نحو السمع والبصر والروح والعقل والهوى ونحو ذلك، وما يُدْرَك نحو الأجسام الكثيفة؛ فلو كانت هي قديمة الأصل فيجب أن يكون كل نوع يتولد ويحدث من جوهره: العقل من العقل، وكذلك البصر والسمع. ومعلوم الاختلاف بين كل جوهر والمتولّد منه في ذلك؛ فلزم الكون والحدث إن كان مختلفا، وفي تثبيت الاختلاف بطلان أن يكون الذي في وصف القدم عالما، أو على ما عليه صفته، وفي ذلك إثبات حدث العالم بمن ليس كمثله. وبعد، فإن الكتابة تدل على الكاتب ، ولا تدل على كيفيته أو مثله ، لما يجوز أن يكون مَلَكًا أو بشراً أو جنّاً، فتكون الكتابة غير دالة على مائية الكاتب وكيفيته ولا على مثلها، وهي تدل على كاتب ما . فمثله العالم بما فيه يدل على مُحْدِث ما ولا يدل على كيفيته ومائيته، وكذلك البناء والنَّسيج والنَّجْر والصناعات؛ لذلك لزم القياس في إثبات صانع العالم بالعالم بما فيه من العجائب والأشياء التي لا يُحتمل كونها إلا بحكيم عليم ، ولا يجب به تعرف الكيفية له والمائية . ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And what they claimed concerning the origin and branch is inverted; because the Eternal and pre-eternity existed, and there was no means by which to infer, therefore it is not necessary to make it a branch of this; rather, it is the origin because this exists through it. Then everything that comes into existence through another, by way of reason, is external to its essence in the witnessed, like building, writing, and all types of actions and sayings that are other than those by whom they are brought about; it is not permissible to attach them to the essence and attribute, so the like of that by which the world exists. However, it is permissible in the witnessed to affirm what is not perceived or encompassed, like hearing, sight, spirit, intellect, desire, and the like, and what is perceived like dense bodies. So if they were eternal in origin, it would be necessary that every type be generated and arise from its essence: intellect from intellect, and likewise sight and hearing. And the difference between every essence and what is generated from it in that is known; so existence and origination are necessitated if it is different, and in establishing difference is the invalidation of the one in the description of pre-eternity being a world, or according to his attribute, and in that is the establishment of the origination of the world by Him who is unlike it. Moreover, writing indicates the writer, but does not indicate his modality or likeness, for it is possible that he be an angel, a human, or a jinn, so writing does not indicate the quiddity or modality of the writer, nor their like, and it indicates a writer of something. Similarly, the world with what it contains indicates an originator of something and does not indicate his modality or quiddity, and likewise building, weaving, carpentry, and crafts; therefore, analogy is necessitated in establishing the Maker of the world by the world with what it contains of wonders and things whose existence is not possible except from a wise, knowing one, and it is not necessary to know his modality and quiddity thereby. There is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage refutes the argument that the witnessed serves as an 'origin' (asl) for the unseen (far'). The author argues that the Eternal (al-Qadim) and pre-eternity existed before any means of inference, so the unseen is the origin, not the branch. Then he explains that things that come into existence through another (like building, writing) are external to the agent's essence, so we cannot attribute the agent's essence to the product. In the witnessed world, we affirm things that are not perceived (like hearing, sight) alongside dense bodies. If these were eternal in origin, then each type would have to generate from its own essence (e.g., intellect from intellect), but we see difference between the essence and what is generated. This establishes the world's origination and that the Creator is unlike the world. The analogy of writing: writing indicates a writer but not his nature or likeness; similarly, the world indicates a Maker but not His modality or quiddity. Therefore, we can infer the existence of a wise, knowing Creator without knowing His nature.

مائية
كتابة
محدث
جوهر
صفة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the writing analogy implies we can know nothing about God at all.
Correction: The analogy only denies knowledge of God's modality and quiddity, not His existence or attributes of wisdom and knowledge.

Checkpoint
What does the writing analogy prove about the Maker of the world?
A) The Maker must be a human like a writer
B) The Maker exists, but His nature is not revealed
C) The Maker is an angel
D) The world is not created

Key Takeaway
Just as writing points to a writer without revealing his form, the world points to its Maker without revealing His modality or essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the wonders of the world—mountains, stars, your own body—do you genuinely see them as signs pointing to a wise and knowing Maker, or do you take them for granted? How can you cultivate that awareness daily?
?
This passage teaches that we cannot know God's modality or quiddity; we can only know He exists and is wise and knowing. Are you content with that limitation, or do you secretly wish to grasp His essence? What does your desire reveal about your faith?
?
If your life is like the 'writing' that points to a Writer, what does your character—your actions, words, choices—say about the One who authored your existence? Are you comfortable that others can read that sign, or do you try to obscure it?

The World Indicates a Maker, Not a Likeness
Maturidi states that the world indicates a Creator or self-existence, and does not indicate a likeness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the world points to a Creator without revealing His likeness or modality.

Core Idea
The world proves a Maker, but not what He is like.

Maturidi begins by stating what the world indicates: either a Creator or self-existence—both opposite to the world's own nature. He then argues that the world's indication does not extend to establishing a likeness, because that would lead to absurdity. This lesson unpacks this foundational point.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation carefully. Notice the key claim that the world does not indicate a likeness.
2
Understand the two possibilities
The world indicates either an originator or self-existence. Both are opposite to the world's own contingent nature.
3
Explore the argument against likeness
If the world indicated a likeness, everyone would imagine the whole world as like themselves—absurd.
4
Consider the substance (jawhar) discussion
Maturidi explains that we can know something absent as similar through shared modes, but that does not establish exact likeness.
This shows the limits of inference.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يدله على محدثه أو كون بنفسه، وهما خلافه؛ ثم يدله على حكمة فاعله أو سفهه، واختياره أو طبعه، وكل ذلك خلاف لما شاهده ولا يدله على أن له مثلاً، إذ لو كان يدله لكان يجب أن يتوهم كل من عاين نفسه أن يكون كل العالم مثله، وذلك بعيد. فثبت أن الجوهر لا تحقق رؤيته مثله غائبا، وتُحقق أحد الوجوه التي ذكرناها. لكن إذا عُرفت كيفية المشاهد، وأخبرت بتلك الكيفية لغائب، عُلمت أنه مثله، لا أنّ ذلك يحقق المثل، بل قد يجوز أن يدل على مثله بهذا الوجه وبما عرف مثلاً الجسم والنار الذين شاهدهما، فيعرف كل جسم ونار وإن لم يشهده. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وما زعم من الأصل والفرع فمقلوب؛ لما كان القديم والقدم ولم يكن ما به استدل، فلذلك لم يجب جعله فرعا لهذا، بل هو الأصل لكون هذا به. ثم كل كائن بغيره، من طريق العقل، خارج عن جوهره في الشاهد، كالبناء والكتابة وكل أنواع الأفعال والأقوال التي هي أغيار لمن كنّ بهم، لم يجز أن يلحقهم بالجوهر والصفة، فمثله الذي به العالم. على أنه جاز في الشاهد إثبات ما لا يُدرك ولا يُحاط به نحو السمع والبصر والروح والعقل والهوى ونحو ذلك، وما يُدْرَك نحو الأجسام الكثيفة؛ فلو كانت هي قديمة الأصل فيجب أن يكون كل نوع يتولد ويحدث من جوهره: العقل من العقل، وكذلك البصر والسمع. ومعلوم الاختلاف بين كل جوهر والمتولّد منه في ذلك؛ فلزم الكون والحدث إن كان مختلفا، وفي تثبيت الاختلاف بطلان أن يكون الذي في وصف القدم عالما، أو على ما عليه صفته، وفي ذلك إثبات حدث العالم بمن ليس كمثله. وبعد، فإن الكتابة تدل على الكاتب، ولا تدل على كيفيته أو مثله، لما يجوز أن يكون مَلَكًا أو بشراً أو جنّاً، فتكون الكتابة غير دالة على مائية الكاتب وكيفيته ولا على مثلها، وهي تدل على كاتب ما. فمثله العالم بما فيه يدل على مُحْدِث ما ولا يدل على كيفيته ومائيته، وكذلك البناء والنَّسِيج والنَّجْر والصناعات؛ لذلك لزم القياس في إثبات صانع العالم بالعالم بما فيه من العجائب والأشياء التي لا يُحتمل كونها إلا بحكيم عليم، ولا يجب به تعرف الكيفية له والمائية. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
It indicates its originator or its being by itself—both contrary to it; then it indicates the wisdom of its agent or his foolishness, and his choice or his nature—all of that contrary to what he witnessed, and it does not indicate that it has a likeness, for if it indicated it, then everyone who sees himself would necessarily imagine that the entire world is like him, and that is far. So it is established that the substance (al-jawhar) is not actualized by seeing it as a likeness absent, but one of the aspects we mentioned is actualized. However, if the mode of the witnessed is known and that mode is reported to an absent person, it is known that it is like it, but that does not actualize the likeness; rather, it may be possible that it indicates its likeness in this way and by what is known, for example the body and the fire that he witnessed, so he knows every body and fire even if he did not witness it. And there is no power except with God. And what he claimed about the root and the branch is inverted; because the eternal and eternity existed, and there was nothing by which to infer, so it was not necessary to make it a branch of this, rather it is the root because this is by it. Then every thing that comes into being by another, by way of reason, is external to its substance in the witnessed, like building and writing and all types of actions and sayings that are others (aghyar) of those by whom they are, it is not permissible to attach them to the substance and attribute, so its likeness is that by which the world exists. Indeed, it is permissible in the witnessed to affirm what is not perceived and not encompassed, like hearing, sight, spirit, intellect, desire, and the like, and what is perceived like dense bodies. For if they were eternal in origin, then every type would necessarily be generated and come to be from its substance: intellect from intellect, and likewise sight and hearing. And the difference between every substance and what is generated from it in that is known; so coming-to-be and occurrence are necessary if it is different, and in establishing the difference is the invalidity of the one in the description of eternity being knowing, or upon what His attribute is, and in that is the proof of the temporality of the world by Him who is not like it. And after that, writing indicates the writer, but does not indicate his mode or his likeness, because it is possible that he be an angel, a human, or a jinn, so writing does not indicate the quiddity (ma'iyya) of the writer or his mode, nor does it indicate a likeness of them, and it indicates some writer. So likewise the world with what is in it indicates some originator (muhdith) and does not indicate His mode or quiddity, and likewise building, weaving, carpentry, and crafts; therefore the analogy is necessary in proving the Maker of the world by the world including what is in it of wonders and things that cannot possibly exist except by a Wise, Knowing One, and it is not necessary thereby to know His mode or quiddity. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's argument that the world points to its Creator but does not disclose His essence, modality, or likeness. The author begins by stating that the world indicates either an originator or self-existence—both opposite to its own contingent nature. It also indicates the agent's wisdom or folly, choice or natural compulsion, but all these are contrary to what is observed of the world. Importantly, the world's indication does not extend to having a likeness, for if it did, anyone observing themselves would have to imagine the whole world is like them, which is absurd. Maturidi then discusses the substance (jawhar): we cannot verify seeing a substance as a likeness when absent, but we can verify one of the mentioned aspects. When the mode of a witnessed thing is known and reported to the absent, they know it is like it, but this does not actualize exact likeness; it only suggests likeness through that mode and through known examples like body and fire, so that one recognizes any body or fire even unseen. He then refutes a claim about 'root and branch' being inverted: because the Eternal and eternity existed without any inference, the world cannot be a branch of something else; rather the world is the root (subject of inference) because through it the Maker is known. Next, every thing that exists through another, by reason, is external to its substance in the witnessed (e.g., building, writing, actions, words) and cannot be attached to the substance and attribute of their agent; thus the world's likeness is that by which it exists. Maturidi further notes that in the witnessed we affirm imperceptible things like hearing, sight, spirit, intellect, desire, as well as perceptible dense bodies. If these were eternal in origin, then each type would have to generate from its own substance (intellect from intellect, etc.), but we observe differences between substances and what they generate, proving that the world is temporal and caused. Finally, using the analogy of writing pointing to a writer without revealing his quiddity or mode, Maturidi concludes that the world and its wonders indicate a Maker—Wise and Knowing—but do not require us to know His mode or quiddity.

Key Terms
محدث (muhdith)
جوهر (jawhar)
كيفية (kayfiyya)
مثل (mithl)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The world's existence proves that God is like something in the world.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the world only proves a Creator, not His likeness. If it proved a likeness, one would have to imagine the entire world as like oneself, which is absurd.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what would happen if the world indicated a likeness for its originator?
A) Everyone would know the originator's nature
B) Everyone would imagine the whole world like themselves
C) The world would be eternal
D) The originator would be known as a substance

Key Takeaway
The world points to a Creator but does not reveal His likeness or modality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the world around you, do you ever catch yourself trying to imagine what God must be like based on what you see? How does this lesson's warning against inferring a likeness challenge the way you think about your Creator? Will you commit to stopping that habit of mental image-making today?
?
You affirm that the world proves a Maker, but does your daily life reflect that truth? If the world cannot tell you what God is like, then how much more humble must you be in your knowledge? Are you willing to accept that you may never know His 'how' and yet still trust Him completely? What would change if you truly lived that acceptance?
?
The passage says that if the world indicated a likeness, everyone would imagine the whole world is like themselves. But isn't that exactly what many people do—project their own attributes onto God? Examine your own heart: have you ever reduced God to a version of yourself—your likes, your anger, your mercy? How can you repent from that and instead worship the God who is beyond all comparison?

The Root and Branch: Why the World Cannot Be a Branch
Maturidi refutes the root-branch inversion and argues from generation to prove the world's temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of the claim that the world is a branch of something else, and his argument from the generation of things proving the world's temporality.

Core Idea
The Eternal existed before all inference; the world is the root, not a branch.

Maturidi now addresses a specific objection: that the world is a branch of a prior cause. He inverts this claim, arguing that the Eternal and eternity existed without any means of inference, so the world cannot be a branch but is the subject of inference. He then extends the argument by considering things that come into being through another and how they relate to substance, culminating in a proof of the world's temporality through the difference between substance and generated things.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the refutation of the root-branch inversion.
2
Understand the inversion
Maturidi states that the claim about root and branch is inverted: the Eternal existed without any inference, so the world cannot be a branch; rather it is the root because through it the Maker is known.
3
Consider things external to substance
Things that come into being through another are external to the substance of their agent, like building and writing.
4
Explore the argument from generation
If things were eternal in origin, they would generate from their own substance. But we observe differences between substances and what they generate, proving that the world is temporal and caused.
This reinforces the need for a Creator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

It also indicates the agent's wisdom or folly, choice or natural compulsion, but all these are contrary to what is observed of the world. Importantly, the world's indication does not extend to having a likeness, for if it did, anyone observing themselves would have to imagine the whole world is like them, which is absurd. Maturidi then discusses the substance (jawhar): we cannot verify seeing a substance as a likeness when absent, but we can verify one of the mentioned aspects. When the mode of a witnessed thing is known and reported to the absent, they know it is like it, but this does not actualize exact likeness; it only suggests likeness through that mode and through known examples like body and fire, so that one recognizes any body or fire even unseen. He then refutes a claim about 'root and branch' being inverted: because the Eternal and eternity existed without any inference, the world cannot be a branch of something else; rather the world is the root (subject of inference) because through it the Maker is known. Next, every thing that exists through another, by reason, is external to its substance in the witnessed (e.g., building, writing, actions, words) and cannot be attached to the substance and attribute of their agent; thus the world's likeness is that by which it exists. Maturidi further notes that in the witnessed we affirm imperceptible things like hearing, sight, spirit, intellect, desire, as well as perceptible dense bodies. If these were eternal in origin, then each type would have to generate from its own substance (intellect from intellect, etc.), but we observe differences between substances and what they generate, proving that the world is temporal and caused. Finally, using the analogy of writing pointing to a writer without revealing his quiddity or mode, Maturidi concludes that the world and its wonders indicate a Maker—Wise and Knowing—but do not require us to know His mode or quiddity.

Key Terms
قدم (qidam)
أصل (asl)
فرع (far')
جوهر (jawhar)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The world is a branch of an eternal cause and therefore does not need a Creator.
Correction: Maturidi argues the opposite: the Eternal existed without inference, so the world is the root through which the Maker is known.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the difference between a substance and what is generated from it prove?
A) That the substance is eternal
B) That generation is impossible
C) That the world is temporal
D) That the substance is knowing

Key Takeaway
The world's temporality is proven by the difference between substances and their generated effects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often act as if this world is permanent and your ultimate home. How does knowing that every substance and its effect are different remind you that this world is not your goal, but a sign pointing to the Eternal?
?
When you look at the world, do you see it as a branch of your own desires, or as a root leading you to the Creator? What would change if you truly saw every event as a sign of His existence?
?
The passage shows that generated things are external to the agent's substance. In your life, are your actions external to your true self? How does this distinction reflect the difference between created and Creator, and what accountability does that place on you?

The Analogy of Writing and Crafts: Proving a Creator Without Essence
Maturidi uses the analogy of writing to show that the world indicates a Maker without revealing His essence or modality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's analogy that just as writing indicates a writer without revealing his quiddity or mode, so the world indicates a Wise Maker without revealing His essence.

Core Idea
Like writing, the world points to its Maker, but not what He is like.

Maturidi presents a powerful analogy: writing indicates a writer, but does not tell us whether the writer is a human, angel, or jinn, nor does it reveal his quiddity (ma'iyya) or mode (kayfiyya). Similarly, the world with its wonders indicates a Wise, Knowing Creator, but does not necessitate knowledge of His mode or quiddity. This lesson explores this analogy and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the analogy of writing and its application to the world.
2
Understand the writing analogy
Writing indicates a writer, but does not reveal his quiddity or mode. The writer could be an angel, human, or jinn.
3
Apply the analogy to the world
The world with its wonders indicates a Maker, but does not reveal His mode or quiddity.
4
Note the conclusion
Therefore, the analogy (qiyas) is necessary to prove a Maker, but it does not require knowing His mode or quiddity.
This limits theological speculation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يدله على محدثه أو كون بنفسه، وهما خلافه؛ ثم يدله على حكمة فاعله أو سفهه، واختياره أو طبعه، وكل ذلك خلاف لما شاهده ولا يدله على أن له مثلاً، إذ لو كان يدله لكان يجب أن يتوهم كل من عاين نفسه أن يكون كل العالم مثله، وذلك بعيد. فثبت أن الجوهر لا تحقق رؤيته مثله غائبا، وتُحقق أحد الوجوه التي ذكرناها. لكن إذا عُرفت كيفية المشاهد، وأخبرت بتلك الكيفية لغائب، عُلمت أنه مثله، لا أنّ ذلك يحقق المثل، بل قد يجوز أن يدل على مثله بهذا الوجه وبما عرف مثلاً الجسم والنار الذين شاهدهما، فيعرف كل جسم ونار وإن لم يشهده. ولا قوة إلا بالله. وما زعم من الأصل والفرع فمقلوب؛ لما كان القديم والقدم ولم يكن ما به استدل، فلذلك لم يجب جعله فرعا لهذا، بل هو الأصل لكون هذا به. ثم كل كائن بغيره، من طريق العقل، خارج عن جوهره في الشاهد، كالبناء والكتابة وكل أنواع الأفعال والأقوال التي هي أغيار لمن كنّ بهم، لم يجز أن يلحقهم بالجوهر والصفة، فمثله الذي به العالم. على أنه جاز في الشاهد إثبات ما لا يُدرك ولا يُحاط به نحو السمع والبصر والروح والعقل والهوى ونحو ذلك، وما يُدْرَك نحو الأجسام الكثيفة؛ فلو كانت هي قديمة الأصل فيجب أن يكون كل نوع يتولد ويحدث من جوهره: العقل من العقل، وكذلك البصر والسمع. ومعلوم الاختلاف بين كل جوهر والمتولّد منه في ذلك؛ فلزم الكون والحدث إن كان مختلفا، وفي تثبيت الاختلاف بطلان أن يكون الذي في وصف القدم عالما، أو على ما عليه صفته، وفي ذلك إثبات حدث العالم بمن ليس كمثله. وبعد، فإن الكتابة تدل على الكاتب، ولا تدل على كيفيته أو مثله، لما يجوز أن يكون مَلَكًا أو بشراً أو جنّاً، فتكون الكتابة غير دالة على مائية الكاتب وكيفيته ولا على مثلها، وهي تدل على كاتب ما. فمثله العالم بما فيه يدل على مُحْدِث ما ولا يدل على كيفيته ومائيته، وكذلك البناء والنَّسِيج والنَّجْر والصناعات؛ لذلك لزم القياس في إثبات صانع العالم بالعالم بما فيه من العجائب والأشياء التي لا يُحتمل كونها إلا بحكيم عليم، ولا يجب به تعرف الكيفية له والمائية. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
It indicates its originator or its being by itself—both contrary to it; then it indicates the wisdom of its agent or his foolishness, and his choice or his nature—all of that contrary to what he witnessed, and it does not indicate that it has a likeness, for if it indicated it, then everyone who sees himself would necessarily imagine that the entire world is like him, and that is far. So it is established that the substance (al-jawhar) is not actualized by seeing it as a likeness absent, but one of the aspects we mentioned is actualized. However, if the mode of the witnessed is known and that mode is reported to an absent person, it is known that it is like it, but that does not actualize the likeness; rather, it may be possible that it indicates its likeness in this way and by what is known, for example the body and the fire that he witnessed, so he knows every body and fire even if he did not witness it. And there is no power except with God. And what he claimed about the root and the branch is inverted; because the eternal and eternity existed, and there was nothing by which to infer, so it was not necessary to make it a branch of this, rather it is the root because this is by it. Then every thing that comes into being by another, by way of reason, is external to its substance in the witnessed, like building and writing and all types of actions and sayings that are others (aghyar) of those by whom they are, it is not permissible to attach them to the substance and attribute, so its likeness is that by which the world exists. Indeed, it is permissible in the witnessed to affirm what is not perceived and not encompassed, like hearing, sight, spirit, intellect, desire, and the like, and what is perceived like dense bodies. For if they were eternal in origin, then every type would necessarily be generated and come to be from its substance: intellect from intellect, and likewise sight and hearing. And the difference between every substance and what is generated from it in that is known; so coming-to-be and occurrence are necessary if it is different, and in establishing the difference is the invalidity of the one in the description of eternity being knowing, or upon what His attribute is, and in that is the proof of the temporality of the world by Him who is not like it. And after that, writing indicates the writer, but does not indicate his mode or his likeness, because it is possible that he be an angel, a human, or a jinn, so writing does not indicate the quiddity (ma'iyya) of the writer or his mode, nor does it indicate a likeness of them, and it indicates some writer. So likewise the world with what is in it indicates some originator (muhdith) and does not indicate His mode or quiddity, and likewise building, weaving, carpentry, and crafts; therefore the analogy is necessary in proving the Maker of the world by the world including what is in it of wonders and things that cannot possibly exist except by a Wise, Knowing One, and it is not necessary thereby to know His mode or quiddity. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage presents Maturidi's argument that the world points to its Creator but does not disclose His essence, modality, or likeness. The author begins by stating that the world indicates either an originator or self-existence—both opposite to its own contingent nature. It also indicates the agent's wisdom or folly, choice or natural compulsion, but all these are contrary to what is observed of the world. Importantly, the world's indication does not extend to having a likeness, for if it did, anyone observing themselves would have to imagine the whole world is like them, which is absurd. Maturidi then discusses the substance (jawhar): we cannot verify seeing a substance as a likeness when absent, but we can verify one of the mentioned aspects. When the mode of a witnessed thing is known and reported to the absent, they know it is like it, but this does not actualize exact likeness; it only suggests likeness through that mode and through known examples like body and fire, so that one recognizes any body or fire even unseen. He then refutes a claim about 'root and branch' being inverted: because the Eternal and eternity existed without any inference, the world cannot be a branch of something else; rather the world is the root (subject of inference) because through it the Maker is known. Next, every thing that exists through another, by reason, is external to its substance in the witnessed (e.g., building, writing, actions, words) and cannot be attached to the substance and attribute of their agent; thus the world's likeness is that by which it exists. Maturidi further notes that in the witnessed we affirm imperceptible things like hearing, sight, spirit, intellect, desire, as well as perceptible dense bodies. If these were eternal in origin, then each type would have to generate from its own substance (intellect from intellect, etc.), but we observe differences between substances and what they generate, proving that the world is temporal and caused. Finally, using the analogy of writing pointing to a writer without revealing his quiddity or mode, Maturidi concludes that the world and its wonders indicate a Maker—Wise and Knowing—but do not require us to know His mode or quiddity.

Correction: Maturidi's analogy shows that the world only indicates 'some Maker' without revealing His mode or quiddity, just as writing does not reveal the writer's nature.

Key Takeaway
The world, like writing, points to a Maker but does not reveal His essence or how He is.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the sky or a tree, do you immediately think 'a Maker made this'? Or do you take it for granted? This lesson says the world is like writing—it proves a Writer. But if you truly believed that every wonder you see is a sign pointing to Allah, how would your day be different? Would you pause more? Would your heart recognize Him more? Are you willing to start today, looking at one thing and saying 'there is a Maker' without trying to picture Him?
?
You know Allah exists, but do you secretly imagine what He is like? Maybe you picture a great being 'above' or a 'light'. This lesson confronts you: the world tells you He is, but not how He is. Can you accept that? If you truly stopped forming mental images of Allah, would your worship become more humble? More awe-filled? What would you need to let go of?
?
The analogy shows that writing points to a writer, but you don't know if the writer is an angel, human, or jinn. When you see the world's wonders—your own heartbeat, the stars—do you allow them to push you to acknowledge the Creator? Or do you need to know 'how' to believe? Maturidi says the proof is enough. Is it enough for you? If you answered 'yes', what is one thing you will do today to let this truth change how you see everything around you?

Four Indications of the World
Maturidi enumerates four indications of the world for its Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four distinct indications Maturidi identifies that the world provides for its Creator: temporality, need for a maker, unity, and divine attributes.

Core Idea
The world's different aspects prove temporality, need, unity, and divine attributes separately.

Maturidi opens by stating a foundational principle: the world's indication of its Creator varies according to its different aspects. He then enumerates four specific ways the world points to its Maker, each highlighting a different truth about the Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage with the four indications
Read the Arabic passage carefully. Notice how Maturidi lists each indication explicitly with 'then' (ثم) and 'also' (أيضا).
The four indications are: temporality, not self-caused, unity of director, and attributes of power/wisdom/knowledge.
2
Identify each indication and its implication
For each of the four, ask: what aspect of the world is observed, and what does it prove about the Creator?
3
See the logical progression
Note how the first two indications (temporality and need) establish existence of a creator, while the last two (unity and attributes) specify His nature.
This is a multi-step proof, not a single argument.
4
Review the key terms
Familiarize yourself with terms like dalāla, ḥudūth, aḍdād, ittisāq – they are central to the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : والأصل أن دلالة العالم مختلفة على اختلاف جهاته : دل احتماله الاستحالة والزوال واجتماع الأضداد في عين في حال على حدثه. ثم دل جهله بمباديه وعجزه عن إصلاح ما فسد منه أنه لم يكن بنفسه. ثم دل اجتماع الأحوال المتضادة واتساق جواهر الخلق على الاستقامة على أن مدبّر الكل ومُحدثه واحد . ويدل أيضا اتساقه واستقامته وحفظ الأضداد في عين على قدرة مدبره وحكمته وعلمه. فاختلفت جهات الدلالة فيما عليه دلالات العيان، فصار دليل إثبات المحدث عجز المحدَث، ودليل علمه لِمَا اتسق جهله بنفسه، فصار وجوه الدلالة به إنما هي على الخلاف لا الوفاق. وأصل آخر أيضاً : إن الضرورات والحاجات هي التي دلت على غير، فلم يجز أن يحتمل ما احتمل هو لِمَا يُحوج إلى غير، ثم ذلك إلى آخر إلى ما لا نهاية له، وذلك فاسد. والله أعلم.
Shaykh Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: The principle is that the indication of the world differs according to the difference of its aspects: Its possibility of change and perishing and the coming together of opposites in a single entity at one time indicated its temporality. Then its ignorance of its beginnings and its inability to repair what becomes corrupted from it indicated that it did not come to be by itself. Then the coming together of contradictory states and the harmony of the substances of creation upon rectitude indicated that the director of the whole and its originator is one. And its harmony and rectitude and the preservation of opposites in a single entity also indicate the power, wisdom, and knowledge of its director. So the aspects of indication differed with respect to what the indications of direct observation are upon; thus the proof for establishing the originator became the incapacity of the originated, and the proof of His knowledge became [the fact] that what harmonized was ignorant by itself; so the aspects of indication through it are only upon difference, not agreement. And another principle also: Necessities and needs are what indicated the other, so it is not permissible that [the other] bear what it bore [i.e., need] because [the world] needs another, then that [goes] to another ad infinitum, and that is invalid. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage classifies the ways in which the world indicates its Creator. Maturidi begins by stating that the world's indication varies according to its different aspects. He then enumerates four indications: (1) The world's possibility of ceasing to exist, changing, and hosting opposites simultaneously proves its contingency and thus its temporality (huduth). (2) The world's ignorance of its own origins and its inability to repair what decays show that it did not bring itself into existence—it is not self-caused. (3) The coexistence of contradictory states and the harmony of the substances of creation upon uprightness demonstrate that the director and originator of the whole universe is one. (4) The harmony, rectitude, and preservation of opposites in a single entity point to the power, wisdom, and knowledge of its director. Maturidi notes that these indications are based on observable realities (dilalat al-'ayan). The proof for establishing the originator is the incapacity of the originated; the proof of His knowledge is that the harmonious universe was inherently ignorant (i.e., could not have organized itself). Thus the aspects of indication point to difference (i.e., contrast between Creator and creation) rather than similarity. Finally, he adds another principle: needs and necessities in the world prove that it depends on another (God). This dependency cannot go on infinitely, so there must be a First Cause—otherwise an infinite regress would be invalid. This passage thus presents a multi-faceted cosmological argument.

Key Terms
دلالة (dalāla)
حدث (ḥudūth)
جهات (jihāt)
أضداد (aḍdād)
اتساق (ittisāq)
عجز (ʿajz)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does the world's ignorance of its own beginnings and its inability to repair itself indicate?
A) That it is eternal
B) That it did not come to be by itself
C) That it has multiple creators
D) That it is self-sufficient

Key Takeaway
The world provides multiple, distinct proofs for its Creator: its temporality, its need for a maker, the unity of its director, and the divine attributes of power, wisdom, and knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The world, including your own body, shows signs of temporality and inability to sustain itself. In what specific area of your life are you acting as though you are self-sufficient, ignoring that you are contingent on Allah every moment? What will you do today to consciously acknowledge your need for Him?
?
Maturidi argues that harmony and preservation of opposites in creation point to a single, wise Director. Think of a situation in your life where conflicting desires or circumstances seem chaotic. How does believing in a single, all-wise Director change the way you approach that chaos? What step can you take to submit to His wisdom in that area?
?
The fourth indication shows that the world's order reveals God's knowledge and power. Consider one area of your life where you feel disorder or lack of control. Instead of relying on your own planning, what would it look like to trust that God's knowledge of that situation is perfect and His power sufficient? What specific prayer or action can you commit to today?

Indications of Difference and the Need Argument
Maturidi concludes on the nature of indications and adds a need-based argument against infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's conclusion that the world's indications point to difference (not similarity) between Creator and creation, and his additional argument that the world's needs require a self-sufficient First Cause, avoiding infinite regress.

Core Idea
Indications prove Creator is different from creation; needs demand a self-sufficient cause.

Having enumerated the four indications, Maturidi now reflects on their overall character: they show difference (khilāf) and not agreement (wifāq) between the Creator and creation. He then adds a new principle: the world's needs and necessities prove its dependence on something else, and this chain cannot go on infinitely.

Guided Path
1
Read the concluding part of the passage
Read the Arabic from 'فاختلفت جهات الدلالة' to the end. Notice how Maturidi summarizes that the aspects of indication show difference.
2
Understand the logic of difference
He says the proof for the originator is the incapacity of the originated, and the proof of His knowledge is that the harmonious world was ignorant by itself. This means the Creator is opposite in attributes.
The world's incapacity and ignorance prove the Creator's power and knowledge.
3
Grasp the second principle on needs
Maturidi argues that the world's needs show it depends on another; that other if also needy would lead to infinite regress, which is invalid. Thus there must be a self-sufficient Being.
This is a classic argument for a First Cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Shaykh Abu Mansur (may God have mercy on him) said: The principle is that the indication of the world differs according to the difference of its aspects: Its possibility of change and perishing and the coming together of opposites in a single entity at one time indicated its temporality. Then its ignorance of its beginnings and its inability to repair what becomes corrupted from it indicated that it did not come to be by itself. (4) The harmony, rectitude, and preservation of opposites in a single entity point to the power, wisdom, and knowledge of its director. Thus the aspects of indication point to difference (i.e., contrast between Creator and creation) rather than similarity. Finally, he adds another principle: needs and necessities in the world prove that it depends on another (God). Key Terms

آخر (ākhar)
غير (ghayr)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the world's indications prove the Creator is similar to creation (e.g., anthropomorphism).
Correction: Maturidi explicitly states the aspects of indication show difference (khilāf), not agreement (wifāq).

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi's second principle, what do the world's necessities and needs indicate?
A) That the world is eternal
B) That it depends on another
C) That it is self-sufficient
D) That it has multiple causes

Key Takeaway
The world's indications prove that the Creator is fundamentally different from creation, and the chain of dependence cannot go on infinitely, necessitating a self-sufficient First Cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You see your own incapacity and ignorance every day. In what specific area of your life are you still relying on your own strength rather than turning to the One who is completely capable and knowing? What is one concrete step you will take today to rely on Him?
?
Everything you depend on – health, wealth, people – will eventually need something else. If you are honest with yourself, where do you place your ultimate trust? Does your heart truly rest in the Self-Sufficient Cause, or are you still attached to a chain of needs that will never end?
?
The passage says that infinite regress is invalid. Your life is finite. If you keep deferring repentance or seeking purpose in temporary things, you are building an endless chain of excuses. What is one 'need' you have been using as an excuse to avoid submitting to the Only Source? What will you do about it within the next 24 hours?

Refuting the Eternity of the World
Maturidi refutes the eternity of the world with an infinite regress argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi refutes the claim of the world's eternity by showing the absurdity of infinite regress.

Core Idea
Infinite regress is a sign of absurdity, not proof of eternity.

This passage introduces Maturidi's refutation of those who argue for the world's eternity based on its apparent endlessness. He argues that if the world had no beginning, it would require an infinite chain of causes, which is rationally impossible. He uses the principle that the witnessed (the present) cannot be used to infer something contrary to reason about the unseen (the past). Let's examine his argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to get the overall flow. Notice the technical terms like 'shahid' and 'ghaib'.
The passage is incomplete; it cuts off mid-sentence, but the key argument is clear.
2
Identify the infinite regress argument
Maturidi starts by mentioning the claim that something exists ad infinitum without an originator. He then argues that if the witnessed present is used as evidence for the unseen past, it would lead to contradictions.
Pay attention to the phrase 'laysa lahu nihaya' (without end).
3
Reflect on the principle of shahid and ghaib
Consider how Maturidi uses the principle that the witnessed (shahid) is evidence for the unseen (ghaib) only when they are analogous. Here, the claim forces a contradiction, showing the argument is invalid.
This principle is foundational in kalam theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور : ثم نذكر أقاويل من يدعى قدم العالم على ما عليه : من كون شيء إلى ما لا نهاية له بلا منشئ، بما كذلك شهده، والشاهد دليل الغائب، فيلزم ذلك في الذي غاب لأنه لو جاز إيجاب خلاف العيان بالعيان لجاز إيجاب إنسان وجسم بخلاف المعقول؛ على أن فيه إيجاب الخروج من التصور في الوهم والتقدر في العقل، وذلك آية النفي. فمثله اعتقاد شيء لا من شيء، نحو الأوقات، إنها تقع تباعا. وقد اعتبره بما لا وقت يتوهم كونه إلا وأمكن توهم مثل ذلك قبله إلى ما لا نهاية له. واعتبر أيضاً بجواز
Abu Mansur said: Then we mention the sayings of those who claim the eternity of the world as it is: from the existence of something ad infinitum without an originator, as he witnessed it likewise, and the witnessed is evidence for the unseen, so that follows for what is absent—because if it were permissible to necessitate what is contrary to direct observation by direct observation, it would be permissible to necessitate a human and a body contrary to reason; moreover, it involves necessitating exit from conceptualization in imagination and estimation in reason, and that is a sign of negation. So likewise the belief in something from nothing, like times, that they occur consecutively. And he considered it by [the fact] that there is no time that can be imagined to exist except that it is possible to imagine something like it before it ad infinitum. And he also considered by the possibility of
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage introduces a refutation of those who claim the world is eternal. Maturidi states he will mention their sayings. He begins with an argument based on infinite regress: if something exists without an originator ad infinitum, as claimed, then the witnessed (the present world) is evidence for the unseen (the past). But if we can necessitate what contradicts direct observation based on direct observation (i.e., using the present to infer a different past), then we could also necessitate a human and a body contrary to reason—a false conclusion. He argues that this line of reasoning forces one out of conceptualization in imagination and estimation, which is a sign of negation (i.e., it is absurd). He then draws an analogy: the belief in something coming from nothing, like the sequential occurrence of times, is similarly flawed. One can always imagine a prior time, leading to an infinite regress. The argument cuts off, but the direction is clear: the claim of an eternal world leads to unacceptable consequences such as infinite regress, contradicting both reason and observation. This passage is the beginning of a more extended refutation.

لا نهاية له (lā nihāya lahu)
شاهد (shāhid)
غائب (ghāʾib)
وهم (wahm)
عقل (ʿaql)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the world's apparent endlessness in time proves its eternity.
Correction: Maturidi shows this leads to infinite regress, which is rationally invalid and contradicts the shahid-ghaib principle.

Checkpoint

B) It involves necessitating something contrary to reason and imagination
C) It is supported by direct observation
D) It proves the world's self-sufficiency

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that claiming the world is eternal leads to an infinite regress, which is rationally absurd and contradicts the principle that the witnessed cannot be used to infer something contrary to reason about the unseen.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself assuming that because something has always been there, it doesn't need a Creator? How does Maturidi's argument challenge that assumption, and what will you do the next time that thought crosses your mind?
?
The passage says using the witnessed to prove something contrary to observation is like claiming a human is a body contrary to reason—clearly false. Where in your own life have you used partial evidence to support a belief that deep down contradicts what you know to be true? Will you commit to re-examining that belief today?
?
If infinite regress is absurd, then time and the world must have a beginning. Does your heart truly rest in the reality that everything depends on Allah, or do you sometimes live as if the world is self-sufficient? What one change can you make today to live more consciously of your utter dependence on Him?

The World's Temporality from Change and Opposites
Maturidi states the first proof for temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the world's susceptibility to change, disappearance, and the coexistence of opposites in one entity at one time provides a proof for its temporal origination (ḥudūth).

Core Idea
Change and opposites in the world prove it is created.

Maturidi begins his proof for the existence of a Creator by examining the nature of the world itself. He points to three observable features: change, perishment, and the gathering of opposites. These features, he argues, cannot belong to an eternal being and thus indicate that the world had a beginning in time.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage once carefully. Notice its concise style.
The passage is a single sentence but contains a complete argument.
2
Identify the three features
Find the three features Maturidi lists: الاستحالة (change/disappearance), الزوال (perishment), and اجتماع الأضداد (coexistence of opposites).
3
Understand why they indicate temporality
Reflect on why each of these features cannot be true of an eternal being. Eternal beings are unchanging, permanent, and cannot contain true opposites simultaneously.
The phrase 'في عين في حال' emphasizes opposites in one substrate at one time.
4
Consider the significance
Think about how this proof grounds the contingency argument. The world's observable qualities point directly to its createdness.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والأصل أن دلالة العالم مختلفة على اختلاف جهاته : دل احتماله الاستحالة والزوال واجتماع الأضداد في عين في حال على حدثه. ثم دل جهله بمباديه وعجزه عن إصلاح ما فسد منه أنه لم يكن بنفسه. ثم دل اجتماع الأحوال المتضادة واتساق جواهر الخلق على الاستقامة على أن مدبّر الكل ومُحدثه واحد . ويدل أيضا اتساقه واستقامته وحفظ الأضداد في عين على قدرة مدبره وحكمته وعلمه. فاختلفت جهات الدلالة فيما عليه دلالات العيان، فصار دليل إثبات المحدث عجز المحدَث، ودليل علمه لِمَا اتسق جهله بنفسه، فصار [ت] وجوه الدلالة به إنما هي على الخلاف لا الوفاق. وأصل آخر أيضاً : إن الضرورات والحاجات هي التي دلت على غير، فلم يجز أن يحتمل ما احتمل هو لِمَا يُحوج إلى غير، ثم ذلك إلى آخر إلى ما لا نهاية له، وذلك فاسد. والله أعلم.
And the principle is that the indication of the world differs according to the diversity of its aspects: its susceptibility to change, disappearance, and the aggregation of opposites in one entity at one time indicate its temporality. Then its ignorance of its own origins and its inability to repair what becomes corrupted from it indicate that it did not come to be by itself. Then the gathering of contradictory states and the consistency of the substances of creation upon rectitude indicate that the Manager of all and its Origination is One. Its consistency, rectitude, and preservation of opposites in one entity also indicate the power, wisdom, and knowledge of its Manager. So the aspects of indication differ regarding what the evident signs (dilālāt al-ʿiyān) indicate. Thus the evidence for the Temporality-creator became the incapacity of the temporal thing, and the evidence for His knowledge became what was consistent despite its ignorance of itself. So the [؟] of the indications thereby are upon difference, not agreement. And another principle also: that necessities and needs are what indicate another, so it is not permissible that what itself is subject to need should be what is needed, leading to something else ad infinitum, and that is false. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the contingency of the world and the attributes of its Creator, based on the different aspects of the created order. Maturidi begins by stating that the world's indications vary according to its different aspects. He then enumerates three lines of reasoning: First, the world's susceptibility to change, disappearance, and the coexistence of opposites in one substrate at one time indicates its temporal origination (ḥudūth). Second, the world's ignorance of its own origins and its inability to repair its own corruption shows that it did not bring itself into existence. Third, the gathering of contradictory states and the consistent ordering of the substances of creation upon rectitude demonstrate that the Manager (mudabbir) and Origination (muḥdith) of the whole is One. Additionally, the consistency, rectitude, and preservation of opposites in one entity indicate the power (qudra), wisdom (ḥikma), and knowledge (ʿilm) of its Manager. Maturidi then explains that the aspects of indication differ: the evidence for the existence of the temporal creator is the incapacity of the temporal thing, while the evidence for His knowledge is the consistency that exists despite the thing's ignorance of itself. He notes that the directions of the indications are upon difference, not agreement—meaning each aspect points to a different attribute. He then adds another principle: that needs and necessities indicate something else that fulfills them, so it is impossible for the thing that itself has needs to be that which is needed, as that would lead to an infinite regress, which is false. The passage thus establishes both the existence of a Creator and His attributes through multiple, distinct proofs.

Key Terms
استحالة
زوال
اجتماع الأضداد
حدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that change and opposites can be explained by eternal cycles without a beginning.
Correction: Maturidi argues that the specific coexistence of opposites in one substrate at one time cannot be eternal because it would imply contradiction in the essence of an eternal being.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what does the coexistence of opposites in one entity at one time indicate?
A) The world's eternal nature
B) The world's temporality
C) The world's self-sufficiency
D) The world's multiple creators

Key Takeaway
The world's features of change, perishment, and opposites in one substrate at one time prove it is not eternal but created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the world you see every day is passing away and even opposites can exist together momentarily, why do you cling to its pleasures as if they will last? What one attachment this week can you begin to loosen because it is only temporal?
?
You say you believe this world is created, but does your daily schedule reflect that belief? Where do you act as if your possessions, health, or relationships are eternal? Write down one specific area where you will remind yourself daily that it will perish.
?
The proof that opposites can coexist in one thing shows that nothing here is truly stable. When you face a situation that feels contradictory—pain and blessing, hardship and ease—do you remember that this very instability proves you are not in an eternal state? How can you use that truth to turn your heart toward the Eternal today?

The World's Dependence and the One Creator's Attributes
Maturidi presents two more proofs for the Creator and His attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the world's ignorance of its own origins, inability to repair itself, and the consistent ordering of substances point to a single Creator with attributes of power, wisdom, and knowledge.

Core Idea
The world's order and dependence prove it has one wise Creator.

Having established that the world is temporal, Maturidi now shows that it must have a Creator who is not like it. He argues from the world's need for an external cause and from the remarkable consistency observed in creation, which suggests a single, powerful, and wise designer.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the passage that includes two proofs: from ignorance/inability and from consistency/opposites.
Notice the transition words 'ثم' and 'ويدل أيضا'.
2
Analyze the first proof: ignorance and inability
Maturidi argues that the world's ignorance of its own origins and its inability to fix what becomes corrupted show it did not create itself.
This is a variation of the argument from contingency.
3
Analyze the second proof: consistency and opposites
The gathering of contradictory states and the consistent ordering of substances indicate a single Manager. Additionally, the preservation of opposites in one entity shows the Manager's power, wisdom, and knowledge.
Note that 'اتساق' (consistency) and 'استقامة' (rectitude) are key.
4
Understand the summary statement
Maturidi concludes that different aspects indicate different attributes: incapacity proves the Creator's existence, consistency despite ignorance proves His knowledge.
The phrase 'على الخلاف لا الوفاق' means each aspect points to a different attribute.

Source Text

The Impossibility of Infinite Regress of Needs
Maturidi presents the argument from need against infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the principle that needs indicate a source of fulfillment and why an infinite regress is impossible, leading to the necessity of a self-sufficient Creator.

Core Idea
Infinite regress is impossible; the chain of need must end with the Self-Sufficient.

Maturidi concludes his argument with a powerful logical principle. He observes that everything in the world is marked by need and dependence. If every needy thing required another needy thing, the chain would go on forever, which is absurd. Therefore, there must exist a being that is not needy, but rather the source of all needs.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read this short but dense passage. It contains a logical argument against infinite regress.
The phrase 'وأصل آخر' indicates a new principle.
2
Understand the premise: needs indicate another
Maturidi states that necessities and needs (ḍarūrāt wa ḥājāt) indicate something else (ghayr) that fulfills them.
This is a cause-effect relationship.
3
Grasp the infinite regress argument
If a thing that itself has needs (like the world) were the one needed, it would require another, leading to an endless chain. Such a chain is impossible (fāsid).
This is a standard argument in kalām.
4
Conclude the necessity of a self-sufficient being
Therefore, the chain must end with a being that has no needs (al-ghanī). That being is the Creator.
Though not explicit in the passage, it is implied by the rejection of infinite regress.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

You recognize that every need points to something beyond it. What is the one deep need in your life right now that you have been ignoring or trying to fill with created things? How does this lesson challenge you to redirect that need to the Self-Sufficient One?
?
Think of a chain of dependencies in your daily life—perhaps your happiness depends on a job, which depends on the economy, which depends on countless factors. Maturidi says such a chain cannot be endless. Are you willing to stop at the first cause, Allah, and find your ultimate satisfaction in Him alone, even if the lower links fail?
?
This passage declares that an infinite regress of needs is false. Yet you often behave as if your needs could be fulfilled by an endless series of worldly goals. What would it mean for you today to truly end that chain and submit your needs to the only One who is not needy?

Six Views of the Deniers of Creation and Their Refutation
Maturidi enumerates six arguments for the world's pre-eternality and refutes them using the impossibility of conceptualizing an infinite regress.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the six common arguments for the pre-eternality of the world and the core rational refutation Maturidi uses against all of them.

Core Idea
All arguments for a pre-eternal world fail because they try to imagine what cannot be imagined.

The debate about whether the world is created or eternal is foundational in Islamic theology. Here, Imam Maturidi presents six distinct views held by those who claim the world has no beginning. After listing each, he delivers a powerful refutation rooted in the limits of human imagination and conceptualization. This lesson walks through the arguments and shows how Maturidi dismantles them with a single, elegant principle.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the translation. Notice the structure: six views followed by a collective refutation.
The passage contains technical terms like wahm (imagination) and hayūlā (hyle).
2
Identify the six views
The passage lists six distinct claims for the world's pre-eternality. Try to list them in your own words before reading the commentary.
They range from infinite chains to primeval matter.
3
Focus on the refutation
Maturidi refutes all views together with a dilemma about imagination (wahm). Pay attention to the logic: either they can conceive of rejecting the inconceivable (contradiction) or they cannot (still problematic).
This refutation shows the weakness of relying on imagination without rational proof.
4
Connect to broader kalam
Understand that this argument is part of establishing the world's temporality (ḥudūth) and thus the existence of a Creator. Maturidi's method is to show that the deniers' positions are not even coherently conceivable.
This lesson complements the classic argument from contingency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال أبو منصور : } ثم نذكر أقاويل من يدعى قدم العالم على ما عليه : ١ من كون شيء إلى ما لا نهاية له بلا منشئ، بما كذلك شهده، والشاهد دليل الغائب، فيلزم ذلك في الذي غاب لأنه لو جاز إيجاب خلاف العيان بالعيان لجاز إيجاب إنسان وجسم بخلاف المعقول؛ على أن فيه إيجاب الخروج من التصور في الوهم والتقدر في العقل، وذلك [١٠] آية النفي. فمثله اعتقاد شيء لا من شيء، نحو الأوقات، إنها تقع تباعا. وقد اعتبره " بما لا وقت يتوهم كونه إلا وأمكن توهم [مثل] ذلك قبله إلى ما لا نهاية له. واعتبر أيضاً بجواز البقاء بما لا يبقى . ٢ - ومنهم من يقول بكون شيء بشيء إلى ما لا نهاية له بمنشيء حكيم، وجعلوه علة كون العالم، ومحال كون العلة ولا معلول. مع ما لا يخلو من [أن] لا يوصف بالقدرة والجود في القدم وذلك آية العجز والحاجة، أو يوصف فيجب المقدور عليه وإفاضة الجود على كل شيء؛ وما ذُكِرَ من التوهم لهم أيضاً . ٣ - ومنهم من يقول بقدم الطينة، وهي الأصل، وحدَثِ الصَّنْعة. { قال أبو منصور رحمه الله : } فقوله بقدم الطينة لما ذكرنا من رفع كون شيء لا عن شيء. ثم كان كل شيء حَدَث عن شيء حدث عند انقلاب الأول وهلاكه ، نحو ما يحدث من النطفة والبيضة . ٤ ـ ومنهم من جعل حدثه بعوارض حلّت بالطينة فانقلبت إلى ما عليه الطبائع من الاعتدال والاختلاف. ٥ - ومنهم من جعل حدثه بالباري. ٦ - ومنهم من قال بالأصل وسماه هَيُولَى . قال الشيخ أبو منصور رحمه الله : } فجملة ما ذهب إليه هؤلاء دفع ما لا يتصور في الوهم ولا يتمثل في النفس، إذ كذلك وجد ولم تحتمل قلوبهم إيجاب خلافه. فيقال: أيتصوّر في أوهامكم دفع ما لا يتمثل في النفس ؟ فان قال : نعم ، كَابَرَ ، لمشاركتنا إياه في ذي الصور، وليس يتصوّر دفعه هذا في أوهامنا . وإن قال : لا ، بل تقديره ؛ فيقال له : متى يتصوّر في الوهم
Abu Mansur said: 'Then we mention the sayings of those who claim the pre-eternality (qidam) of the world according to what they are: 1. From something existing ad infinitum without an originator, according to what they witnessed, and the witness is evidence of the absent, so that would necessitate in the absent because if affirming contrary to the witnessed by the witnessed were permissible, then it would be permissible to affirm a human and a body contrary to the intellect; on that there is necessitating exiting from conceptualization in the imagination (wahm) and estimation in the intellect, and that is a sign of negation. Similarly is believing in something from nothing, like times, that they occur successively. And he considered it by what there is no time that can be imagined to exist except that the imagining of something similar before it to infinity is possible. And he also considered by the permissibility of permanence by what does not abide. 2. And among them are those who say that something exists by something ad infinitum with a wise originator, and they made it the cause of the world's existence, and it is impossible for the cause to exist without the effect. Along with what is inevitable: either He is not described with power and generosity in pre-eternity, and that is a sign of incapacity and need; or He is described, and then the determined thing upon Him and the flowing of generosity upon everything become necessary; and what was mentioned of imagination for them also. 3. And among them are those who say the pre-eternality of the clay (ṭīna), which is the origin, and the temporality of the craftsmanship. Abu Mansur said: 'His statement about the pre-eternality of the clay is because of what we mentioned of raising the existence of something from nothing. Then everything that arises from something occurs upon the destruction and annihilation of the first, like what arises from the sperm and the egg.' 4. And among them are those who made its arising by accidents that befell the clay, so it transformed into what the natures (ṭabāʾiʿ) are upon of moderation and difference. 5. And among them are those who made its arising by the Creator (al-Bārī). 6. And among them are those who said the origin and called it Hyle (hayūlā). Imam Abu Mansur said: 'The totality of what these people have gone to is the repulsion of what cannot be conceptualized in the imagination (wahm) nor represented in the soul, for thus they found it, and their hearts could not bear affirming its opposite. So it is said to them: Can you conceptualize in your imaginations the repulsion of what cannot be represented in the soul? If they say yes, they are stubborn, because they share with us in the realm of forms, and this repulsion cannot be conceptualized in our imaginations. And if they say no, rather its estimation, then it is said to them: When can it be conceptualized in the imagination?'
Source pages p. 92

Commentary
This passage is a classification and refutation of the views of those who claim the world is pre-eternal (qadīm). Maturidi first lists six lines of argument: (1) The claim of an infinite chain of existents without an originator, based on what they observe; but this is invalid because it would allow affirming something contrary to what the senses show, and it violates imaginability and conceptualization. (2) The claim of an infinite chain with a wise originator, who is the cause of the world; but then either the cause is not described with power and generosity, implying need, or He is described and then the determined effects become necessary, which contradicts the observed absence of universal generosity. (3) The view that the primeval clay (ṭīna) is pre-eternal and the craftsmanship is temporal; Maturidi notes that this still avoids something from nothing but requires an infinite regress of changes. (4) The view that temporality arises from accidents befalling the pre-eternal clay. (5) The view that the temporal arises from the Creator directly. (6) The view that the origin is Hyle (hayūlā), the prime matter. Maturidi then refutes all these views collectively: they are trying to reject something that cannot be conceptualized in the imagination (wahm) or represented in the soul (nafs). He then poses a dilemma: either they claim they can conceive of rejecting that which is inconceivable, which is stubbornness and contradicts their own ability to conceive of forms; or they admit they cannot conceive of it but merely estimate it, in which case the question remains: when does it become conceivable? The refutation shows that their arguments are based on arbitrary assumptions about what can be imagined, and that consistency undermines their position.

طينة
هيولى
توهم
وهم
غائب

According to Maturidi, what is the common flaw in all the arguments for the world's pre-eternality?
A) They rely on miracles
B) They attempt to repel what cannot be conceptualized in the imagination
C) They deny the existence of God
D) They are based on empirical evidence

Key Takeaway
The world cannot be pre-eternal because arguments for its pre-eternality rely on the impossible task of conceptualizing an infinite regress or other inconceivable notions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself trying to imagine the pre-eternal world or the mind of God, relying on your own mental images? What does this lesson teach you about the limits of your imagination in such matters?
?
When you reflect on your own belief in the creation of the world, do you sometimes feel a tension between what the intellect demands and what the heart finds comfortable? This lesson shows that the deniers' hearts 'could not bear' affirming the opposite. Where in your own faith do you find it hardest to submit to intellectual truth?
?
The dilemma Maturidi poses forces one to admit either stubbornness or estimation. In your own life, are there beliefs you hold not because they are rationally necessary but because you have not honestly faced the question 'Can I conceptualize the alternative?' How will you rectify that starting today?

Why Things Cannot Come from a Pre-Existing Source
Maturidi refutes the materialist claim that things originate from a pre-existing source.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that the claim of things preexisting in their source is rationally impossible and why it leads to affirming a Creator and the temporal origination of the world.

Core Idea
If a thing were contained in its source, one substance would hold infinite beings—an impossibility.

In this passage, Maturidi tackles a subtle but crucial point: the claim that things simply emerge from pre-existing material. He shows that this idea collapses under rational scrutiny. By examining what it would mean for a thing to be 'in' its source, he leads us to see the necessity of a Creator and that the world itself is temporally originated.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the main claim being refuted
Find the phrase 'كون الشيء من الشيء' and understand what it means. Maturidi is refuting the idea that a thing emerges from a pre-existing source by being concealed in it.
3
Understand the impossibility argument
Maturidi points out that if a human or tree were contained in a source like water, it would require infinite parts in one substance—a rational impossibility. Ruminate on why this is absurd.
4
Examine the nutritional examples
Maturidi gives examples from food and growth to show that food does not directly become body. Different animals produce different substances from the same food, indicating a design beyond mere material transformation.
This supports the conclusion that a wise Arranger is necessary.
5
Trace the conclusion
Realize that the passage ends by asserting the necessity of a Creator and the temporal origination of the world. Note how the argument builds from impossibility to theistic conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قدم الشيء أو بقاؤه بعد التفرق وأن يصير بحيث لا يأخذه البصر ؟ وقد يقول بذلك كله . ومع ذلك في الأنْفُس ما لا يتمثل من السمع والبصر وجَرْي قوى جوهر واحد من الطعام وتولّد قوة الجواهر المختلفة به كالسمع والبصر والفهم واليد والرجل وغيرها ، فما ينكر مثله في تلك الجملة بالأدلة. ثم يقال له: لا يعدو كون الشيء من الشيء من أن يكون مستجنّاً فيه فظهر . وذلك محال : أن يكون الإنسان بكليته، والشجر بكليته مع ما يثمر يكون في ذلك الأصل؛ أو بجوهرهم يكونون في أصل الماء الذي كان في صلب، فيسع في الشيء الواحد ما لا يحصى من الأضعاف. وذلك مما لا يُحتمل تمثله في نفس صحيحة ولا يصبر عليه عقل سليم. وذلك يبطل قوله كون الشيء من الشيء، لأنه بكليته لم يكن من النطفة. وليس له أن يدعى كونه في الأغذية؛ لأنه يبلغ وقتا في العِظم لا يزداد ألبتة، وتلك الأغذية كلها موجودة أو فيها زيادة بالجوهر. وكم من جوهر يُسمن ، وآخر يأكل ذلك عُمْرَه فلا يظهر ؛ وترى التوت وورقه يأكله نَعَم فيخرج من كلّ غير الذي يخرج من غيره، وكذلك الثمر وغيره. فهذا يبين أن ذلك ليس بعمل الأغذية. على أن الأغذية هُنَّ مَوَاتٌ لا يُحتمل أن تصير كذلك إلا بتدبير مدبر عليم، لا أن استفادَ ذلك المعنى من غيره بلا تدبير. وفي ذلك لزوم القول بالذي قلنا. أو أنْ كان حَدَث شيء منه ء منه أو بعضه - لا أن كان [ مستجنّا] في شيء مما ذكر ـ فيجب القول بحدث العالم بما لزم في بعضه.
The existence of a thing or its remaining after separation and becoming such that the eye does not perceive it? And yet one says all that. But in the souls there is what cannot be represented from hearing and sight and the flow of powers of one substance from food and the generation of power of different substances through it, like hearing and sight and understanding and hand and leg and others. So what denies the like of that in that whole by evidence? Then it is said to him: The being of a thing from a thing does not go beyond being concealed in it and then appearing. And that is impossible: that a human being in his entirety, and a tree in its entirety with what it produces, should be in that origin; or that by their substance they should be in the origin of water that was in the loins, so that in one thing there would be countless multiples. That is something that cannot be represented in a sound soul nor endured by a sound mind. And that invalidates his statement that a thing comes from a thing, because in its entirety it was not from the sperm-drop. And he cannot claim its existence in foods, because it reaches a time in size when it does not increase at all, and those foods all exist or have an increase in substance. And how many a substance fattens, and another eats that its whole life and it does not appear? And you see mulberries and their leaves eaten by cattle, and from each comes out other than what comes out from another, and likewise fruits and other things. This shows that that is not the work of foods. Moreover, foods are lifeless and cannot become like that except by the arrangement of an arranging, knowing [God], not that they gained that meaning from another without arrangement. And in that is the necessity of asserting what we have said. Or if a thing came into existence from something else or some part of it - not that it was [concealed] in something mentioned - then it is necessary to assert the temporal origination of the world because of what is necessary in some part of it.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that things originate by emerging from a pre-existing material source. Maturidi begins by noting that even within human experience, there are things like the faculties of hearing and sight that cannot be physically represented as coming from food, yet they are accepted by evidence. He then argues that if someone claims a thing comes from another, it must have been concealed within it and then appeared. This is impossible because it would require an entire human being and tree to be contained in a single origin like water, leading to infinite parts in one thing—which is rationally untenable. He further illustrates with nutrition: food does not directly become body, as evidenced by growth stopping while food continues, and different animals producing different substances from the same food. Thus, the process requires an all-knowing Designer. The conclusion is that even if something originates partially from another, the world's temporal origination follows.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think Maturidi is arguing against biological reproduction or embryology.
Correction: The argument is philosophical: it targets the notion of eternal matter and the impossibility of containment, not biological processes.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's refutation of the claim that things come from pre-existing matter demonstrates that the world's existence requires a wise Creator and is temporally originated.

?

?

Yet we often attribute the complexities of our own existence to natural processes like heredity and biology without thinking. Is your faith in Allāh's creation merely a mental assent, or does it actually shape how you view your own origin and purpose? What would change if you truly internalized that you are not a product of matter but a direct act of creation?
?
The proof shows that even the variety of outputs from the same food (e.g., mulberries eaten by cattle) cannot be explained by the food itself. If you do not, what is preventing your heart from seeing the Hand of the Creator in every transformation?
