Finitude of Parts Necessitates Finitude of the Whole
Maturidi argues that since each observed thing is finite, the whole world must be finite and thus created.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the finitude of each observed part necessitates the finitude of the whole world, and that this implies the world's temporal origination.

Core Idea
If every part is finite, the whole cannot be infinite.

Imam Maturidi presents a concise but powerful argument for the temporality of the world. He begins with a premise that even his opponents accept: every observable thing has an end. Then he challenges them to apply the same logic to the whole. This passage shows the internal consistency required in theological reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and its translation
Read the Arabic text once to get a feel for Maturidi's style, then read the literal translation. Note that the argument is presented as a rhetorical challenge to opponents.
Pay attention to the phrase 'ثم يقال لهم' which sets up a direct address.
2
Identify the main structure of the argument
The argument has three parts: (1) the premise that every observed thing is finite, (2) the question why the whole would not be finite, (3) a supplementary reasoning using the concept of place and the conclusion of temporal origination.
The argument uses a reductio by parity of reasoning.
3
Understand the supplementary argument about place
Maturidi adds that parts serve as places for each other, but the whole cannot occupy a place, implying its finitude. This strengthens the main argument.
The phrase 'زوال الحمل' refers to the removal of the attribute of bearing, i.e., the whole cannot be contained.
4
Reflect on the conclusion 'لزوم الحدث'
The conclusion is that the necessity of temporal origination follows. Consider why the finitude of the world implies it is created.
This connects the argument to the broader Islamic theological claim that the world is not eternal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم يقال لهم : إذ كل مشاهد ذو نهاية ، وجعلتموه دليل العالم، لم لا كان الكل كذلك؟ وإلا لو جاز كون شيء منه متناه وجملته لا ، لِمَ لا جاز كون شيء منه عن شيء وجملته لا؟ وكذلك نرى بعضه لبعضه مكانا، ولا يحتمل جملته المكان لزوال الحمل. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وفي ذلك لزوم الحدث.
Then it is said to them: Since every observed thing has an end, and you have made it a proof for the world, why is not the whole likewise? Otherwise, if it were permissible for a part of it to be finite and its whole not, why is it not permissible for a part of it to be from a thing and its whole not? And likewise we see some of it a place for some other, and its whole cannot take place because of the removal of bearing. And there is no power except with God. And in that is the necessity of temporal origination.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the temporal origination of the world based on the finitude of its parts. Maturidi says to the opponents: you accept that each observable thing has an end (is finite), and you use that as evidence for the world's composition. So why would the whole not be finite as well? If you say that a part can be finite while the whole is not, then why not also say that a part originates from something while the whole does not? He then adds that we see some parts serve as a place for others, but the whole cannot occupy a place because the relation of place-holder fails. Therefore, the world must be temporally originated (hadith).

Key Terms
مشاهد
نهاية
الكل
متناه
مكان
زوال الحمل
الحدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument is about the world being finite in size.
Correction: Maturidi is not primarily concerned with spatial finitude but with logical contingency: the finitude of parts implies the whole cannot be self-sufficient or eternal, leading to temporal origination.

Checkpoint
What conclusion does Maturidi draw from the fact that each observed thing is finite?
A) That the whole world must be infinite
B) That the whole world must also be finite
C) That the world has no beginning
D) That parts can be infinite

Key Takeaway
Maturidi argues that the finitude of each part logically entails the finitude and thus creation of the whole world.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
Maturidi's proof shows the world cannot be eternal. You believe in an eternal Allah. How does this realization affect your view of time and your priorities? Are you living for the eternal or the temporary?
?
The passage ends with 'There is no power except with God.' How often do you truly rely on Allah's power in your daily decisions, versus acting as if you are independent? Commit to one specific situation today where you will consciously acknowledge your dependence on Him.

Maturidi's Proof from Imagination and Accidents
Maturidi proves the world's origination by imagination and accident arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the conceivability of a prior moment and the impossibility of being accident-free to prove the world's beginning.

Core Idea
If you can imagine a time before any moment, the world must have a beginning.

Maturidi presents two concise arguments that together show why the world cannot be eternal. The first argument uses a simple logical tool: whenever you pick a moment, you can always imagine another one just before it — this forces a beginning. The second argument shows that if the world could exist without any accidents, rationality itself would collapse. Read carefully to see how these two threads weave together.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without pausing. Then read the translation. Let the flow of the argument settle before you analyze.
The text is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the first proof (imagination)
Find the sentence about 'tawahhum'. Maturidi says that for any time you imagine, you can imagine a like time afterwards. That implies a starting point.
This is a logical argument, not a psychological one.
3
Identify the second proof (accidents)
Look for the part about 'ikhla'' (emptying). If the world could be empty of all accidents, then rational consistency would be overturned, e.g., a living thing could be dead.
The impossibility of emptiness proves the world is always changing, so it must have a beginning.
4
Connect the two proofs
Understand that both arguments point to the same conclusion: the world is hadith (originated). They are independent but complementary.
Maturidi uses 'fa' (so) to link the second proof to the conclusion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذكرنا من البقاء قد بيناه فيما تقدم. وما ذُكر من التوهم، فكذلك ما من وقت يتوهم / إلا وأمكن توهم كون مثله من بَعْدُ، فيجب به حدثه ؛ مع ما إذا لم يُجْعَل لأوليّته وقت يبطل كله . وبعد، فإنه لو جاز إخلاء العالم أو أصله عما يُحتمل من الحوادث لجاز أيضا قَلْبُ كل معقول : من جواز حي ميت في حال ، فثبت حدث الكلية بما لا يخلو عنه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And what we mentioned of remaining we have already explained previously. And what was mentioned of imagining, likewise there is no time one imagines except that it is possible to imagine another like it afterwards, so its temporal origination is necessitated; along with the fact that if no time is assigned for its beginning, the whole is invalidated. Moreover, if it were permissible to empty the world or its origin from what is possible of accidents, then it would also be permissible to overturn every rational: from the permissibility of a living thing being dead at a state. So the temporal origination of the whole is established by what it is not free from. And there is no power except with God.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents two interconnected proofs. First, the argument from 'tawahhum' (imagination): for any given time, one can imagine another similar time subsequent to it. If the world were eternal, there would be no first moment, but the possibility of imagining a like at every point implies that the world must have had a beginning (hadith). Second, the argument from accidents: if the world or its matter could be devoid of all possible accidents, then rational consistency would be overturned—e.g., a living being could be dead at the same moment. Since the world is never free from accidents, its temporal origination is established.

Key Terms

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The imagination argument is merely psychological: because we can imagine a prior time, that proves the world is old.
Correction: Maturidi is using logical conceivability to expose incoherence in the idea of an eternal past. It is not about human psychology but about the impossibility of an infinite regress of moments without a starting point.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what follows from the fact that at any time one can imagine another like it?
A) The world is eternal
B) The world must have a beginning
C) The world is uncreated
D) Imagination is irrelevant

Key Takeaway
The world must have a beginning because at every moment we can imagine a prior moment, and because it is never free from accidents that themselves began.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the entire universe had a beginning from nothing, what does that say about your own sense of self-sufficiency? Are there areas of your life where you act as if you are self-originated, forgetting your dependence on the Creator? Reflect on a specific attachment or habit that you treat as eternal, and commit to remembering its temporal nature today.
?
The proof from accidents shows that everything in the world is constantly changing and dependent. How does your desire for stability and control confront this reality? In what aspect of your life are you resisting change or refusing to accept that you are not in control? What would it mean to surrender that area to God right now?
?
Maturidi argues that without a beginning, the whole concept of time is invalidated. Your own existence also has a beginning. When you really internalize that you were brought into existence from nothing, how does that reshape your purpose? What one action will you take today to live in awareness of that fact?

The Whole and Part Argument for Creation
Maturidi argues that if a part is finite, the whole must also be finite, proving creation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's whole-and-part argument for the createdness of the world and how it functions as a reductio against the eternity of the world.

Core Idea
If a part is finite, the whole cannot be infinite—therefore the world must be created.

Imam Maturidi presents a concise but powerful argument based on the relationship between parts and the whole. He challenges the notion that the world could be eternal by showing a logical consequence of finitude in its parts. This passage is a classic example of kalam reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
This passage is dense, so take your time.
2
Identify the logical structure
Notice the conditional statement at the beginning: 'If it were possible for something from it to be finite and its whole not...' Then see the example of place. Finally, the conclusion: 'And in that is the necessity of createdness.'
3
Understand the reductio
This argument is a reductio ad absurdum against the opponent who claims the world is eternal. It shows that accepting a finite part leads to a contradiction if the whole is infinite.
The opponent's position is that the world is eternal (uncreated).
4
Connect to the conclusion
The argument forces the opponent to accept that the whole must be finite like its parts, which implies the world is created. The example of place reinforces this: if parts can contain each other, the whole cannot be infinite.
This is a classic argument in Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لو جاز كون شيء منه متناه وجملته لا ، لِمَ لا جاز كون شيء منه عن شيء وجملته لا؟ وكذلك نرى بعضه لبعضه مكانا، ولا يحتمل جملته المكان لزوال الحمل. ولا قوة إلا بالله . وفي ذلك لزوم الحدث. وما ذكرنا من البقاء قد بيناه فيما تقدم.
If it were possible for something from it to be finite and its whole not, why is it not possible for something from it to be from something and its whole not? Likewise we see some of it a place for some of it, and its whole cannot bear the place because of the removal of bearing. And there is no power except with Allah. And in that is the necessity of createdness. And what we mentioned of [eternal] subsistence we have already explained previously.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents an argument for the createdness (huduth) of the world based on the relationship between a part and the whole. Maturidi begins with a conditional: if it were possible for a part of the world to be finite while the whole is not, then why should it not be possible for a part to originate from something while the whole does not? This is an attempt to expose a contradiction in the opponent's position. He then gives an example: we observe that some parts of the world serve as a place for other parts (like a body containing another), but the whole cannot serve as a place because that would remove the notion of bearing (a infinite whole cannot contain something). The conclusion is that the world must be created (hadith). He also refers to a previous discussion on remaining (baqa') which he had already explained.

Key Terms
جزء
كل
متناه
حدث
بقاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument assumes what it proves.
Correction: It is a reductio ad absurdum against those who claim the world is eternal.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what follows if a part of the world is finite while the whole is not?
A) The whole must also be finite.
B) The whole can be infinite.
C) The world must be eternal.
D) The part must be infinite.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's whole-part argument demonstrates that finitude in the parts necessitates finitude in the whole, proving the world's createdness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every part of your life—your health, wealth, relationships—is finite, why do you invest so much in them as if they will last forever? What would it take for you to reorient your priorities toward the One who alone is infinite?
?
You rely on created things every moment for your existence (air, food, etc.). How does the fact that these 'parts' are finite and dependent affect your sense of security? Are you truly trusting the Creator who sustains them?
?
Maturidi argues that the whole world cannot bear the weight of itself—it needs a Sustainer. In what area of your life are you trying to be self-sufficient, bearing a burden that only Allah can carry? What step can you take today to surrender that to Him?

The Argument from Temporal Moments
Maturidi argues from temporal imagination to the world's createdness

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the impossibility of an infinite past proves the world's creation.

Core Idea
If time has no beginning, the whole series collapses.

This lesson unpacks Maturidi's second argument for the world's createdness. He uses the concept of imagined moments to show that the past must have a starting point. Without a first moment, the entire temporal chain becomes logically invalid.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its literal translation. Pay attention to the key terms 'توهم' (imagination) and 'وقت' (time).
2
Understand the main claim
The passage asserts that for any imagined time, one can always imagine a later time after it. This means the series of past moments cannot be infinite because you could always add another.
3
Grasp the conclusion
Because of this, the world's createdness (حدوث) is necessitated. If no beginning is set, the whole temporal series becomes invalid.
4
Reflect on the consequence
Consider why an infinite past is impossible: if there were no first moment, you could never reach the present. This logical contradiction proves the world must have a beginning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذُكر من التوهم، فكذلك ما من وقت يتوهم إلا وأمكن توهم كون مثله من بعد، فيجب به حدثه; مع ما إذا لم يُجْعَل لأوليّته وقت يبطل كله
And what is mentioned of imagination, so likewise there is no time that is imagined except that it is possible to imagine the existence of something like it after it, so its createdness is necessitated by it; along with the fact that if no time is set for its beginning, the whole becomes invalid.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage gives a second argument for the world's createdness, focusing on the concept of time and imagination. Maturidi states that for any imagined point in time, one can always imagine another similar time after it. This means the temporal series cannot be infinite in the past because there would be no first moment. If no beginning is posited, the entire temporal series becomes impossible (yabtulu kulluhu). Therefore, the world must have had a beginning and is created.

Key Terms
توهم
وقت
أولية
حدث

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument is psychological, based on what we can imagine.
Correction: It is logical: an infinite past is impossible because any past moment can be succeeded by another, leading to contradiction.

Checkpoint
What is the consequence of not setting a beginning for time, according to Maturidi?
A) Time becomes infinite.
B) The whole becomes invalid.
C) The world persists forever.
D) Time is cyclical.

Key Takeaway
The impossibility of an infinite past, shown by the ability to always imagine a later moment, proves the world was created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If every moment of your life is contingent and could have been followed by another but not infinitely, what does that say about the urgency of your purpose? Are you living as if your timeline has a definite start and end?
?
The argument shows that without a beginning, the whole series becomes invalid. In your own life, if you ignore the ultimate beginning (your creation and purpose), do your daily actions risk becoming hollow? How can you reconnect with that beginning today?
?
You can always imagine a later time, but that doesn't mean you have it. If this very moment is the only one guaranteed, what is one thing you would change in your relationship with your Creator immediately?

The Argument from Inability to be Devoid of Accidents
Maturidi argues that if the world could be devoid of accidents, rational principles would collapse.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's proof that the world's inability to be free of accidents demonstrates its createdness.

Core Idea
If the world could lack accidents, reason itself would break down.

Maturidi presents a concise but powerful argument for the temporal creation of the world. He uses a reductio ad absurdum: if the world could exist without accidents, then rational distinctions like life and death could coexist. Since that is impossible, the world must always have accidents and therefore be created. This passage lays out the core logic in just a few lines.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once without analyzing. Let the argument flow naturally.
Notice the conditional structure: 'if... then...'
2
Identify the absurd consequence
Maturidi claims that if the world could be empty of accidents, then 'every rational thing could be reversed'. What example does he give?
The example is a living thing being dead at the same time.
3
Connect to the conclusion
Understand why the impossibility of that consequence leads to the conclusion that the world must have accidents and thus be created.
The phrase 'bima la yakhlu 'anhu' is key: the world is established as created by that which it cannot be devoid of.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإنه لو جاز إخلاء العالم أو أصله عما يُحتمل من الحوادث لجاز أيضا قَلْبُ كل معقول من جواز حي ميت في حال، فثبت حدث الكلية بما لا يخلو عنه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
And after that, if it were possible to empty the world or its origin of what it can bear of accidents, then it would also be possible to reverse every rational thing, such as the permissibility of a living thing being dead at one time. So the createdness of the whole is established by that which it cannot be devoid of. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage presents a classic kalam argument for the world's createdness: the world cannot be devoid of accidents (hawadith), and anything that cannot be devoid of accidents must itself be created (hadith). Maturidi argues by reductio: if the world could exist without any accidents, then rational distinctions would break down—e.g., something could be both alive and dead simultaneously. Since that is absurd, the world must always have accidents, and therefore it is temporal. The phrase 'bima la yakhlu 'anhu' (by that which it cannot be devoid of) concludes the proof.

Key Terms
إخلاء
حوادث
قلب معقول
حدث الكلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking that this argument refers only to physical accidents like earthquakes or storms.
Correction: The term 'accidents' (hawadith) in kalam includes all non-essential attributes that come into existence, such as motion, rest, life, etc. The argument is about metaphysical dependence: anything that cannot exist without temporal attributes must itself be temporal.

Checkpoint
What absurd consequence does Maturidi say would follow if the world could be empty of accidents?
A) The world would cease to exist.
B) A living thing could be dead at the same time.
C) Time would stop.
D) The world would become eternal.

Key Takeaway
The world's inability to be free of accidents proves its createdness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the world's inability to be without accidents proves its dependence on a creator, why do you live as if the world is self-sufficient? What would change if you truly recognized every moment as a created accident?
?
The argument shows that rational categories would collapse if the world were accident-free. Are there areas in your life where you try to be 'free of accidents'—where you deny your dependence on Allah? How does this argument expose your pride?
?
Maturidi ends with 'la quwwata illa billah.' Do you internalize that there is no power except Allah in your daily struggles, or do you rely on your own strength? What is one situation where you need to say this with conviction?

Proof of the World's Createdness from Life and Death
Maturidi presents a proof from the impossibility of life in the dead.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the observable phenomena of life and death to argue for the world's createdness.

Core Idea
Life and death are temporal events, so the world containing them must be created.

Maturidi presents a concise argument from the common human experience of life and death. He observes that life cannot exist in a dead thing, proving life itself is a created accident. Similarly, death comes after life, so it too is created. Since the world contains these contingent events, the world itself must be created. This lesson unpacks this brief but powerful proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once and then the literal translation. Let the author's own words set the stage.
2
Understand the core logical move
Identify Maturidi's key insight: life cannot exist in a dead thing, so life must be a created accident. Ask yourself why this is a logical necessity.
Life is an accident that inheres in a subject; a dead subject cannot host life.
3
Apply the analogy to death and the world
Notice that death is also temporal (it occurs after life). Therefore both life and death are created. Conclude that the world, which contains these temporal events, must itself be created.
This is the final step in the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودليل حدث العالم إحالة كون حياة في ميت لأنه بها يحيى. ثبت أن حياة الأشياء حدث، فكذلك موتها، إذ قد يكون بعد الحياة.
And the proof of the world's createdness is the impossibility of the existence of life in a dead thing, because it lives by it. It is established that the life of things is created, and likewise their death, since it may occur after life.
Source pages p. 95

Commentary
This passage provides a concise proof for the createdness (huduth) of the world. Maturidi states that it is impossible (ihala) for life to exist in a dead thing, because a dead thing lives by life (i.e., life is an accident that brings life). Since life is a property that comes and goes, it is created (hadith). Similarly, death occurs after life, so it is also created. Since the world contains these temporal phenomena, the world itself must be created. The argument is based on the observation that life and death are events that begin and end.

Key Terms
حياة
موت
حدث
دليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think this is a biological argument about life appearing spontaneously.
Correction: Maturidi is not discussing biology; he is using the logical principle that life is an accident that cannot inhere in a non-living subject, so it must be created.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say life must be created?
A) Because it is eternal.
B) Because it cannot exist in a dead thing.
C) Because it is invisible.
D) Because it is a substance.

Key Takeaway
Maturidi shows that life and death are created temporal events, proving that the world which contains them is also created.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that life is a created gift from Allah, not something you own. How does this truth change what you will do with the next hour? What specific action will you take to use that time for His sake?
?
Death is a created event that will surely come to you. If you truly believed it could happen today, what one thing would you immediately change in your life? Commit to doing that now.
?
The proof shows that life cannot arise from dead matter. Your own existence screams that you have a Creator. What does it say about your relationship with Him that you often ignore this sign? What will you do today to acknowledge Him as the Giver of life?

Refutation of Imagination of Pre-eternity for Temporal Things
Maturidi argues that imagining temporal things as eternal does not make them eternal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that imagining temporal things as eternal does not make them eternal, and how Maturidi uses a reductio argument to prove the world's createdness.

Core Idea
Imagination cannot grant reality; temporal things cannot be eternal.

Maturidi addresses a subtle objection: if we can imagine a temporal thing as eternal, doesn't that imply the world could be eternal? In this lesson, we will see his clear response. He shows that mere imagination does not confer actual eternity, and that the impossibility of imagining a temporal thing as eternal leads to the conclusion that the world must have a beginning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic source passage once, then read the literal English translation. Let the author's argument unfold without interruption.
Notice that the passage is concise but contains a full logical argument.
2
Identify the examples Maturidi gives
Maturidi lists several temporal things that can be imagined as eternal: an old man, a newborn, a person arriving at a place, motion, rest, separation, and union. Identify these and note that they are all created things.
3
Trace the logical argument
Focus on the conditional statement: 'If you say: that is impossible, then similarly the existence of the created in pre-eternity is impossible.' This is a reductio ad absurdum. The opponent must accept that imagining a temporal thing as eternal is impossible, therefore the world cannot be eternal.
This is the key move: the same impossibility applies to the world as a whole.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما ذُكر من التوهم فإنه قد يُتوهّم في كل شيخ في أوّل ما شاخ بقدمه، وفي كل مولود بقدمه ، وفي كل من أتى مكانًا بقدمه ، ولم يجب به الوصف في الأزل، وكذا في كل حركة وسكون وتفرق واجتماع. فإن قلت: ذَا محال، فمثله كون الحدث في الأزل محال. والله الموفق.
And what is mentioned of imagination: it is possible to imagine in every old man, at the beginning of his old age, [that he is] with his eternity, and in every newborn with his eternity, and in everyone who came to a place with his eternity, but the attribute of pre-eternity is not necessitated by that. Likewise in every motion and rest, separation and union. If you say: that is impossible, then similarly the existence of the created in pre-eternity is impossible. And Allah is the granter of success.
Source pages p. 96

Commentary
This passage responds to an objection about imagination (tawahhum) being used to argue for the world's eternity. The opponent might claim that we can imagine temporal things as if they have always existed (e.g., an old man at the start of his old age imagined as eternal). Maturidi says such imagination does not actually make them eternal; the attribute of pre-eternity (azal) does not apply. Similarly, one can imagine motion, rest, etc., as eternal, but that is impossible because they are temporal by nature. The argument is a reductio: if you say imagining a temporal thing as eternal is impossible (as it is), then the same applies to the world—its existence in pre-eternity is impossible. Therefore, the world must have a beginning.

Key Terms
توهم
قدم
أزل
محال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Imagining something as eternal could be evidence for its eternity.
Correction: Imagination does not confer actual attributes; the createdness of the world is proven by rational argument, not by subjective imaginings.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say about imagining an old man as eternal?
A) That would make him eternal.
B) It does not necessitate the attribute of pre-eternity.
C) It proves time is infinite.
D) It shows the world is eternal.

Key Takeaway
Imagining a temporal thing as eternal does not make it eternal; the world's pre-eternity is impossible, so it must have a beginning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever imagined a personal weakness or sin as a permanent part of you, as if it has always been there and always will? How does this lesson's truth—that imagination does not make something eternal—force you to see that your struggle can change? What one step will you take this week to act against that false permanence?
?
Consider a belief you hold that you have never questioned, something you treat as self-evident. Are you confusing the fact that you can imagine it as always true with it actually being eternal? What would it cost you to test that belief against reason, as Maturidi tested the world's eternity?
?
If you find yourself assuming that your current state—whether good or bad—is unchangeable, are you committing the same error as those who imagine the world as eternal? What is one specific area of your life where you must now reject that assumption and actively seek change, knowing that only Allah is eternal?

Proofs from Good and Evil and the Nature of Mixing
Maturidi presents two proofs for tawheed from good/evil mixture and analysis of mixing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's two arguments for tawheed based on the mixture of good and evil and the nature of mixing, and how they refute dualism.

Core Idea
The mixture of good and evil in everything proves a single creator.

Maturidi provides two powerful arguments that show the impossibility of two separate sources for good and evil. He first observes that nothing is purely good or purely evil, then analyzes the nature of mixing itself. This lesson unpacks these arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the two proofs
The passage contains two distinct arguments. The first is based on the empirical observation that nothing is purely good or purely evil. The second is a logical analysis of mixing itself. Find each one and notice how they are presented.
3
Understand the refutation of dualism
Ask yourself how each argument specifically refutes the claim that good and evil come from two separate sources (light and darkness). The commentary will help you connect the dots.
This directly undermines Dualist doctrines.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومما يبيّن أنهما فعل الواحدٍ ما ليس في العالم شيء بجوهره خير حتى لا يكون منه شر في وجه أبدا، ولا شر حتى لا يكون منه خير في وجه أبدا ؛ ثبت أن إنكار مثله عن الواحد غير ممكن. ثم الأصل أن الامتزاج لا يخلو من أن يكون شرًا أو خيرا. فان كان خيرا لا يخلو من يكون من الظلمة، فيكون منها الخير وبطل قولهم بالاثنين من حيث لا يكون من الشر خير ولا من الخير شر؛ وإن كان شراً فقد شاركه الخير في القبول فصار شراً؛ وإن كان ذلك من النور فالوجهان قائمان فيه .
And what makes clear that they are both the act of the One is that there is nothing in the world which is by its substance purely good such that no evil ever comes from it, nor purely evil such that no good ever comes from it; it is established that denial of the like of this from the One is not possible. Then the principle is that mixing is not free from being either evil or good. If it is good, it cannot be free from being from darkness, so good would come from it, and their claim of dualism is nullified, since from evil no good comes and from good no evil comes; and if it is evil, then good has participated in its acceptance so it becomes evil; and if that is from light, then both aspects are present in it.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage provides two arguments for the unity of the creator. First, Maturidi observes that nothing in the world is purely good (such that no evil ever proceeds from it) or purely evil (such that no good ever proceeds from it). This mixture of good and evil in everything proves they cannot come from two separate substances, so denying that they come from one creator is impossible. Second, he analyzes the nature of mixing (امتزاج) itself: mixing must be either good or evil. If it is good, it must originate from darkness (since they claim darkness is evil), but then good would come from evil, contradicting their principle. If it is evil, then good (light) must have participated in it, so again the separation fails. If mixing comes from light, then both good and evil are present in light. Thus their dualism collapses.

Key Terms
جوهر
خير
شر
امتزاج
وجه
قول بالاثنين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Good and evil come from two separate, independent sources (e.g., light and darkness).
Correction: Maturidi shows that nothing is purely good or purely evil, and that mixing itself cannot be neatly attributed to one source, thereby disproving dualism.

Checkpoint

A) That good and evil are illusions
B) That the world must have two creators
C) That denying the one creator is impossible
D) That mixing is impossible

Key Takeaway
Maturidi uses the empirical observation of good and evil mixed in everything to demonstrate that they must come from one creator, not two.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your own character, do you find pure traits ever? How does the fact that nothing in you is purely good or evil challenge your pride or despair? In which area do you need to acknowledge that even your good deeds are mixed with Allah's mercy?
?
If your trials (evil) this week came from Allah alone, how does that change your response? Can you see His wisdom in the mixture? What would it mean to trust that He is one even in your pain?
?
Dualism isn't just ancient—if you blame shaytan for your sins more than yourself, you're acting like evil comes from a separate source. How can you take responsibility for your mixed nature and run back to the One Creator who holds both good and evil in His hand?

Two Proofs for Calling God 'Thing': Revelation and Reason
Maturidi provides scriptural and rational proofs for using 'thing' of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scriptural and rational basis for affirming that God is called 'thing' and why this refutes the denial of God's attributes.

Core Idea
Calling God 'thing' affirms His existence and negates atheism.

Maturidi begins by establishing that the term 'thing' can be applied to God, using both Quranic evidence and rational argument. This might seem like a mere semantic point, but it carries heavy theological weight: it affirms God's real existence and rejects those who deny His attributes. Let's examine his two proofs one by one.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the two-part structure: 'a' for revelation and 'b' for reason.
The passage is concise and uses technical terms like 'سمع' and 'عقل'.
2
Analyse the scriptural proof
Identify the two Quranic verses Maturidi cites and understand how each one implies that God is a 'thing'. Reflect on the logic: if God were not a thing, the verses would be meaningless.
'There is nothing like Him' (42:11) and 'Say: What thing is greatest in testimony? Say: God is a witness' (6:19).
3
Examine the rational proof
Understand Maturidi's linguistic argument: 'thingness' is a term of affirmation, while 'nothing' is negation. Calling God 'thing' thus affirms His existence and refutes ta'til (denial of attributes).
Note that 'thing' in Arabic can also mean 'something' (affirmation) or be used for diminution, but here it is clearly affirmative.
4
Connect to broader theology
Consider why this matters: establishing that God is a 'thing' safeguards His real existence and attributes against those who would strip them away. This is a foundational point in Maturidi's defense of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ودليل إثبات القول بـ «الشيء» وجهان: أ - أحدهما السمع من قوله: {ليس كمثله شيء} [الشورى، 11/42]، ولو لم يكن هو شيئًا لم ينف عنه شيئية الأشياء باسم الشيئية؛ إذ الشيء في التحقيق خلاف ما لا يحتمل القول بالشيء. وكذلك قوله: {قل أي شيء أكبر شهادة قل الله شهيد} [الأنعام، 19/6]، فلو لم يكن يقع عليه اسم الشيء لكان لا يحتمل تضمنه ذلك القول حتى يُنسب إليه . بــ وأما العقل فهو أن الشيئية اسم الإثبات لا غيرُ في العُرْف، إذ القول بـ «لا شيء» نفي إذا لم يُرَد به يُرَدُّ به التصغير ، فثبت أنه اسم الإثبات ونفي التعطيل.
The proof for establishing the saying 'thing' has two aspects: (a) One is revelation from His saying: 'There is nothing like Him' (42:11), and if He were not a thing, He would not have negated the thingness of things by the name of thingness; for the thing in reality is other than what does not admit the saying of thing. And also His saying: 'Say: "What thing is greatest in testimony?" Say: "God is a witness"' (6:19), so if the name 'thing' did not apply to Him, it would not be possible for that saying to be contained so as to be attributed to Him. (b) As for reason: thingness is a name of affirmation only in convention, since the saying 'nothing' is negation if it is not intended to denote diminution, so it is established that it is a name of affirmation and negation of ta'til.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage provides two proofs for applying the term 'thing' to God. The first is from the Quran, where God says 'There is nothing like Him' – implying that He is a thing, otherwise the negation would be meaningless. Also 'Say: "What thing is greatest in testimony?"' shows that God is called a 'thing'. The second proof is rational: 'thingness' is a term of affirmation, meaning existence, as opposed to 'nothing' which is negation. Therefore, calling God a 'thing' affirms His existence and negates ta'til (denial of attributes).

Key Terms
سمع
عقل
شيئية
إثبات
تعطيل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that calling God a 'thing' is anthropomorphic or implies He is like created objects.
Correction: Maturidi shows it is scripturally commanded (Quran uses 'thing' of God) and rationally necessary to affirm His existence. It does not imply similarity, as the same verse says 'there is nothing like Him'.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the two proofs Maturidi gives for calling God a 'thing'?
A) Revelation from the Quran
B) Rational analysis of the term 'thingness'
C) Consensus of the scholars
D) Use of the word 'thing' in Quranic verses

Key Takeaway
Calling God a 'thing' is established by both Quran and reason; it affirms His existence and refutes the denial of His attributes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly affirm that Allah exists as a 'thing' in your heart, or do you sometimes act as if He is just an abstract concept? What would change if you saw Him as the most real Reality?
?
The rational proof shows that 'thing' means affirmation. Is your faith merely a verbal affirmation, or does it compel you to live as if Allah is the only true 'Thing'? What would it look like to treat Allah's existence as the most certain fact of your life?
?
Maturidi refutes ta'til (denial). Are there areas in your life where you effectively deny Allah's attributes—by forgetting He sees, hears, or knows? How can you let this verse remind you that He is a 'thing' more real than anything you perceive?

Using 'Hastiyya' and Affirming Existence
Maturidi advises using 'hastiyya' and contrasts 'thing' with 'body'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi recommends using 'hastiyya' for clarity and how the term 'thing' affirms God's existence and greatness without implying quiddity or corporeality.

Core Idea
The word 'thing' for God affirms existence and majesty, not essence or body.

When speaking about God, precision is vital. Maturidi addresses a practical challenge: some people might misunderstand the Arabic word 'thing' (shay') as implying something definite or corporal. To avoid confusion, he suggests using the Persian term 'hastiyya' (existence) for clarity. This passage explains that 'thing' only affirms existence and greatness, not quiddity, contrasting it with terms like 'body' that specify dimensions and accidents.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Start by reading the Arabic passage once and then the English translation. Notice the flow: Maturidi moves from practical advice to conceptual distinctions.
Pay attention to the contrast between 'thing' and 'body'.
2
Understand the reason for 'hastiyya'
Identify why Maturidi recommends using 'hastiyya'—it is clearer in affirming existence, especially for those who might misunderstand 'thing'.
3
Grasp the meaning of 'thing'
Maturidi explains that 'thing' affirms the essence (dhat) and its magnification (ta'zim), not quiddity. Note how it is contrasted with 'body' which specifies dimensions and accidents.
4
Apply the distinction to attributes
The same logic applies to attributes: saying 'not knower' negates majesty, while 'knower' affirms it without specifying quiddity.
See how this reinforces that divine names are about praise, not definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن كان قوم لا يعرفون أن معنى «الشيء» [هو ] الإثبات والخروج من التعطيل يتقي عن ذلك بينهم كراهة أن يعتقد قلوبهم معنى مكروها، ويقول بالهستية ، فانه أوضح في معنى الإثبات، وإن كانا واحدا عند أهل العلم بهذا اللسان. مع ما كان القول بـ «لا شيء» يستعمل في نفي الحقيقة أو تصغير الثابت، [۲۰] فثبت أن القول بـ «الشيء» إنما هو في إثبات الذات ،وتعظيمه / والله حقيق لذلك. والقول لا بـ لا جسم يوجب واحدا منهما؛ فكذلك القول بـ «الجسم» ليس فيه تثبيت واحد مما يُحمد وجوده أو يُعظَّم؛ لذلك اختلفا. وعلى ذلك القولُ بـ«لا عالم ولا قادر» اسم ينفي العظمة والجلال، فمثله في العالم والقادر إيجاب الوصف بالعظمة والجلال. وبالله التوفيق. فإنه في الشاهد لا يُفهم من قول الرجل «شيء» مائية الذات، ولا من قوله «عالم وقادر»" ، وإنما يفهم من الأول الوجود والهستيّة، ومن الثاني أنه موصوف بالصفة ، لا أنّ فيه بيان مائية الذات، كقول الرجل «جسم»، إنه ذكر مائيته [من] أنه ذو أبعاد أو ذو جهات أو محتمل للنهايات وقابل للأعراض ، وكذا ذا في الإنسان» وسائر الأعيان ولا قوة إلا بالله .
If there are people who do not know that the meaning of 'thing' is affirmation and emergence from ta'til, one should be cautious about that among them, out of dislike that their hearts might believe a disliked meaning, and one should say 'hastiyya' (existence), for it is clearer in the meaning of affirmation, even if they are the same according to the people of knowledge of this language. Along with the fact that the saying 'nothing' is used for negation of reality or belittling of the established, so it is established that the saying 'thing' is only for the affirmation of the essence and its magnification, and God is worthy of that. And the saying 'not a body' does not necessitate either of them; likewise the saying 'body' does not contain establishment of anything that is praised for its existence or magnified; therefore they differ. And upon that, the saying 'not knower nor powerful' is a name that negates greatness and majesty, so its opposite in 'knower' and 'powerful' is the affirmation of the description with greatness and majesty. And with Allah is success. For in the witnessed realm, one does not understand from the statement of a man 'thing' the quiddity of the essence, nor from his statement 'knower and powerful'; rather, from the first one understands existence and hastiyya, and from the second one understands that he is described with the attribute, not that there is a clarification of the quiddity of the essence, like the statement of a man 'body', for it mentions his quiddity as being possessed of dimensions or directions or susceptible to ends and capable of receiving accidents, and likewise in 'human' and all entities. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage discusses a practical issue: when people might misunderstand 'thing' as implying corporeality, Maturidi suggests using the Persian word 'hastiyya' (existence) for clarity. He emphasizes that 'thing' affirms the existence and greatness of God, whereas 'body' does not convey praise or majesty. He contrasts 'not a body' with 'body' and shows that claiming God is 'not a knower' negates majesty, while calling Him 'knower' affirms it. In everyday language, saying 'thing' does not specify quiddity (essence) but only existence, while saying 'body' specifies dimensions and accidents. Thus, applying 'thing' to God is safe and affirms His existence without defining His essence.

Key Terms
هستية
ذات
تعظيم
أبعاد
جهات
نهايات
أعراض

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that calling God 'thing' implies He has a quiddity or body.
Correction: Maturidi clarifies that 'thing' only affirms existence and greatness, not quiddity. It does not specify dimensions or accidents like 'body' does.

Checkpoint

A) Because 'hastiyya' means 'nothing'
B) Because 'hastiyya' is clearer in affirming existence for those unfamiliar with the technical meaning of 'thing'
C) Because 'thing' implies corporeality
D) Because 'hastiyya' specifies quiddity

Key Takeaway
Maturidi teaches that calling God 'thing' is permissible as it affirms existence and majesty without implying quiddity or corporeality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you refer to Allah using terms like 'the Knower' or 'the Powerful', do you stop at the mere affirmation, or does it stir a genuine sense of reverence and accountability in your daily actions? How can you move from mental acknowledgment to heartfelt magnification?
?
This lesson warns that even stating 'Allah is not a body' does not necessarily affirm His majesty. Have you ever settled for merely negating false ideas about Allah without actively magnifying His greatness in your heart and worship? What steps can you take today to ensure your words about Allah truly glorify Him?
?
Maturidi advises using clearer terms like 'hastiyya' to avoid misunderstandings. Think of your own speech about Allah: are there phrases you use that might inadvertently imply a limited or diminished view of Him? What change can you make to speak of Allah with the awe and clarity He deserves?

Proof of Divine Choice from Consistency of Action
Maturidi presents a concise syllogism proving divine choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the world's consistent order demonstrates that God acts by choice, not by necessity.

Core Idea
Consistent order without corruption proves voluntary action.

Imam Maturidi presents a powerful and concise argument for divine choice. By observing that the world continues in a consistent manner without corruption or deviation from wisdom, we can infer that the Creator acts voluntarily, not by compulsion or nature. This lesson unpacks that brief but profound proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice how concise and packed the argument is.
The passage is a single syllogism; every word matters.
2
Identify the premises
Identify the two key premises: (1) the world's action is consistent and without corruption, (2) such consistency proves the agent acts by choice.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
Consider the conclusion: creation is truly by God's action. Ask yourself how consistency implies volition.
4
Address the misunderstanding
Some may think order could come from natural necessity. The commentary explains why consistency actually shows choice.
Check the misconception section below.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن اتساق الفعل المتوالى - بلا فساد يظهر ، ولا خروج عن طريق الحكمة - يثبت كون المفعول بالاختيار من الفاعل فثبت أن الخلق كان بفعله حقيقة. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And also, the consistency of continuous action, without apparent corruption or departure from the path of wisdom, proves that the effect is by choice from the agent. Thus it is established that creation was truly by His action. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This short proof argues that the world's order and consistency without corruption indicates that the Creator acts by choice (ikhtiyar), not by necessity or nature. This is a concise syllogism: continuous flawless order implies a voluntary agent.

Key Terms

اختيار
حكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think the world's order results from natural necessity, without a choosing agent.
Correction: The text argues that consistency without corruption or deviation from wisdom specifically indicates volition, not necessity.

Checkpoint

A) That He is eternal
B) That He acts by choice
C) That He is powerful
D) That He is one

Key Takeaway
The world's consistent order, free from corruption, proves that God acts by choice, not by nature or necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If the consistency of creation proves God's voluntary action, then every blessing you receive is a direct result of His deliberate choice for you. How does this truth change your response to the next small blessing you receive? Commit now to acknowledge one specific blessing today as an act of His choice, and thank Him for it in that moment.

Proof of Divine Choice from Creation, Annihilation, and Recreation
Maturidi uses the cycle of day and night to prove God's action is by choice.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the observable cycle of day and night demonstrates that Allah's actions are volitional and not compelled by nature.

Core Idea
The reversibility of creation proves God's action is by choice, not nature.

Maturidi presents a concise argument from everyday experience. He uses the alternation of day and night, a phenomenon we witness constantly, to show that the one who creates and annihilates must be acting by choice. This lesson unpacks that reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Maturidi uses a very compressed style here.
2
Identify the key concepts
Look for the terms إنشاء (creation), إفناء (annihilation), and إعادة (restoration). These are the three stages Maturidi uses to build his proof.
Also note the word طبع (nature) which appears in the refutation.
3
Understand the argument
Maturidi argues that because we see things being brought into existence, then annihilated, then restored (like day and night), the agent must be acting by choice. A natural process cannot reverse itself like this.
Ask yourself: Why can't nature do this? Nature always produces the same effect. Only a volitional agent can choose to annihilate and recreate.
4
Reflect on the implication
The conclusion is that God's actions are voluntary, not necessary. This distinguishes Islamic theology from views that see creation as a necessary emanation.
5
Check your understanding
Answer the checkpoint question to ensure you have grasped the key point of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن الله تعالى إذ أنشأ غير شيء واحد ثم أفناه، وفيه أيضا ما قد أعاده نحو الليل والنهار، ثبت أن فعله بالاختيار ؛ اذ تحقق به إصلاح ما قد أفسده، وإعادة ما قد أفناه، وإيجاد المعدوم، وإعدام الموجود، فثبت أن طريق ذلك هو الاختيار؛ إذ من كان الذي منه يكون بالطبع لا يجئ منه نفي ما يوجد ، وإيجاد ما يعدمه. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
And also, when Allah created more than one thing, then annihilated it, and in it also that which He restored, like night and day, it is established that His action is by choice, because He is able to repair what He corrupted, re-create what He annihilated, bring the non-existent into existence, and annihilate the existent. It is established that the way of that is choice; because one whose action is by nature cannot negate what exists nor bring into existence what He annihilates. And there is no power except with Allah.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This proof uses the observable alternation of day and night and the cycle of creation and annihilation to show that God's actions are volitional, not natural. Nature cannot reverse its effects; only a volitional agent can.

Key Terms
إنشاء
إفناء
إعادة
طبع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The cycle of day and night is a natural automatic process that does not require a volitional agent.
Correction: Maturidi shows that the ability to annihilate and recreate (like night and day) cannot come from a natural process, because nature cannot reverse its own effects. This proves the agent acts by choice.

Checkpoint
What phenomenon does this passage use to prove God's action is by choice?
A) The consistency of the world
B) The alternation of night and day
C) The creation of the world from nothing
D) The attributes of God

Key Takeaway
The alternation of day and night proves that God acts by choice, not by nature, because nature cannot annihilate and recreate.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
When you experience a setback or a 'corruption' in your life, do you trust that God can repair and recreate it as He does with night and day, or do you view your situation as irreversible? How does your view of God's choice affect your response to difficulties?
?
If God's action is by choice, not nature, then your existence right now is not a necessary outcome of past causes but a voluntary act of God. Does this make you feel more accountable to Him? What is one way you can live today that reflects this truth?

Proof of Divine Choice from Creation from Nothing
Maturidi argues that creatio ex nihilo necessitates a willful agent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why creation from nothing (creatio ex nihilo) proves that God acts by choice, not by nature.

Core Idea
Creation from nothing demands a willful agent, not a natural one.

Imam Maturidi presents a concise logical proof that the very act of creating the world from nothing cannot be attributed to a natural process. In this lesson, we will unpack his reasoning and see why nature implies compulsion, while true creation requires perfect choice.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let Maturidi's own words set the argument.
2
Identify the main claim
Notice that the passage states creation from nothing is a type that can only be achieved by an agent with utmost choice (ikhtiyār). This is the core claim.
The phrase 'fi ghāyat ma'nā al-ikhtiyār' emphasizes perfect will.
3
Contrast with natural action
The text argues that what occurs by nature (bi al-ṭab‘) is subject to compulsion (iḍṭirār) and dependence, which are signs of weakness and temporality. Therefore a Creator who creates from nothing cannot be acting by nature.
This contrast is key: nature implies limitation, but creation from nothing requires absolute power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إنا قد بينا حدث العالم لا من شيء، وذلك نوع ما لا يبلغه إلا فعل مَن هو في غاية معنى الاختيار. وما يكون بالطبع فحقه الاضطرار، ومحال أن يكون من يبلغ شأنه إلى إنشاء الأشياء لا من شيء، ثم يكون ذلك بالطبع مع ما كان وقوع الشيء بالطبع هو تحت قهرِ آخر، وجَعلَه بحيث يسقط عنه الإمكان، وذلك آية الحدث وأمارة الضعف، جَلّ ربّنا عن
And also, we have already proven the temporal origination of the world from nothing. That is a type that can only be reached by the action of one who is in the utmost sense of choice. And that which is by nature is subject to compulsion, and it is impossible that one whose status reaches to creating things from nothing should then be by nature, especially since the occurrence of something by nature is under the coercion of another, and makes it such that possibility is removed from it. That is a sign of temporality and a mark of weakness. Exalted is our Lord above
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This proof argues that creating from nothing (creatio ex nihilo) requires a willful agent, not a natural one. Nature implies compulsion and dependency, which contradicts the absolute power required for such creation. The passage ends with an exaltation of God above such weakness.

Key Terms
حدث العالم
لا من شيء
اضطرار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Creation from nothing could be an act of nature, like a natural force producing existence.
Correction: The passage shows that nature implies compulsion and dependence, but creation from nothing requires absolute will and power, which nature lacks.

Checkpoint
Why does creating from nothing prove that God's action is by choice?
A) Because nature is weaker
B) Because nature implies compulsion, but creation from nothing requires perfect will
C) Because it is a miracle
D) Because the Quran says so

Key Takeaway
Creating from nothing is a proof that God's action is by perfect choice, not by nature.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?

The Argument from Creation: Temporality, Originator, and Oneness
Maturidi argues that creation's inherent indications prove divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the very act of creation itself indicates its own temporality, the existence of an originator, and the oneness of that originator, and how this demonstrates divine knowledge.

Core Idea
Creation's design points directly to its Creator's oneness and knowledge.

Maturidi presents a concise yet profound argument: the fact that creation exists in such a way that it clearly points to its own contingency and to its Creator's attributes is itself evidence that the Creator created with knowledge. This lesson unpacks that argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once without analyzing, then read the translation. Notice the compact style.
2
Identify the three indications
The passage states that creation indicates (1) its own temporality (ḥudūth), (2) that it has an originator (muḥdith), and (3) the oneness (waḥdāniyya) of that originator. Underline these three points.
3
Understand the conditional statement
After listing the indications, Maturidi adds a conditional: were it not for God's knowledge, creation would not come forth in this manner. This ties the argument back to divine knowledge.
The phrase 'فلولا أن علم بالخلق' is key.
4
Reflect on the logical flow
Trace how creation's features lead to the conclusion that God knows what He creates. This is a deeper proof than mere existence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إن الله سبحانه خلق الخلق خلقا دل على حدثه، وعلى أن له محدثا، وعلى وحدانية محدثه؛ فلولا أن علم بالخلق - يعلم أنه إذا خلق على ما خلق كان فيه دليل العلم به وبخلقه - لا يحتمل أن يخرج على ذلك خلقه. وبالله التوفيق. وعلى ذلك مجيئ الرسل بالأمر الذي لو اتبعوا وعملوا بما جاءوا به ما احتمل الخلاف ولا التفرق ولا الفساد؛ فلولا علمه أمكنة متفرقة.
And also, Allah the Glorified created the creation a creation that indicates its temporality, and that it has an originator, and the oneness of its Originator; were it not that He knows by creation—He knows that when He creates as He created, there is in it evidence of knowledge of Him and of His creation—it would not be likely that His creation would come forth in that manner. And with Allah is success. And upon that is the coming of the messengers with the command which, if they followed and acted by what they brought, would not admit discord, nor division, nor corruption; were it not for His knowledge, they would be scattered places.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage presents two related arguments. The first is that the very act of creation (khalq) itself serves as evidence for its own temporal origination (ḥudūth), for the existence of an originator (muḥdith), and for the oneness (waḥdāniyya) of that Originator. Maturidi adds that had God not known that when He creates in this specific manner, the creation itself would contain evidence of knowledge of Him and of His creation, then it would not be plausible for creation to come forth in that way. In other words, the fact that creation so clearly points to its own contingency and its Creator's attributes proves that God's act of creation is based on knowledge. The second argument concerns the sending of messengers. The messengers brought a command (amr) that, if people followed and acted upon, would prevent discord (khilāf), division (tafarruq), and corruption (fasād). The fact that this message is so cohesive and universally beneficial indicates that it comes from One who knows human nature and what unites them; otherwise, the messengers would have been scattered and their teachings conflicting. Thus, both creation and revelation demonstrate divine knowledge.

Key Terms
حدث (ḥudūth)
محدث (muḥdith)
وحدانية (waḥdāniyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the argument from creation only proves existence, not attributes.
Correction: Maturidi shows it also proves knowledge and oneness.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, what three things does creation indicate directly?
A) Temporality, originator, oneness
B) Eternity, multiple gods, ignorance
C) Chance, chaos, multiplicity
D) Necessity, creator, power

Key Takeaway
Creation's very nature provides evidence not only for God's existence but also for His oneness and knowledge.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If creation itself is evidence of God's oneness and knowledge, why do you still doubt His presence in your life? What created thing have you been ignoring that points directly to Him? Will you pause today to reflect on one such sign and let it strengthen your faith?
?
Maturidi argues that creation could not have come forth as it did unless God knew it would serve as evidence. Does your life show that you truly believe this? Do you see every detail of your existence as a sign pointing back to its Creator, or do you dismiss it as mere coincidence? What would change if you lived each moment as a witness to His oneness?
?
The passage teaches that the order and unity of creation demand a single Originator. Yet your heart often divides its allegiance among multiple priorities, as if there were multiple creators. Are you living as though your life has one ultimate purpose, or are you pulled in many directions? How can you realign your actions to reflect the oneness you profess?

The Unity of the Prophetic Message: Evidence for Divine Knowledge
Maturidi argues that the unified prophetic message reveals divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the universal and consistent message of the prophets points to divine knowledge and planning.

Core Idea
The unified prophetic command that prevents discord proves it comes from the All-Knowing.

Maturidi extends his argument from the natural world to revelation. The fact that all prophets brought a command that, if followed, would eliminate discord and corruption, indicates that this message originates from One who knows human nature and unity. This lesson explores that argument.

Guided Path

Read the passage carefully

The word 'أمكنة' may mean 'places' or by extension 'diverse locations'.
4

The unity of the prophetic message across time and place is evidence that it comes from one source who knows what unites humanity.

Evidence for God's Choice: Wisdom in Creation
Maturidi presents the evidence for God's choice from the diversity of creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the diversity of created essences (ma'iyyat) serves as evidence for God's selective will (ikhtiyar) and oneness.

Core Idea
The variety of natures in creation cannot result from chance—it demands a wise chooser.

Maturidi presents a concise yet profound argument for God's choice (ikhtiyar) based on the observable diversity in the essential natures of created things. This diversity, far from being random, points to a purposeful selector who gives each thing its unique constitution. The same evidence also reinforces the oneness of God. Let us examine the passage closely.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once for its rhythm, then the literal translation. Note the key terms: 'ikhtiyar' (choice) and 'tafawut al-ma'iyya' (variation of quiddity).
The passage ends with a phrase of reliance on God.
2
Understand the logical flow
The argument moves from 'wisdom' to 'proof' to 'conclusion'. The proof is the variation itself; the conclusion is that God chose each thing to be as it is.
Maturidi says 'then the proof' – meaning wisdom is assumed first.
3
Connect to tawhid
Notice that the variation also indicates God's oneness – because multiple creators would produce chaos, not ordered diversity.
This refutes polytheism and dualism.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider a specific created thing (e.g., a bird, a tree) and ask: could it have been otherwise? Its specific nature points to a deliberate choice.
This strengthens the belief in God's wisdom.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكمة ثم الدلالة عندنا على الاختيار خروج الخلق على تفاوت مائيته، على ما فيه من والدلالة على وحدانية الله؛ فدل ذلك على اختيار كون كل شيء على ما هو عليه. ولا قوة إلا بالله.
Wisdom. Then the proof according to us for choice is the emergence of creation upon the variation of its quiddity, along with what is in it of evidence for the oneness of God. So that indicates choice of each thing being as it is. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage presents the proof for God's choice (ikhtiyar). The proof is the observable variation in the essential natures (ma'iyyat) of created things. The diverse forms, properties, and essences of creatures could not result from blind nature or chance; they point to a selective, wise cause that chooses each thing to be exactly what it is. This diversity also serves as evidence for God's oneness (tawhid). Thus, the fact that every creature has a specific, unique constitution indicates that God actively chose to create it that way, not that it necessarily had to be that way. The passage ends with a phrase of reliance on God.

Key Terms
اختيار
تفاوت المائية
وحدانية الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some argue that the diversity of created kinds suggests multiple creators, one for each kind.
Correction: Maturidi uses the very same diversity to prove oneness: the ordered, complementary variation points to a single wise chooser, not multiple conflicting agents.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

Refuting Natural and Astral Causes
Maturidi refutes natural and astral causation by showing their dependency on an ultimate first cause.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's refutation of naturalistic and astral explanations for creation and why they all require an uncaused first cause.

Core Idea
All explanations of creation that rely on finite causes ultimately require an uncaused cause.

Maturidi addresses several alternative theories that deny a wise, knowing Creator. Some attribute creation to natures and nutriments, others to stars and planets, others to celestial rotation, and others to hereditary management. He demonstrates that all these views reduce to a chain of causality that cannot stand without an ultimate origin. This lesson examines these false views and the decisive argument against them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the four views
Read the Arabic and translation, noting the four groups Maturidi mentions: naturalists, astrologers, astronomers, and materialists.
The passage enumerates them clearly.
2
Understand the common feature
Notice that Maturidi says all these views reduce to 'a thing coming from a thing' (شيء بشيء). This means they each explain one thing by another, without reaching a first cause.
This is the key observation.
3
Examine the two-part refutation
Maturidi presents a conditional argument: if a first cause is not posited, the chain is invalid; if a first cause is posited for each genus, that cause cannot be self-caused.
The phrase 'لما يوجد نفسه' is crucial: a cause cannot bring itself into existence.
4
Reflect on the implication
The only consistent alternative is an uncaused, transcendent Creator. Maturidi thus refutes all alternatives without explicitly naming God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من يقول بخروج الخلق على ما عليه بالطبائع والأغذية، ومن يقول: ذلك عمل النجم والشمس والقمر، ومن يقول: ذلك بدوران الفلك، ومن يقول في التوالد بتدبير والأمهات، يرجع كله إلى كون شيء بشيء. إذا لم يثبت له أولية يبطل بالأدلة التي مر ذكرها. وإذا ثبت له أو لكل جنس من ذلك أولية، استحال كونه بنفسه؛ لما يوجد نفسه.
Then whoever says that the emergence of creation as it is [is] by natures and nutriments, and whoever says that is the work of the star, the sun, and the moon, and whoever says that is by the rotation of the sphere, and whoever says in generation by the management of fathers and mothers, all of it reduces to a thing coming from a thing. If an ultimate origin (awwaliyya) is not established for it, it is invalidated by the proofs that have passed. And if an ultimate origin is established for it or for each genus of that, it is impossible for it to be by itself; because it would find itself [?].
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage refutes various alternative explanations for the existence and order of creation. Maturidi lists four groups: (1) those who attribute it to natures and nutriments (naturalists), (2) those who attribute it to the action of stars, sun, and moon (astrologers), (3) those who attribute it to the rotation of the celestial sphere (astronomers), and (4) those who attribute it to hereditary management of parents (materialists). He argues that all these views reduce to the same problem: they explain one thing by another thing, but they never arrive at an ultimate first cause (awwaliyya). Without such a first cause, the chain is invalidated by earlier proofs (presumably the proofs for God's existence). If one does posit a first cause for each genus (e.g., star as the cause of earthly events), then that first cause cannot be self-caused (since it would have to bring itself into existence, which is impossible). Therefore, the only consistent explanation is a transcendent Creator who is not caused by anything else.

Key Terms
طبائع
النجم
دوران الفلك
التوالد
أولية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Natural or astral causes can explain creation without needing a Creator.
Correction: Maturidi shows that these causes themselves require an ultimate origin that cannot be self-caused, pointing to a transcendent Creator.

Checkpoint
What common flaw does Maturidi identify in all alternative explanations of creation?
A) They deny the existence of God
B) They all rely on a first cause that cannot be self-caused
C) They are based on non-observable entities
D) They contradict the Qur'an

Key Takeaway
Maturidi's argument shows that any finite causal explanation inevitably leads to an uncaused first cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a success in your life, do you instinctively attribute it to your own efforts, your genetics, or the support of others, without pausing to ask: Who set these causes in motion? Will you now make it a habit to consciously trace every finite cause back to its ultimate source?
?
Look up at the night sky or think of the regular cycles of day and night. Do you perceive these as independent forces that simply operate on their own, or do they remind you of their dependence on a Creator? What would change in your daily awareness and worship if you saw every natural phenomenon as a sign pointing to its Maker?
?
The materialist view suggests that you are merely the product of your parents and environment—a random outcome of a chain with no ultimate purpose. Do you live as if your existence is accidental, or as if you are deliberately created by God? Which belief gives you a stronger sense of purpose and accountability, and what step can you take today to live more consistently with that belief?
