Diversity as Proof of Divine Choice
Maturidi presents the evidence for choice through the diversity of creation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the diversity of created essences (mahiyya) as evidence for God's deliberate choice (ikhtiyar) and oneness.

Core Idea
Diversity in creation's essences proves divine choice, not blind necessity.

In this lesson, we encounter Maturidi's concise argument for God's choice (ikhtiyar) in creation. He points to the fundamental diversity of created things — their distinct quiddities — as evidence that they are not the product of a necessary or random process, but of a deliberate, wise chooser. This diversity also serves as proof for God's oneness (tawhid).

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the translation. Notice the logical flow: 'The proof... is the emergence of creation in variation of its quiddity... so that indicated choice.'
The heading 'الحكمة' means 'Wisdom' — this argument is part of Maturidi's treatment of divine wisdom.
2
Understand the key term 'mahiyya'
Mahiyya (مائية) means 'quiddity' or 'whatness' — the essential nature or defining essence of a thing. Maturidi says things differ in their mahiyya, not just accidentally.
3
Connect diversity to choice
Why does variation imply choice? If creation were necessary or automatic, all things would be uniform. Diversity requires an agent who selects and specifies each thing's essence.
This is the core of the argument: choice is inferred from the fact that things could have been otherwise but are not.
4
Note the link to tawhid
The passage says the variation also contains proof for God's oneness. Coordinating such diverse essences into a coherent world indicates a single, wise creator, not multiple gods.
5
Reflect on the closing phrase
'And there is no power except through God' — this affirms that all agency ultimately belongs to Allah, reinforcing the theological framework.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكمة
ثم الدلالة عندنا على الاختيار خروج الخلق على تفاوت مائيته، على ما فيه من والدلالة على وحدانية الله ؛ فدل ذلك على اختيار كون كل شيء على ما هو عليه. ولا قوة إلا بالله .
Wisdom. Then the proof with us for choice is the emergence of creation in the variation of its quiddity (mahiyya), upon what it contains of the proof for God's oneness; so that indicated the choice of [determining] each thing to be as it is. And there is no power except through God.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This short passage presents Maturidi's own positive proof for God's choice (ikhtiyar) in creation. The evidence is the diversity and specific essences (mahiyya) of created things. The fact that things differ in their fundamental natures—not merely accidentally—shows that they are not the product of a blind necessity but of a deliberate choice that assigns each thing its particular quiddity. This diversity also serves as a proof for God's oneness (tawhid), implying that only a single, wise agent could coordinate such variety. The phrase 'there is no power except through God' reinforces the theological reliance on divine agency.

Key Terms
اختيار (ikhtiyar)
مائية (mahiyya)
وحدانية (wahdaniyya)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The diversity of creation only shows design, not choice; it can be explained by natural necessity.
Correction: Maturidi's argument specifically ties diversity to choice: the variety of quiddities cannot be explained by necessity because necessity would produce uniformity. The fact that things have distinct essences implies a chooser who determines each thing's nature.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi present as proof for God's choice?
A) The uniform nature of creation.
B) The variation in the quiddity of created things.
C) The existence of evil.
D) The consensus of philosophers.

Key Takeaway
The variation in the fundamental natures of created things is evidence that they are products of a deliberate divine choice, not blind necessity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the unique traits Allah gave you—your personality, abilities, circumstances. Do you truly believe they are divinely chosen for a purpose, or do you sometimes dismiss them as random? How would your life change if you lived with the certainty that every detail of your existence is intentionally selected by Allah? What one thing can you do today to acknowledge His choice in your life?
?
This lesson says that diversity in creation itself points to God's oneness. Yet we often focus on differences that divide us—racism, sectarianism, class. How does this proof challenge your own biases about people who are different from you? Will you commit to seeing diversity as a sign of Allah's wisdom rather than a cause for division?
?
Maturidi argues that the variety in quiddities proves Allah's choice, not necessity. Reflect on one area of your life where you feel trapped by circumstances—a difficulty you think is 'necessary.' How does this lesson free you to see that even your trials are chosen by Allah for a specific wisdom? What does that mean for your trust in Him?

The Impossibility of Self-Creation
Maturidi refutes self-creation through a logical dilemma.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why all alternative explanations for creation reduce to an infinite regress or a logical contradiction, and why a self-caused first cause is impossible.

Core Idea
Nothing can bring itself into existence – that is a logical contradiction.

Maturidi begins by listing common non-theistic explanations for the world's order. He then shows that they all share a fatal flaw: they either lead to an endless chain or require something to create itself, which is impossible. This lesson examines this foundational argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze. Then read the translation. Notice how Maturidi enumerates the false views.
2
Identify the two possibilities
The passage presents two logical possibilities after reducing all views to one thing causing another. Find them and understand the first cause dilemma.
The two possibilities are: no primacy (infinite regress) or primacy (self-creation).
3
Reflect on the self-creation dilemma
Understand why self-creation is impossible: a thing cannot create itself before it exists, and if it exists already, it doesn't need creation.
This is a classic kalam argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرد على منكري صانع حكيم عليم
ثم من يقول بخروج الخلق على ما عليه بالطبائع والأغذية، ومن يقول: ذلك عمل النجم والشمس والقمر، ومن يقول : ذلك بدوران الفَلَك ، ومن يقول في التوالد بتدبير الآباء والأمهات، يرجع كله إلى كون شيء بشيء. إذا لم يثبت له أوليَّة يبطل بالأدلة التي مرّ ذكرها.
وإذا ثبت له أو لكل جنس من ذلك أوليَّة، استحال كونه بنفسه؛ لما يُوجد نفسه : أ) إما حين عدمه، وذلك محال أن يوجد عديما ؛ مع ما إذ رجع إليه تدبير كل شيء لا يحتمل أن يبلغه العدم ؛ فإنه لو جاز أن يوجد نَفْسَهُ لجاز في ذلك الوقت أن يُعدمه، وذلك متناقض ; ب) أو بعد الوجود، فثبت الوجود بغيره. وبالله التوفيق.
وأيضاً إن جميع ما يُذكر إنما هو نوع الموات إلا من ذكر من الآباء والأمهات، مما يعلمون أن تدبيرهم لا يبلغ مبادئ الأولاد، وأنهم يكونون على غير ما يأملون، وأنهم لو فسدوا لا يملكون إصلاحهم، وأن وسعهم لا يبلغ ما استتر من الأشياء فضلاً من تقديرها هنالك، وغير ذلك من الوجوه التي تُبطل كون ذلك بهم. ونوع الموات غير عالم بما فيه من المنافع، ولا بالذي يُحتمل منهم منع ذلك. ثبت أن الذي يكون بالاغتذاء وبالطبائع إنما كان ذلك فيهم بجعل حكيم عليم فيها، وفي اللزوم بغير جعل كل شيء على ما عليه من النفع والضرر على أنه في الحيوان معان ليس في شيء مما وصف آثار ذلك من نحو السمع والبصر وبخاصة في البشر من نحو النطق والميز والوقوف على أشياء. مع ما كان ذلك في أول الأحوال غير كائن، ولا يربى إلا بأغذية الآباء والأمهات، ثم لم يؤثر ذلك فيهم، فكيف في الأولاد.
وبعد، فإن كل شيء له حد، إذا بلغ ذلك الحد لا يزداد له طول ولا عرض ولا سمع ولا بصر ولا عقل، بل يأخذ كل شيء من ذلك بالانتقاص، على قيام الأغذية ودوام التربية؛ عقل أن ذلك كذلك لا بما ذكر، ولكن بمن هو عالم بذاته حتى لا يعرب عنه شيء، قادر
Refutation of deniers of a wise, knowing Maker. Then whoever says that creation emerges as it is by natures (taba'i') and nutrients, and whoever says that is the work of the star, the sun, and the moon, and whoever says that is by the rotation of the sphere, and whoever says that in procreation it is by the management of fathers and mothers—all of it reduces to something being through something else. If no primacy (awwaliyya) is established for it, it is invalidated by the proofs that have been mentioned. And if primacy is established for it or for each kind of that, then it being by itself is impossible; because [if] it brings itself into existence: a) either at the time of its non-existence, and that is impossible for a non-existent to exist; considering that when the management of everything is referred back to it, it cannot be reached by non-existence; for if it were possible for it to bring itself into existence, then at that time it would be possible for it to annihilate itself, and that is contradictory. b) Or after existence, then existence is established by another. And success is from God. And also, all that is mentioned is only a type of inanimate (mawat) except for what is mentioned of fathers and mothers, of which they know that their management does not reach the beginnings of children, and that they come to be contrary to what they hope, and that if they become corrupt they cannot rectify them, and that their capacity does not reach what is hidden of things, let alone their predetermination there, and other aspects that invalidate that being through them. And the type of inanimate is not aware of the benefits in it, nor of what is likely to prevent them. It is established that what comes to be by nutrition and by natures—that occurs in them only by the making (ja'l) of a wise, knowing One in them, and in the necessity without a making of everything [being] as it is in benefit and harm, [considering] that in animals there are meanings that are not in anything that describes their effects, such as hearing and sight, and especially in humans such as speech, discernment, and comprehension of things. While that [these capacities] at the beginning of states were non-existent, and they do not grow except by the nutrients of fathers and mothers, yet that did not effect them; so how [would it effect] the children? Furthermore, everything has a limit; when it reaches that limit, it does not increase in length, width, hearing, sight, or intellect; rather, everything of that begins to diminish, despite the persistence of nutrients and continuity of upbringing; it is understood that this is so not by what was mentioned, but by One who knows by Himself so that nothing escapes Him, capable.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive refutation of all views that deny a wise, knowing Creator. Maturidi lists four alternative explanations for the existence and order of the world: (1) natures (taba'i') and nutrients, (2) the influence of stars, sun, and moon, (3) the rotation of the sphere, and (4) the management of parents in procreation. He argues that all these reduce to the idea that one thing is caused by another (kulluhu yarji'u ila kawni shay'in bi-shay'in), leading either to an infinite regress if no first cause is posited, or, if a first cause is posited, to the impossibility of self-creation. The impossibility of self-creation is shown through a dilemma: if a thing were to bring itself into existence, it would have to do so either before it exists (impossible) or after it exists (superfluous, proving its existence depends on another). Then he attacks the specific cases: parents lack full control and knowledge of their offspring, and inanimate objects (stars, spheres) are unaware of their effects. The existence of complex faculties like hearing, sight, speech, and intellect in beings that start as helpless infants and grow only through external nutrition points to a wise maker, not blind processes. Finally, he notes that all things have fixed limits beyond which they do not grow, and even with continued nutrition they eventually decline—this indicates a transcendent, all-knowing, and all-powerful agent, not internal mechanisms.

Key Terms
أولية (awwaliyya)
الطبائع (al-taba'i')

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: A first cause can be self-caused.
Correction: Maturidi shows that self-causation is logically impossible because a thing cannot act before it exists, and if it exists already, it doesn't need to cause itself.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, why is self-creation impossible?
A) Because it leads to an infinite regress.
B) Because a non-existent cannot bring itself into existence, and an existent does not need to bring itself into existence.
C) Because it contradicts the Quran.
D) Because parents are the true creators.

Key Takeaway
A first cause cannot bring itself into existence; it must be uncaused and eternal.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself attributing a good outcome in your life entirely to your own effort or natural causes, forgetting the One who created both you and those causes? How does this passage's dismantling of self-causation confront that tendency in you?
?
If you truly internalize that nothing can bring itself into existence, then every breath you take is a gift from an uncaused Cause. What would change about your morning if you woke up and said, 'I did not create myself; I am utterly dependent on Allah'?
?
The passage shows that a first cause cannot be self-caused—it must be eternal. Does your heart truly rest in the fact that Allah is that uncaused Cause, or do you subtly rely on secondary causes as if they were ultimate? What is one practical step you can take today to shift that reliance back to the Creator?

Parents and Inanimate Objects Cannot Be Ultimate Creators
Maturidi refutes parents and inanimate objects as creators.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why parents and inanimate objects like nature or stars cannot be the true creators of complex beings.

Core Idea
Parents lack knowledge and control over their children; inanimate objects are unaware.

After the logical refutation, Maturidi turns to the specific claims that parents or natural objects produce life. He points out their clear limitations: parents cannot guarantee the outcome, and inanimate objects have no awareness. This attack shows that these proposed creators are powerless.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on parents and inanimate objects
Focus on the arguments Maturidi raises against parents as creators and against inanimate objects (nature, stars, spheres) as creators.
2
List the limitations of parents
Identify the reasons Maturidi gives why parents cannot be the ultimate cause of their children (e.g., they don't control beginnings, cannot fix corruption, etc.).
3
Understand the inadequacy of inanimate objects
Notice that inanimate objects are unaware of the benefits in them and cannot produce complex outcomes.

Source Text

What is one reason Maturidi gives why parents cannot be the ultimate cause of their children?
A) Parents are themselves created.
B) They cannot prevent their children from becoming corrupt.
C) They do not control the diet of their children.
D) They are not aware of the stars.

Key Takeaway
True creation requires knowledge and power that only a wise Creator possesses; parents and inanimate objects are merely instruments.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you observe your own abilities—like speech or reason—do you ever take them for granted as mere biology? How does this passage force you to see the direct hand of the Maker in every faculty you possess? Will you pause today to thank Him specifically for one of these gifts?
?
You likely have expectations from your parents or for your own children. But this passage says parents cannot determine the outcome. Are you living as if your planning and effort alone guarantee results? Identify one area where you have been relying more on your own management than on the Wise Maker, and commit to turning that over to Him today.
?
This lesson shows that even the nutrition from parents does not produce your deeper capacities. Do you act as though your physical efforts and natural processes are the ultimate causes of success in your life? What would change if you truly believed that every meaningful outcome comes only from Allah's making (ja'l)? Write one practical step to realign your daily trust.

Evidence from Complex Capacities and Fixed Limits
Maturidi argues from fixed limits and capacities to a wise Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the presence of faculties like speech and the fixed limits of growth point to a wise Creator.

Core Idea
The limits and complexity of life cannot arise from blind processes.

Maturidi concludes with a positive argument: the existence of high-level faculties in animals and humans, and the fact that everything has a fixed limit, shows that the world is the product of a knowing, powerful agent. This lesson explores that final, powerful reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the final argument on limits and capacities
Focus on how Maturidi uses the concept of 'limit' (hadd) and the decline of faculties despite ongoing nutrition to argue for a transcendent creator.
2
Understand the argument from fixed limits
Everything has a maximum beyond which it does not grow, and eventually declines. This suggests an external determiner, not internal processes.
3
Connect to the attributes of the Creator
The passage ends by describing the Creator as knowing by His essence and all-powerful (qadir). Reflect on why these attributes are necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

حد (hadd)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi say happens to a thing once it reaches its limit?
A) It continues to grow indefinitely.
B) It begins to diminish despite continued nutrition.
C) It becomes immortal.
D) It changes into another species.

Key Takeaway
The fixed limits and eventual decline of all things indicate that they are governed by a wise, all-powerful Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You see your own body and mind grow and then begin to decline. Do you ever stop to consider that the One who set that limit is calling you to recognize Him? Will you let the fading of your strength turn your heart towards the eternal?
?
The nutrients and upbringing you rely on continue, yet your faculties still wane. What does that tell you about where your true sustenance comes from? Are you trusting more in the means or in the Limit-Giver?
?
If everything in creation has a fixed limit set by Allah, what does that say about your ambitions and your contentment? Are you striving within the limits He has ordained, or are you ignoring them and forgetting the One who determines them?

The Impossibility of Self-Causation: Proof of a Creator
Proof from impossibility of self-causation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that nothing can cause its own existence, and therefore everything must be caused by another.

Core Idea
Self-causation is logically impossible; all existence depends on another.

Maturidi presents a concise but profound proof that cuts through the question of origins. He shows that if something could bring itself into existence, it could also annihilate itself — a contradiction. This forces us to recognize that all contingent beings are caused by an external source.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage and Translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once through, then read the literal translation. Let Maturidi's own words and structure guide your initial understanding.
Notice the disjunction 'either...or' (أ) إما ... بـ) أو).
2
Unpack the Two Options
Identify the two options Maturidi presents: existence at the time of non-existence (impossible) or existence after non-existence, implying causation by another. See how he argues the first option leads to contradiction.
3
Understand the Contradiction
Reflect on why self-causation is impossible: if a thing could bring itself into existence, it could also annihilate itself, which is contradictory. This is the key logical move.
This argument is a classic in kalam theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أ) إما حين عدمه، وذلك محال أن يوجد عديما؛ مع ما إذ رجع إليه تدبير كل شيء لا يحتمل أن يبلغه العدم؛ فإنه لو جاز أن يوجد نفسه لجاز في ذلك الوقت أن يعدمه، وذلك متناقض؛ بـ) أو بعد الوجود، فثبت الوجود بغيره. وبالله التوفيق.
A) Either at the time of its non-existence, and it is impossible that it should exist as non-existent; since when the management of everything is referred back to it, it cannot be reached by non-existence; for if it were possible for it to bring itself into existence, then at that time it would be possible for it to annihilate itself, and that is contradictory. B) Or after existence, so existence is established by another. And with God is success.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage presents a brief but fundamental proof for the existence of a creator. Maturidi begins by stating a disjunction: either a thing exists before its own existence (which is impossible, as a non-existent cannot bring itself into existence), or it comes into existence after non-existence, implying that its existence is caused by another. The first option is self-contradictory because if something could cause its own existence, it could equally cause its own non-existence. Thus, the second option is necessary, affirming that everything owes its existence to an external cause. This is a classic kalam argument for a necessary being.

Key Terms
وجود
عدم
غيره

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: This argument is circular because it assumes a creator exists.
Correction: The argument does not assume a creator; it simply argues that self-causation leads to contradiction, and thus there must be an external cause.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of Maturidi's argument in this passage?
A) Everything exists eternally.
B) Nothing can cause its own existence.
C) Existence is an illusion.
D) The world is self-sufficient.

Key Takeaway
Self-causation is logically impossible; therefore, everything that exists must have been caused by an external source.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own existence—your heartbeat, your breath, your consciousness—do you ever pause to realize that you did not bring yourself into being and you cannot sustain yourself for even a moment? How can you turn that realization into a daily habit of gratitude and reliance on Allah?
?
The argument proves that everything depends on another—ultimately on Allah. In what areas of your life do you still cling to an illusion of self-sufficiency, as if you are the cause of your own success? What practical step can you take today to break that illusion and acknowledge your total dependence on Allah?
?
This passage challenges you to think logically about the impossibility of self-causation. How does your intellectual acceptance of this truth actually change how you live? If you truly believe you are caused by another, then what does that demand of you in terms of obedience and trust? Be specific about one area where you need to submit more fully.

Parents Are Not the True Creators of Their Children
Maturidi lists limitations of parents to refute their role as causes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that parents cannot be the true cause of their children's existence and characteristics.

Core Idea
Parents lack the control and knowledge to be true creators.

This passage from Imam Maturidi challenges the common assumption that parents are the primary causes of their children. By pointing out the many limitations parents have, Maturidi redirects our attention to the ultimate Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the list of limitations Maturidi presents.
The passage is a single sentence with multiple clauses.
2
Identify the limitations of parents
Maturidi lists several limitations: parents cannot control early development, children often turn out differently, parents cannot fix defects, and their knowledge is limited.
These are enumerated by 'and' (واو) clauses.
3
Understand the conclusion
Reflect on why these limitations lead to the conclusion that parents are not the true cause. The phrase 'وهو الذي يبطل كون ذلك بهم' indicates the nullification of their role as cause.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً إن جميع ما يذكر إنما هو نوع الموات إلا من ذكر من الآباء والأمهات، مما يعلمون أن تدبيرهم لا يبلغ مبادئ الأولاد، وأنهم يكونون على غير ما يأملون، وأنهم لو فسدوا لا يملكون إصلاحهم، وأن وسعهم لا يبلغ ما استتر من الأشياء فضلاً من تقديرها هنالك، وغير ذلك من الوجوه التي تبطل كون ذلك بهم. ونوع الموات غير عالم بما فيه من المنافع، ولا بالذي يحتمل منهم منع ذلك. ثبت أن الذي يكون بالاغتذاء وبالطبائع إنما كان ذلك فيهم بجعل حكيم عليم فيها، وفي اللزوم بغير جعل كل شيء على ما عليه من النفع والضرر على أنه في الحيوان معان ليس في شيء مما وصف آثار ذلك من نحو السمع والبصر وبخاصة في البشر من نحو النطق والميز والوقوف على أشياء. مع ما كان ذلك في أول الأحوال غير كائن، ولا يربى إلا بأغذية الآباء والأمهات، ثم لم يؤثر ذلك فيهم، فكيف في الأولاد.
And also, everything that is mentioned is only a type of inanimate thing except for what is mentioned of fathers and mothers, while they know that their management does not reach the beginnings of children, and that they happen contrary to their hopes, and that if they become corrupted they cannot repair them, and that their capacity does not reach what is hidden of things, let alone their estimation there; and other aspects that nullify that this occurs through them. And the inanimate type is not aware of the benefits in it, nor of what may be prevented from them. It is established that what occurs through nutrition and natural dispositions was only in them by the making of a wise, knowing One in them, and necessarily without making everything upon its state of benefit and harm, and that in animals there are meanings not found in anything described by those effects, such as hearing and sight, and especially in humans such as speech, discernment, and understanding of things. While that was in the first states non-existent, and it does not grow except by the nutrition of fathers and mothers, then that did not affect them, so how [could it affect] the children?
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage refutes the idea that parents or natural processes are the true causes of life and design. Maturidi argues that inanimate matter (موات) and even parents lack the knowledge and power to produce children with complex faculties like hearing, sight, speech, and reason. He points out that parents cannot control the outcome, cannot repair corruption, and do not know the hidden details. Therefore, the wise and knowledgeable creator must have implanted these abilities. The argument concludes that since nutrition and natural processes do not produce these faculties in parents themselves, they cannot produce them in children, proving a higher cause.

Key Terms
موات
آباء
أمهات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Parents are the true creators of their children.
Correction: Maturidi shows parents lack control and knowledge, so a higher cause is needed.

Checkpoint
According to Maturidi, which of the following is a reason why parents are not the true cause of their children?
A) Parents are made of inanimate matter.
B) Parents cannot repair their children if they become corrupted.
C) Parents are not aware of their actions.
D) Children are born spontaneously.

Key Takeaway
Parents are mere intermediaries; the true Creator is beyond their limitations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which areas of your life do you rely most on your own planning and control, and how does this passage challenge you to trust Allah's hidden knowledge and power instead? What is one practical step you can take today to surrender that area to Him?
?
When your children (or those you care for) turn out differently than you hoped, what does your emotional response reveal about your belief in who the true Creator is? How will you repent from that false belief and realign your heart?
?
You try to fix your children's flaws and failures, but this passage says you cannot repair them if they become corrupted. Does that realisation make you anxious or relieved? How does it push you to depend solely on Allah for their wellbeing and guidance?

Evidence of a Wise Creator from the Faculties of Living Beings
Maturidi uses inanimate matter and faculties to argue for a wise Creator.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument that the faculties in animals and humans, such as hearing, sight, speech, and reason, point to a wise Creator.

Core Idea
Inanimate matter cannot give life, so a wise Creator must be the source.

Continuing his argument, Maturidi considers inanimate matter and the special faculties found in living beings. He shows that these faculties cannot arise from natural processes like nutrition, leading to the conclusion that they are placed by a wise and knowing Creator.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the structure: first about inanimate matter, then about faculties, then the nutritional argument.
2
Understand the argument about inanimate matter
Maturidi states that inanimate matter is unaware of its own benefits. Therefore, any order or benefit must come from an external wise maker.
This establishes the need for a 'Hakim 'Aleem'.
3
Identify the faculties unique to living beings
Animals have hearing and sight; humans have speech, discernment, and understanding. These are not found in inanimate matter.
4
Reflect on the nutritional argument
Maturidi points out that these faculties were not present initially in parents' nutrition, so nutrition cannot produce them. Therefore, they must be from the Creator.
This strengthens the case for a transcendent cause.

Source Text

He points out that parents cannot control the outcome, cannot repair corruption, and do not know the hidden details. Therefore, the wise and knowledgeable creator must have implanted these abilities. The argument concludes that since nutrition and natural processes do not produce these faculties in parents themselves, they cannot produce them in children, proving a higher cause.

What does Maturidi use as evidence for a wise Creator in this passage?
A) The existence of inanimate matter.
B) The fact that parents are not aware of their children's development.
C) The presence of faculties like hearing, sight, speech, and reason in living beings.
D) The ability of parents to repair their children.

Key Takeaway
The unique faculties in living beings, not deriving from parents or nutrition, prove a wise Creator.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at your own ability to think and speak, do you truly acknowledge that this is a direct gift from Allah, not something you or your parents earned? How does this realization change your gratitude today?
?
The passage argues that inanimate matter cannot give life. Yet we often live as if life is just a biological accident. Where in your daily life do you act as if your faculties are merely natural rather than divine gifts? Will you consciously remind yourself of their source?
?
If every faculty you have is placed by a wise Creator, how should that affect your use of your speech, sight, and hearing today? Will you dedicate them to His service, or will you use them heedlessly?

Everything has a limit indicating a wise creator
Maturidi argues that fixed limits in creation prove a wise designer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Maturidi uses the observation of fixed limits in natural entities to argue for a creator who is all-knowing and all-powerful.

Core Idea
Limits in creation prove a wise and powerful creator.

Maturidi presents a simple yet profound observation: every created thing has a fixed limit. Despite ongoing nutrition and care, growth stops and eventually declines. This stopping point cannot be explained by the nutrition itself, so it must be imposed by a being with perfect knowledge and power. In this lesson, you will follow his reasoning and understand its theological significance.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the original Arabic and its literal translation. Notice the specific terms for 'limit', 'knowing by Himself', and 'powerful by Himself'.
These key terms (حد, عالم بذاته, قادر بنفسه) are central to the argument.
2
Identify the observation and the puzzle
Maturidi observes that everything reaches a limit and then declines, even while nutrition continues. Ask yourself: why doesn't growth continue indefinitely? That is the puzzle he sets up.
3
Follow the reasoning to the conclusion
The passage states that this limit cannot be due to the nutrition or upbringing themselves because they are still present. Therefore, the limit must be imposed by an external being who is knowing and powerful. See how the argument moves from effect to cause.
This is a classic argument from design, focusing on boundaries.
4
Connect to Allah's attributes
The conclusion directly affirms that the creator is 'knowing by Himself' and 'powerful by Himself'. Think about why these two attributes are specifically highlighted and how they relate to the observation.
These attributes are necessary for the creator to set precise limits.
5
Check your understanding with the quiz
Answer the multiple-choice question to confirm you have grasped the main point. Then consider the common misunderstanding mentioned.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبعد، فإن كل شيء له حد، إذا بلغ ذلك الحد لا يزداد له طول ولا عرض ولا بصر ولا عقل، بل يأخذ كل شيء من ذلك بالانتقاص، على قيام الأغذية ودوام التربية؛ عقل أن ذلك كذلك لا بما ذكر، ولكن بمن هو عالم بذاته حتى لا يعرب عنه شيء، قادر بنفسه فلا يعجزه شيء، جل ثناؤه.
And after that, everything has a limit; when it reaches that limit, its height, width, sight, and intellect do not increase; rather, everything of that begins to decrease, despite the continuance of nutrition and persistence of upbringing. It is understood that this is so not by what was mentioned, but by Him who is knowing by Himself so that nothing escapes Him, powerful by Himself so that nothing incapacitates Him, exalted is His praise.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage observes that every entity has a fixed limit in its growth and abilities. Despite continuous nourishment and care, once the limit is reached, no further increase occurs, and decline eventually begins. This natural stopping point cannot be due to the nutrition or care themselves, as those continue. Therefore, the limitation must be imposed by a being who is knowing and powerful in Himself, a clear proof of a wise creator who sets boundaries.

Key Terms
حد
عالم بذاته
قادر بنفسه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Limits in creation are just natural laws that need no external cause.
Correction: Maturidi argues that since nutrition continues but growth still stops, the limit must be set by a conscious agent with knowledge and power.

Checkpoint
What does Maturidi conclude from the fact that things stop growing despite continued nutrition?
A) Nutrition is ineffective after a point.
B) A wise creator set the limits.
C) Things choose to stop growing.
D) Growth is an illusion.

Key Takeaway
Fixed limits in creation, unexplained by ongoing nourishment, point necessarily to a creator who is knowing and powerful by Himself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of an area in your life where you have hit a limit—whether in your intellect, strength, or patience. According to this lesson, that limit is not just a natural fact but a signpost pointing to a wise Creator who set it. How does this change your attitude toward your limitations? Will you thank the One who measured you, or will you resent the boundary?
?
You continue to receive nutrition and upbringing every day, yet you do not keep growing. This lesson argues that the ongoing means do not explain the stop. So what does that tell you about the One who decided your limit? Are you living as though your limits are just random accidents of biology, or are you actively acknowledging the Creator who personally assigned them to you?
?
Everything has a limit, including the number of breaths you have left. That limit was set by a Being who is knowing and powerful. You cannot guarantee finishing this reflection. In light of that, what will you do differently today to submit to the scale that He has used for you? What one concrete action will you take to live within your limits with gratitude, rather than fighting against them?

Substances Need an External Sustainer
Maturidi argues that composite things prone to decay require a sustainer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Maturidi argues that substances' ability to be corrupted and repaired proves they require an external sustainer.

Core Idea
Changeable things cannot be self-sufficient; they need a sustainer.

Maturidi continues his argument for the existence of a sustainer by examining the nature of physical substances. He points out that everything we observe can both decay and be restored, indicating instability. This short passage presents a logical proof that such things are sustained by something beyond themselves.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Note that 'جواهر' means substances or atoms.
2
Identify the key observation
Maturidi notes that all substances admit both corruption (إفساد) and repair (إصلاح). This is the starting point.
Corruption and repair are opposites.
3
Understand the logical inference
Since these opposites cannot cooperate within the same thing, the substance's state must come from an external agent.
This is the core of the argument.
4
Connect to self-sufficiency
If a thing were self-sufficient, it would not change. Change implies dependence.
This is the conclusion that leads to the need for a sustainer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضا إنه ما من شيء مما ذكر من أنواع الجواهر إلا وقد يحتمل الإفساد والإصلاح جميعا؛ وذلك أيضا كله متضاد متدافع لا يحتمل الاجتماع للتعاون؛ ثبت أنها كانت على ما عليه بغيرها؛ إذ كل شيء على جهة بنفسه لا يحتمل التغير ما دامت نفسه. وبالله العصمة.
And also, there is nothing of what was mentioned among the types of substances that does not admit both corruption and repair; and that all is also mutually contradictory and repelling, not admitting together for cooperation; it is established that they were in their state due to another; for everything in its own manner by itself does not admit change as long as its essence remains. And with God is protection.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
Here Maturidi points out that all material substances (جواهر) are subject to both corruption (فساد) and repair (إصلاح). These two processes are opposite and cannot cooperate within the thing itself. The fact that they undergo change indicates they are not self-sufficient but are sustained by an external agent. If something were completely self-sufficient, it would remain unchanged. Therefore, the existence of change necessitates a sustainer.

Key Terms
جواهر
إفساد
إصلاح
تغير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think substances change by themselves.
Correction: Maturidi shows their inherent instability requires an external maintainer.

Checkpoint
Why does Maturidi say substances depend on another?
A) They are eternal.
B) They can be corrupted and repaired, showing they are not self-sufficient.
C) They are made of atoms.
D) They have no essence.

Key Takeaway
Substances that can change need an external sustainer, because change implies lack of self-sufficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your body is constantly changing—cells die, you age, you experience health and sickness. If even your own existence is not self-sufficient but depends entirely on God’s constant sustenance, what does that demand from you today? Are you living as if you are self-reliant, or do you thank and rely on Him for every breath? What one habit could you start to remind yourself of this dependence?
?
Think of a recent change in your life—a success or a failure. Did you attribute it to your own ability or to circumstances, or did you see the hand of the Sustainer who keeps you in existence? If you truly believed that your entire being depends on Him from moment to moment, how would your attitude toward gratitude and humility change? What would you do differently tomorrow?
?
Maturidi shows that even the possibility of change proves you cannot sustain yourself. Does this shake your sense of independence? How can you cultivate a constant awareness that you are held in existence by God alone, and what would that awareness lead you to do or stop doing?

8 min
Living beings' needs and desires prove a provider
Maturidi argues from needs/desires and subjugation to prove a provider.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Maturidi's argument from the inherent needs and desires of living beings to establish a wise creator who provides for them.

Core Idea
Needs are not self-caused; they point to a provider who created both the need and its fulfillment.

Imam Maturidi presents a powerful argument from the very fabric of life: every living being is subject to needs and desires that drive it to seek sustenance. But can these needs arise from the beings themselves? Maturidi argues they cannot, and in doing so, he establishes the existence of a wise manager (مدبر) who created living beings with these needs and also created the means to satisfy them. This passage also extends the argument to the subjugation of all things in the world, showing that nothing is self-sufficient but depends on a higher manager.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the three reasons Maturidi gives, marked by 'أحدها', 'ولأن', 'أو للإحالة'.
2
Identify the three reasons against self-caused needs
List the three reasons Maturidi provides to show that living beings cannot have desires and needs by themselves. Understand each reason's logical force.
These reasons are concise but powerful; try to rephrase each in your own words.
3
Examine the second argument from subjugation
After establishing the provider from needs, Maturidi adds a second argument: everything in the world is subjugated and cannot manage itself. See how this reinforces the conclusion.
Examples like permanent rest and continuous movement show that things operate under an external command.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأيضاً إن كل حي فيما يعاين مبني على الحاجات والشهوات التي تغلبهم وتقهرهم، ولولا ذلك ما احتاجوا إلى الأغذية. ثم كانت هي أسبابًا عند وجودها للغناء، لم يحتمل أن يكون منها تهيج الشهوات وحدوث الحاجات. ولا يجوز أن تكون لأنفسها شهوات وحاجات، لأوجه: أ) أحدها، أن القيام بالذات دون الغير دليل الغناء، لا يجوز أن يصير حاجة؛ ب) ولأن ما كان لذاته على جهة لا يحتمل زواله، وقد يقع لها الغناء من الغير؛ جـ) أو للإحالة أن يكون الشيء من حيث نفسه محوجاً إلى غير، بل إذا أحوج إلى غير من حيث نفسه تدوم الحاجة ما بقيت نفسه. فثبت أن هنالك غيرا أنشأهم على الحاجات وركب فيهم الشهوات، ثم أنشأ لهم ما به الغناء وقضاء الشهوات. فيكون بما أريد به نفي المدبر للعالم تثبيته. ولا قوة إلا بالله. مع ما لا يوجد شيء من أعيان العالم وصفاتها إلا مسخرا به مذللا بما لولا ذلك كان أهون عليه وألذ، نحو القرار الدائم والسير المتتابع مما كان به معاش أحد وما له المعاش فصار العالم بكليته بالمعنى الذي ذكرت ولا يجوز أن يكون المسخر المذلل يملك التدبير حتى يكون به غنى الغير وقيامه، ولا يملك إزالة الذلة عن نفسه والسخرة. ثبت أن لكل ذلك مدبرا عليما علم وجوه حاجاتهم وغناهم فخلقهم على ذلك مع ما أحوج بعضاً إلى بعض.
And also, every living being as is observed is built upon needs and desires that overcome and subdue them; were it not for that, they would not need nourishment. Then when these [needs and desires] exist, they become causes of sufficiency; it is unlikely that from them arise the excitation of desires and the occurrence of needs. And it is not permissible that they should have desires and needs by themselves, for reasons: A) One: existence by essence without another is proof of self-sufficiency; it cannot become need. B) And because that which is by essence in a certain manner does not admit its removal, and yet sufficiency from another occurs to it. C) Or because it is absurd that a thing should need another from its own perspective; rather, if it needs another from its own perspective, the need would continue as long as its essence remains. So it is established that there is another who created them upon needs and implanted desires in them, then created for them that by which is sufficiency and fulfillment of desires. Thus, what was intended to negate the manager of the world becomes its affirmation. And there is no power except with God. Along with that, nothing from the essences of the world and their attributes is found except subjugated and humiliated by that which, were it not so, would be easier and more pleasurable for it—such as permanent rest and continuous movement, by which is the livelihood of one and the life of another. So the whole world became according to the meaning I mentioned; and it is not permissible that the subjugated, humiliated one should possess management such that through it the sufficiency of another and its subsistence occur, while it cannot remove humiliation and subjugation from itself. It is established that all of that has a knowing manager who knew the aspects of their needs and sufficiency, so He created them upon that, along with making some of them need others.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage presents a detailed argument from the inherent needs and desires of living beings. Maturidi observes that living beings are structured around needs and desires that compel them to seek sustenance. These needs and desires cannot arise from the beings themselves for three reasons: (1) self-sufficiency of essence precludes need; (2) if something is self-sufficient by essence, it cannot acquire need from another; (3) if a thing needed another by its own essence, the need would be permanent. Therefore, needs are imposed by an external creator who also provides means of satisfaction. Additionally, everything in the world is subjugated to certain functions (e.g., rest, movement), which shows it cannot manage itself. Hence, a wise manager (مدبر) is proven, who knows and creates accordingly, even establishing interdependence among beings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Needs and desires are natural properties of living beings that arise spontaneously without an external cause.
Correction: Maturidi argues that needs cannot be self-originating because a self-sufficient essence cannot acquire need. Instead, needs are imposed by an external creator who also provides for them.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of Maturidi's reasons why living beings cannot have desires by themselves?
A) Self-sufficiency precludes need.
B) Self-sufficient things cannot acquire need from another.
C) Need would be permanent if inherent.
D) Desires are always harmful.

Key Takeaway
The inherent needs of living beings and the subjugation of all things logically require a wise creator who provides and manages.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a deep need or desire, do you recognize it as a sign pointing to the Provider who placed it within you, or do you try to satisfy it independently? What would change in your daily reliance on Allah if you truly saw every hunger, longing, or desperation as a proof of His existence and care?
?
You are subjugated in countless ways—your heartbeat continues without your command, your needs return relentlessly, your body weakens even as you plan. How does this reality challenge your sense of self-sufficiency? Will you submit to the Knowing Manager of your life, or continue to resist His management?
?
The passage argues that if a need were part of your essence, it would never be fulfilled. Yet you experience temporary satisfaction. Who do you think removes your need each time? And what does your immediate forgetfulness of that Provider say about your gratitude?
